# Full manuscript

Successful Ways of Seeing Software Project Management

Mark Stringer

**About the Author**

**Mark Stringer** has worked in the software development industry for 30
years. He's been a programmer, applications researcher and, for the last
15 years, a project manager. He's worked with organizations such as IBM,
Xerox and Cambridge University.

Mark wrote this book because of a realization -- he seemed to be
repeatedly reading the same book about project management. The same
book, but with different titles. This book claimed that, if only you
followed some prescribed methodology, everything would be lovely. His
experience was, no matter what methodology you used, things often
weren't lovely.

In recent years, as well as writing this book, he has written and
performed the one-man shows "Delivering the Impossible," and "The
Biscuit Tin and the Biscuit," at the Brighton and Edinburgh Fringe
Festivals. These are possibly the only fringe shows about project
management -- ever! He has an honors degree in philosophy from the
University of St. Andrews, Scotland.

**About the Technical Reviewer**

Acknowledgments

I have just a small number of people to thank. But they are very
important. Without them, this book wouldn't have happened. First and
foremost, I need to thank Andrew Brown for reading and re-reading full
chapters and half-formed, barely sketched-out chapters. When he was
experiencing the most difficult of times, he still managed to read what
I had written, comment on it, and say yes to a video call. Andrew, my
heartfelt thanks.

Next, I'd like to thank my wife Themis Chalvantzi-Stringer. Three years
ago, she encouraged me to take a show to the Edinburgh Festival Fringe
with the title "Delivering the Impossible." Hey! That's the title of
this book! If I had not done that, this book probably wouldn't exist. I
never would have discovered how helpful talking out loud, in a field, to
no one, is for organizing your thoughts. And "Delivering the Impossible"
wouldn't have had its premiere performance, in front of a large audience
who were expecting an entirely different show! So, also thanks to Peter
Merre for messaging me from his sick bed and lending me his venue spot
and his fringe audience.

Finally, I'd like to thank my father, Peter Stringer, to whom this book
is dedicated. He didn't know much about project management, or software
development. But he loved books. He literally showed me where the
library was. He took me with him from an early age. And he recommended
to me all kinds of books: poetry, sci-fi, historical fiction. All the
time I knew him, he read at least one book a week.

Introduction
Chapter INTRODUCTION

Many projects appear impossible. Most of them aren't. There are almost
always things you can do to make them run more smoothly. You can deliver
more value to their owners and users. And you can make the people who
work on them happier and more effective. You just need to look at them
in the right way. This book offers some different ways to do just that.

The title of this book is "Delivering the impossible." But of course,
you, or anyone, can only deliver possible things. So why did you pick up
this book that has a contradiction right there on the front cover?

Perhaps this sounds familiar.

You're working on a project. You're the project manager, and you feel
stuck. It seems like the project you've been asked to deliver is
impossible.

You're not quite sure how this happened. But at some point, somebody
promised somebody that the project would be finished by a certain date.
That date was probably Christmas. Maybe you have lots of evidence or
maybe it's just instinct, but somehow you know that your team can't
deliver the project by then.

Maybe it's worse than that: maybe you still don't even know what needs
to be delivered. People are telling you that the project has to be
finished by a certain date. But when you or your team try to get a
definitive answer as to what "finished" means, you don't get the
information you need.

If you're that project manager, then this book is for you.

How do you deliver a project that seems impossible? There is a good
answer, which I explore in depth in this book. It's that you look at it
from different angles. You find a way of seeing -- or multiple ways of
seeing -- that make it much more possible. I'm going to present several
different ways of looking at projects. And I'm going to show how
switching between these ways of seeing can give projects their best
chance of success.

Alan Kay is a software pioneer. He invented overlapping windows in the
graphical user interface. He also invented an approach to programming
known as object-oriented programming. As I'm writing this, I'm using
both of those inventions. And in this book, we're going to use another
one of his ideas. It's a quote from Alan Kay which he has repeated
several times:

> "Point of view is worth 80 IQ points."
>
> Alan Kay (Fitzhenry)

What does this mean? It means that if you can just figure out the right
way to look at a problem, solving it becomes much easier. An additional
80 IQ points would make pretty much anyone a genius. And that's the crux
of my message to you. I'm going to show you different ways of looking at
the problem of project management that can make us much smarter. The
idea is that if we can just find the right perspective, we can be much
cleverer and more effective in what we do.

But of course, there's also a potential downside to Alan Kay's nugget of
wisdom. Looking at something in the wrong way could take away 80 IQ
points. Some ways of looking at things can make us stupid -- and some of
these unhelpful ways of looking at a project are extremely common. So,
I'll talk about these as well. And I'll try to explain why looking at
things in certain ways is so unhelpful. I'll also talk about some ways
of escaping from their grip of these unhelpful ways of seeing.

# Why now?

You might think: "OK, I get it, seeing things in a different way is
useful. But my project has some specific problems. I just want to fix
them. I don't have time to kick everything up in the air and
philosophize. Maybe we could do this another time?"

Part of my message throughout this book is that if you want to fix your
project's immediate problems, now is absolutely the time. Well, for most
projects, now is much later than would have been ideal. But now is
better than never.

We've needed better ways of thinking about software projects since the
1950s. Humans have been managing the writing of software for at least 75
years, and we're still not very good at it.

And now, more than ever, the ability to write good software is a huge
competitive advantage. Customers want software that makes their lives
easier and better. They want software that is genuinely valuable to
them. They've never needed it more. Being good at software development
has become a way of -- quite literally -- conquering the world.

Am I overstating things? I don't think so. Marc Andreessen wrote the
first widely used web browser -- Mosaic. Now he runs a hugely
influential venture capital firm in Silicon Valley. As he declared more
than ten years ago:

> "Software is eating the world."
>
> Marc Andreessen (Andreesen)

And since he wrote that article, software has increased its appetite.
It's taken huge bites out of relationships and dating, politics, social
life, music and television. Now, with the arrival of AI, it looks like
it's also taking a massive bite out of lots of other, completely
different kinds of work. That includes the writing of software.

Some companies are good at developing software. Amazon, Facebook and
Google are good at it. And some of those companies are now more powerful
than some governments.

But this is just one side of the coin. A lot of companies and government
organizations are terrible at writing software. Often, the software is
paid for and developed, but either it is never used or it never performs
as well as promised.

Sometimes it even makes things worse. What if these organizations that
are struggling to write good software could be better at it? Maybe they
would be just as successful and powerful as companies like Amazon and
Facebook. The struggle to deliver good software in a timely manner is
real. And it doesn't look like it's going away any time soon.

# Why me?

Let's say that you believe me. Let's say you agree that now is a good
time to get very good at project managing software development. Why
should you listen to me in particular?

Well, there are some obvious, boring reasons. I've been working in the
software development industry since 1994. I started out as a developer.
I wrote software for oil companies, the military and then a newfangled
thing called the internet. Then I worked as a researcher trying to come
up with new applications for digital technologies, first for Xerox, then
for Cambridge University.

Perhaps a more specific reason why you should listen to me is because I
know how to deliver software projects. I've been doing it now for
fifteen years. Mostly I've been successful.

Some of these projects made millions for their owners. Some of them
weren't about money at all. They were literally about making the world a
better place. They helped their users do scientific research, fight
Covid-19 and cure cancer. Or they helped the course of criminal justice
run more smoothly. I'm not going to say I foiled any gang of criminals
single-handed (I didn't), but I did a tiny little bit to help.

I believe that some of those projects wouldn't have done as well if I
hadn't been involved. And that's about as much as you can ever say if
you're a project manager.

But there's a slightly less boring, less obvious reason. Most people who
claim to be experts on project management will try to give you the
impression that it is a rational, straight-forward thing. They will
suggest that if you follow the steps in their methodology, your project
will be guaranteed to succeed. I am under no such illusion. I know, and
I'm going to show you with multiple examples, that project management is
an irrational and emotional business. This, in itself, is a different
point of view.

As we'll see, irrationality and emotionality are built into the way
projects happen. There will be periods of time in any project where
things will be tense and you'll feel bad. That's pretty much guaranteed
if you're managing projects. What I try to explain in this book is
something that I haven't seen explained in many other places. I want to
explain why you're feeling bad, why it's OK to feel bad and what you can
best do to deal with these negative feelings. And of course, it's not
all about you. By using these techniques, you will also give the
projects that you work on the best chance of success that they possibly
could have.

And I'm going to show you that the way to do this is by seeing project
management in a number of different, smart ways. By doing this, we can
also avoid the "dumb" ways of seeing. These are the ways that can take
away 80 IQ points, that are always present and are often tempting.

# Why you?

I think there are three groups of people who should read this book. And
you're probably in one of them.

The first group is the most obvious. Are you directly involved with the
development of software? Or are you involved with the project management
of software development? OK, then you should read this book.

But there's also a second group. Software is eating the world, as we've
just established. Do you work in a business that's getting eaten by
software? Do you work for a company that needs to be able to deliver
software to stay relevant and competitive? Well then, you should
probably be interested in smarter ways of doing project management for
software development. You should be interested in getting software
developed and delivered, and so you should also be interested in this
book.

Then, finally, there's a third group of people. And that's everybody
else. OK, maybe not ***everybody*** else. But remember software is
eating a lot of things. So, it's perfectly possible that whatever you do
in your life, software is sniffing around it. Do you eat? Software has
already eaten restaurant reservations, takeout delivery and the entire
supply chain for groceries. Do you date? You're probably going to do
that through an app. Do you ever feel the need to be entertained? You're
probably looking at reviews, watching films and television programs and
listening to music through more software. Whatever you are doing in your
life, the chances are that software is thinking about taking a nibble of
it. And if that's the case, there is probably some value for you in
understanding the ways of seeing that I talk about in this book.

# Conclusion

In this introduction, we established that now is a good time to be
thinking about better ways of doing project management of software
development. Because software is eating everything. I made the case that
I'm a suitable person to help you think about those better ways of doing
project management, because I've been involved in software development
for over thirty years -- and I've been doing project management for more
than fifteen.

I also made clear that the way we're going to try to improve how we do
software development project management is by seeing things differently.
By looking for way of seeing things that can make us smart, and avoiding
the ways of seeing that make us dumb.

In the first section of this book, I'm going to discuss ways of seeing
that make us smarter. I'm going to talk about streams (Chapter STREAM),
swamps (Chapter SWAMP), cars (Chapter CAR), escaping from prison
(Chapter ESCAPE), pirate ships (Chapter SHIP) and bets (Chapter
BET)(Chapter NOT BET).

Then, in part 2 of this book, I'm going to talk about ways of seeing
that can make us dumb. I'm going to talk about the awesome and terrific
power that the principles of commitment and consistency hold over us
(Chapter COMMIT).

I'll talk about our tendency to unconsciously favor "all," "same,"
"forever" and "exact match" in our thinking (Chapter ALL). Do you see
projects as a source of problems, embarrassment and bad news? Then you
might be a boss. In this last chapter I explain a better way of seeing
projects that gives bosses an opportunity to be much smarter in the way
they see projects.

As a conclusion, I talk about why we, the members of our team and our
bosses are tempted to see things in ways that make us dumb. Then
finally, I explain the practical steps we can take to help everybody
involved with a project see things in ways that make us smart (Chapter
CONCLUSION).
Chapter STREAM

> "No man ever steps in the same river twice, for it's not the same
> river and he's not the same man."
>
> Heraclitus

A value stream is a set of processes that add value to a product and
then delivers the product to people who want it. When you manufacture
existing products, there is an existing value stream. But when you are
developing new products, there are two value streams: a weird one, and
one that doesn't exist.

# What are value streams?

Value streams are a way of seeing borrowed from the manufacture of other
things -- specifically cars in Japan.

Could they help as a way of seeing software development projects?
Several authors believe so. Such as David Anderson in his book "Kanban."
And Mary and Tom Poppendieck in their book "Lean Software Development."
(Anderson)(Poppendieck)

In the mid-1970s there was an energy crisis, and oil prices were high.
American car manufacturers started to lose out to the Japanese. Japanese
cars were more reliable than American cars. They were also cheaper and
far more fuel-efficient. In many ways, Japanese cars were just better.

Researchers at US business schools began to research what was going on.
They started to write about why the Japanese made better cars than the
Americans (Womack). They came across a key idea. This was something that
could explain why the Japanese had become so successful. And it
particularly applied to the Toyota car company. This was the idea of the
"value stream." And together with the idea of a value stream came a very
specific notion of waste.

A value stream is a series of actions that culminate in the production
of something that is valuable to its customers. So, as the chassis of a
car moves down a production line, value gets added to it. What sorts of
things add value? Well, in the case of a car, an engine. An engine adds
value. Paint, wing mirrors, seats. You get the idea. But note, none of
this value gets realized until the car is finally being driven by the
people who want it, the end-users.

American observers also noted the Toyota understanding of what *doesn't*
add value. Activities that go on in a factory without contributing to
the value stream are considered waste. There's an example in Taiichi
Ohno's book "The Toyota Production System." (Ohno) There is a pressing
machine. It presses the wings of each car. This is part of the
production line. It must be changed whenever a different model of car is
made. All the time that it takes to swap the machine, is, from the point
of view of a value stream, waste. And so, reducing that time -- from
days to minutes -- is a huge saving for the factory.

That seems easy to understand. But Taiichi Ohno also identified other
kinds of waste at Toyota that perhaps aren't so intuitive.

Once they have rolled off the production line, any completed cars
waiting around in a yard aren't delivering any value. This is also
waste. If parts are stacked high in warehouses and are not being added
to a car for long periods of time, this is waste too.

This is a crucial aspect of thinking about value streams. *The value is
only truly realized at the end.* This is when the product that has been
through the production line is sold. The value only really happens when
it's delivered into the hands of a customer.

Studying how the Japanese made cars resulted in many interesting ideas.
But here are three:

1.  The manufacture of a product can be arranged into a series of
    value-adding steps.

2.  Any waiting around between these value-adding steps is waste.

3.  Any waiting around before the final step of getting the product to
    the people who use it -- that is also waste.

These ideas were interesting to a lot of people. They appeared to offer
an insight into how any organization might improve its processes. They
suggested ways for an organization to be more efficient. They did,
however, involve quite a leap: because, as you may have noticed, not all
organizations make cars.

Above all, one core idea came out of these discussions: that it is
possible to map a value stream. It's possible to see what all the steps
in a process are. By extension, it's possible to see where there are
delays and where there is waste. Once you've found where the waste is,
you can work to reduce it. From this way of thinking came a whole new
way of organizing many industries: Just-in-Time manufacturing.

You might be able to see the appeal of looking at work in this way. If
we can map the value stream, we can reduce waste between processes. We
can look at reducing waiting times. Then we can, at least in theory,
speed up any production process and make it more efficient.

But is this a way of seeing that can make us smarter when it comes to
the production of software? It's easy to get enthusiastic about this new
way of thinking. And then, it can be easy to forget something very
important. Software development is very different from the production of
cars.

Many people have made good, solid efforts to apply these ideas to
software development (Poppendiek). And much of the thinking that has
come out of these attempts is extremely useful.

An example of this useful thinking comes from David Anderson (Anderson).
He suggests a two-step approach to tackling any project. First,
visualize all the work that needs to be done. And then second, limit the
amount of work in progress. This is an approach that I've used
repeatedly to help projects be more effective.

But applying these value stream ideas from production lines to software
development doesn't always work. Certainly, it hasn't been as successful
as some people thought it could be. And I think that's because of the
reason that we've already mentioned. Cars and software are different
kinds of things. The ways in which are made are two very different kinds
of activities.

Does that mean that we should abandon the value stream as a way of
seeing? No, I don't think it does. But, if we are going to see projects
as value streams, there's something extra that we need to understand.
Every software development project has to build its own value stream. It
can't just work with one that already exists. And this is the big
difference. Every software development project is creating a new
product. A car factory is reproducing an existing product.

*Every software development project is a new product.*

# A value stream that doesn't exist

Making existing products is very different from creating new ones.
Womack and his colleagues from MIT (Womack) were looking at the
production line in the 1980s. That means they were looking at a product
that had been in development for 60 years -- the car! Actually, they
were looking at two products that had been in development for 60 years.
The cars that were coming off the production line, and the production
line itself.

Writing software isn't about building an existing product. There isn't
an existing production line. It's about creating a new product. A
completely new product needs a completely new value stream. And someone
has to build it.

This is a realization we come to about value streams as a way of seeing
software development. If we are going to use them, we need to make some
changes. Value streams can be useful as a way of seeing in software
development. Because they can help us understand that part of what we're
doing in software development is *building* a new value stream.

But then there's a second realization. The beginning of this value
stream -- the product value stream -- doesn't come out of nowhere. It is
the product of a separate process. This is the process that generated
the idea.\
\
Maybe this is the story of that process?\
\
Someone has an idea.

This original idea doesn't attract that much attention.

But then the idea gets developed and modified.

The person who originally had the idea collaborates with someone.

Or, they put in some extra work. They add value.

And then the idea does get some attention.

And then someone decides that the idea was worth looking at.

And then someone else decides that the idea is worth funding.

Then the funding gets approved and a project team starts to be put
together.

# Two value streams

This is a second crucial realization that we need to reach if we're
going to use value streams as a helpful way of seeing.

In new product development, there are *two* value streams. Yes, the
product value stream that we've been talking about. But also, *an idea
value stream*, which came before it, and which is why we're here at all.

Let's think about the beginning of a software development project. At
this point, the value stream that the professors from MIT saw in the
Toyota factory doesn't exist. It needs to be built. But there's
something else that *is* there at the beginning. If a project aims to
create a new product, the idea is already there. This is what kicks off
the project.

We're so familiar with cars now that it's easy to forget that somebody
had to come up with the idea of a car. They had to decide what would be
valuable about it and how those valuable things could be put together in
a product. Almost everything about the modern car is different from the
very first "horseless" carriages. That phrase alone gives away just how
radically the idea of what constitutes a car has changed.

The driver's position has moved. The controls have changed and moved.
Many different forms of engine have been tried. Over the same time, the
value stream that creates modern cars has undergone a similar long and
complex transformation.

When we have a new product, we're going to have to do some version of
all that work. We need to build the product value stream. We need to
understand what features go together. We need to know how they need to
perform. And crucially, we need to understand how they will interact
with the people who use the product.

Value streams can be an extremely useful way of seeing for software
development, because they can offer these two important insights:

-   In a project that aims to develop a new product, development of the
    product value stream is part of the work of the project.

-   In a project to develop a new product, there is another value
    stream. This is a value stream which has attracted support and
    enthusiasm. It got funding. This is the idea value stream.

# The idea value stream

Is this really a value stream? In some sense, it must be. Because ideas
don't just instantly appear fully formed. OK, sometime ideas *do* just
pop into our head. But they don't emerge from nowhere fully funded and
greenlit for development. Projects don't instantly get funded. So, there
must be a value stream here.

But compared with the product value stream, the idea value stream is a
bit weird. Very often, it's not easy to see how value got added to an
idea in the idea value stream. Very often the people who are funding an
idea that came from the idea value stream don't want to admit exactly
why they're funding it. Sometimes, they don't know themselves.

In Taoist thinking, there is an idea that I find useful. It captures the
difference between the idea value stream and the product value stream.
This is the idea of the difference between flowers and fruit (Lao Tzu).

It might seem that as we go through this chapter that I'm a little bit
down on flowers. I'm not down on flowers. Flowers are great. In project
management, the part of flowers is played by ideas and dreams. Without
ideas there wouldn't be any projects. So, we can't be too harsh on
flowers.

People have ideas, those ideas gain enthusiasm, money and support, and
that's how projects are born. Something that some ideas and some flowers
have in common is that people like them straight away. They don't have
to think about it. Flowers have some attributes that make them instantly
attractive. It doesn't take any thought to like a rose. Of course, some
people might not like roses. But however they feel, they don't have to
take time to work it out.

Something similar seems to happen with ideas. Ideas that get money to
turn into projects have a structure that makes people like them without
too much thought. Just like flowers, those who are exposed to them tend
to like them -- without doing much or any thinking.

Often ideas are talked about in these terms: easy, fast or cheap. At
other times, the idea's appeal is that some things won't change:

-   "Fully compatible"

-   "The same functionality"

Sometimes the idea's appeal is its difference:

-   "A fresh look"

-   "A different take"

Sometimes all these things get bundled together:

-   "A radically different take. It does exactly what the old system
    did, but faster, quicker, more easily and for less money."

And of course, all of this is almost always going to be delivered using
the "latest technology":

-   "Does everything that the old system does. But quicker, cheaper and
    using AI!"

-   "Deals with all customer inquiries. Uses chatbots without the need
    for human involvement."

This "instant appeal" aspect of ideas plays a substantial part in why I
say that the virtual value stream is weird.

Another reason that the idea value stream is weird is that it can appear
to have a series of logical steps. It can appear to require proof that
there's demand for the new thing. Maybe there will be some kind of
calculation: "I will show that the expense will be more than covered by
the value it creates." But this is just "theater." It's just a ritual
that people are going through. There is a bit of an irony here. They
wouldn't be going through this ritual if the idea didn't already have
some instant flower-like appeal.

This is captured in a "fact" discussed in Robert L. Glass's book "Facts
and Fallacies in Software Engineering." (Glass)\
\
*The answer to a feasibility study is almost always "yes."*

But it's also possible that none of these "logical" steps are taken at
all. There's just some kind of consensus that this new thing is a good
idea.

The appeal of certain ideas might not have any obvious logic to it. But
from the examples I've given, we can identify some common themes in the
ideas that do get funded. The chosen ideas often have these concepts
lurking within them: "all" and "same", but also "new," "fast" and
"cheap." Later in the book, in (Chapter ALL), we'll explore why ideas
like "all" and "same" often have unconscious appeal.

Where ideas come from is a fascinating subject. Ideas get bought into
emotionally. This happens whether or not people go through the show of
doing a "feasibility" study. And this is an important thing to
understand. Because it explains some of the problems that we see later
on in the project. It explains how the idea value stream can cause us
problems when we start to build the product value stream.

But understanding where ideas come from isn't the aim of this chapter.
Our aim here is to grasp that there are two streams. And to understand
the difference between the idea and product value streams. If we can do
that, maybe we'll have the best chance of getting a project to deliver.
The project needs to stop being so fascinated by the idea. And it needs
to start being fascinated by the reality. We need to stop smelling the
flowers and think about how we're going to grow the fruit.

Because there's another way that flowers and fruit are different. Users
eat fruit. They only look at flowers. Nobody ever died from looking at a
flower (probably). But a sour apple can give you bad indigestion, and
some fruit is poisonous. A *lot* of fruit is unpalatable until it is
ripe.

Lots of companies get into trouble. They even destroy themselves in the
pursuit of an idea. Falling for an idea isn't, in itself, the problem.
The problem is the difficult and dangerous business of trying to make
that idea into a reality.

Delivered projects need to give value to their users. This involves
interacting with users. And this is a very different kind of activity
than looking at, or smelling, flowers. Users need to get some good, some
value out of using a software product. The nature of the "good" or value
is different for different kinds of products. For example, it's very
different for a social media product than for a government form. But
like fruit, if it doesn't taste right, users will spit it out. Also,
like fruit, if software isn't produced properly, it could do harm to its
consumers.

So how do you do this? How do you take the output of the idea value
stream and use it to construct the product value stream? How do you put
together the value-adding steps? How do you assemble series of actions
that deliver something valuable for the customer?

Well, you have to do two things:

-   *Discover value:* you need to explore and find out what the
    potential users of this product might want.

-   *Construct a value stream:* you need to put some software together
    that can deliver those values.

But then there's a third thing that you need to do. And this is where
all the trouble starts. You need to use the experience that you get from
discovering value and building the value stream. And then you need to do
this:

-   Go back and change the idea.

# Ideas and products fight

In the next chapter (Chapter SWAMP), we'll talk about why, how and with
whom you should work to discover value. Then in (Chapter ESCAPE) we'll
consider how to put these bits of value together and build a value
stream.

Simple, right? Well, it would be, if it weren't for a third thing that
you have to do as a result of these same two steps. You have to deal
with all the flak and fallout that comes from discovering value and
constructing a value stream.

This is a central paradox of project management.

*The ideas value stream and the product value stream fight each other.*

Starting to do the project results in the alienation of the people who
got the project funded. Why? Because when you do things, you uncover
problems with *their idea*. And remember, this is an idea that probably
did not get funded and supported because of its logical value. Rather it
got funded because of its emotional appeal. Or maybe there was some
appeal to something "shiny." Same but different. Faster and cheaper.
Using the latest technology. And a lot of what's going on in that
emotional appeal might be opaque to us.

When we try to implement the idea, very often we'll need to go back and
change the idea. And when we do that, most of the time, we don't know
whose toes we'll be trampling on.

This is especially true of using iterative and incremental approaches.
These approaches to software development are what people call "Agile."
Most of these approaches to project management have a short meeting that
happens every day. This meeting is the team's opportunity to report
problems. These are problems with the idea.

Often the issues that surface in these meetings are practical problems.
The project needs more servers. The developers need access to servers.
There is a new technology. Everybody thought it would work well. It
isn't working at all.

One might think that some of these problems undermine the idea and some
don't. But in some way, all of these problems undermine the idea.
Because they make the reality look more expensive and more difficult to
make real.

On projects that understand the value of their presence, user
researchers get added into this mix. They talk to the users about their
needs. So, pretty soon after the project starts, you begin to get a
picture of what users think of the idea. Maybe they love it, maybe they
hate it, maybe they think about it in a way that is totally
unreasonable. Maybe they point out really good reasons why it won't
work. They think about it in a way that you really didn't anticipate.

What users think about the idea doesn't really matter. Well, it does
matter, but not for the purposes of this discussion. What matters is
that the way users think about the project idea is different, more
detailed, more nuanced even. What the users think is almost guaranteed
not to mirror how the people who had the original idea think about it.

To the people who had the idea, this is going to feel like a threat.
It's only if they have deep and strong experience in product management
that they can avoid negative feelings. Most people who had the idea are
probably going to feel anger and fear. They are "keepers" of the idea.
They are the people who have attached their reputations to getting the
idea funded.

This is a sticky, difficult, delicate business. And we're going to look
at it from a series of different angles, in the hope of reaching a
smarter way of viewing it. But it's always going to be tricky. On one
side, we have dreams. This is the idea's value stream. It's like
flowers. It got funded because of its beauty -- its direct, emotional
appeal.

The other side -- where we're headed -- where we *need* to be headed, is
a product value stream. And this is a value stream that, if it delivers
at all, will deliver something that is slightly, or hugely, different to
the dream. This is like fruit. It needs to be nutritious for those who
consume it. It shouldn't poison them -- or make them sick.

The reality, if it's to be a success, will be intimately involved with
users. It will engage with their enthusiasms. It will be aware of their
capabilities and limitations. If it's going to have a chance of success,
it should also address the interests and concerns of lots of other
stakeholders. Regulators will take an interest when they finally hear
about it. People who might invest will be interested. And of course, so
will the people who came up with the idea.

This is a vision of a project as a balancing act between dreams and
reality. On the one hand are the dreams of the people who had the idea
and funded it. On the other are the wants and needs of the people who
will experience the reality. And between these two is a hard place -- a
very hard place indeed. This is where support from the people who had
the idea is running out. Or has turned into antagonism. But support from
the people who will benefit from the reality has yet to start. This
isn't exactly a "standard" view of project management.

But it is a standard account of something else. It's the basic structure
of stories and adventures.

# In the desert

In a lot of adventures, the hero has a dream, a vision. Or there's
always been a dream hanging around, maybe a prophecy. The hero has no
real idea how they're going to realize that vision. But, after some
reluctance, they set off to try anyway. On the way, they find things (a
sword, a statue, some writing on a wall). People tell them things (an
old man in the forest points in a certain direction). They get hungry,
they eat poisoned, or cursed, or hallucinogenic food. They see visions.
They nearly die. But they survive. And finally, after a lot of
adventures, they start to see the way. They start to get a better
picture of what their quest might be about.

They have lots of struggles and setbacks. They think about giving up.
But they don't. In a final scene, they put all these things together,
save the day and win the prize. Then they go home for whatever is the
nicest meal possible, in that time period, on that planet or in that
culture. This is the basic story, the "monomyth" that Joseph Cambell
(Campbell) talks about in his book "The Hero with a Thousand Faces." You
might recognize this story. It's the story of many successful novels and
films, which you have probably loved.

If you're trying to deliver a project, you're on that kind of adventure.
You might not love this so much. And, weirdly, neither will the people
who had the original idea behind the project. The author Iris Murdoch
captured the pain of this perfectly when she said:

> "Every book is the wreck of a perfect idea"

And the people who funded the project? They might not be pleased about
this "wreckage" at all.

But that's where you are. The idea was just the start. Someone has to
head out on that journey. You have to find what's valuable. You have to
find the jewel, the chalice and the sword. Then you have to throw those
things that you found into the fire in the temple in the right order.
Then and only then can you save the world and go home for tea.

How does this help? How does seeing a project in these terms make
"delivering the impossible" any easier. Remember the quote from Alan
Kay? "Point of view is worth 80 IQ points."

Thinking of a project in terms of two competing value streams is an
interesting and different point of view. One value stream is a virtual
one (the flowers). It deals in dreams. The other is a virtuous one (the
fruit). It needs to be built through the course of the project. This
point of view makes it easier to understand what's going on in a
project. Especially when we think about the difficult trek. This is the
undiscovered country that has to be crossed between the two streams.

In a couple of chapters' time, we'll talk about seeing problems in a
project as pirate ships on the horizon (Chapter SHIP). And we'll talk
about why the "crew members" of a ship (i.e. the team working on a
project) are so often reluctant to report problems.

Why don't the team want to tackle the pirate ship? Because they don't
want to go on the adventure! And why would they? Adventures are scary.
They are uncertain. That sense is compounded if the captain (the boss
who had the idea) has said that this shouldn't be an adventure. What if
the captain makes it clear he doesn't want to hear about anything going
wrong? Then the team gets the message. "Not a word about any problems."
They start to pretend that the idea is perfect. They stop pushing for it
to change in any way. Which of course means that it won't be turned into
a reality.

There is a natural reluctance for teams to go on an adventure. To embark
on an adventure, a team needs encouragement and support. Its members
need to know that it's alright. They need someone who knows the kinds of
things that will happen to them on that journey. They need someone who
is prepared to go with them. Someone who knows that this is what
normally happens on projects. They want someone to acknowledge their
struggles. They need someone to provide them with an environment that
gives them the best possible chance of success. And, if you're a project
manager, that someone is you!

Taking this point of view, seeing a project as constructing a path
through a wild and unknown territory, helps. It helps with one of the
major things that makes any project manager feel bad. This is the
powerful idea of *commitment and consistency* that we talk about in a
later chapter (Chapter COMMIT).

The thing that gets a project funded is an idea. The aspects of an idea
that get it funded aren't very closely, if at all, connected with their
deliverability. Rather they're very closely connected with a certain
kind of "shiny" simplification.

When you understand this, you start to realize that a funded project is
quite simply a signpost. It points in a certain direction. Nobody knows
*exactly* what you're going to find there. Nobody knows if you're going
to find value. If you're the project manager of this "gesture in a
certain direction," what can you possibly promise? What can you possibly
commit to? How can you be consistent?

This isn't a rhetorical question. There are a bunch of things you can
commit to. You can commit to going on the journey and taking the team
with you. You can commit to doing all you can, as a team, to explore the
area that the project points to. This is the "swamp" that we'll talk
about in the next chapter (Chapter SWAMP). And you can commit to finding
what's useful, valuable and attractive there. You can commit to putting
together a prototype value stream.

When you start to look at projects in this way, there is both good news
and bad news. The bad news is that for almost all projects you will have
to go on "the adventure." There is no way of taking the idea, the thing
that got funded through the idea value stream, and just implementing it.
There is almost never enough detail in the idea. In fact, lack of detail
is probably part of the idea's appeal. And then there's even more bad
news. The only way to realize the idea is to extensively explore the
area that it points at. What you're trying to do is *discover* value.
Then, based on all the valuable things you've found, your team needs to
make some decisions.

Oh sure, you'll consult with stakeholders. But ultimately the team needs
to decide which of these things are going to be put together. Which bits
will form a value stream. Finally, you have to build that value stream.
That's a lot of work. And it's work that has to be done while somehow
not upsetting the people who had the idea. Well, at least not upsetting
them too much.

Maybe to realize the idea for this new consumer application, the idea
needed to be brutally trimmed. Only a few core functions are present in
the first working prototype. And then it needed large amounts of
expensive server time. Because the users needed it to respond quickly.
This is a bit like what might need to be done to turn a flower into
edible fruit. The tree needed to be aggressively pruned. Fields needed
to be bought. And the trees needed to be surrounded by an awful lot of
smelly manure.

For the product value stream to deliver, the idea will probably need to
be changed. The idea value stream and the product value stream will have
to fight.

So, lots of bad news. Is there any good news? Well yes, there is.

If you follow this approach, you will have given yourself and the people
you work for the best odds of delivering. You will have given yourself a
chance of success. You'll have done the best you can, even on the most
impossible-sounding projects. Looking at projects in this way might just
give you the bump of 80 IQ points that Alan Kay talked about. It helps
you know what you're doing.

Why are you doing user research? Because that's how we discover values.
Why are you putting together working software and putting it in the
hands of users? Because that's how we take the values that we've
uncovered and start to put them together into a value stream.

Why are your stakeholders so annoyed and hostile to the suggestion that
the idea needs to be changed or improved? Because they are emotionally
attached to it in a way that you probably can't guess. If you're
implementing the idea, you're probably criticizing and undermining the
idea. Even if that wasn't your intention.

A strategy that combines user research and working software can deliver
***something*** for almost any project. It almost doesn't matter how
crazy the initial idea was. But this will almost always need to include
modification of the original idea. And this will involve careful
handling of some very upset senior people.

# Connecting to pull

There's another thing that it's important to understand about this way
of seeing. If we look at things this way, it's the job of the project
manager to try to get the team out of the desert. They find themselves
in a dry, unsupported, no-man's land. This is the place between the idea
value stream and the product value stream. It's the project manager's
job to try to get them out of there as soon as possible. Because if the
project manager isn't careful, lots of projects can get stuck there.

I worked on a project that started off with a feasibility study. Guess
what? It decided that the project was feasible! But it probably
shouldn't have done that.

As part of this feasibility study, we discovered several dead projects.
They'd had a similar goal to our project. And then they had been
canceled. They'd tried to do what we were trying to do. They'd got stuck
in the desert between the idea and product value stream. They'd never
managed to make their work pay off.

But that wasn't the worst of it! Our investigations uncovered a project
that was still going! This project claimed to be doing exactly what our
project was doing. This project was losing funds and support, but it was
still alive! And stuck in the desert.

Of course, that was the point at which we should have read the signs.
And what should have been written on those signs was "abandon hope all
ye who enter here."

Twelve months later, our project found itself in the middle of the same
desert. We couldn't get enough support to continue. We didn't have
enough working software to connect to any customers. Even though we knew
they desperately wanted what we were trying to build. And our project
died in the desert.

Finally, there's one more bit of news that you could get. It doesn't
come to every project, but when it does, it's really good news.

This comes from the infamous veteran sales copywriter Gary Halbert.
Halbert wrote one book -- and he wrote it from jail (Halbert). He tells
a story of a question that he asks people who come along to his
copywriting courses. He sets the scene. These aren't the exact words.
But this is the drift: "You're going to have a hamburger stand, and I'm
going to have a hamburger stand. You can have anything you want to make
your hamburgers sell and I'm going to have just one thing, and I'm going
to beat you every time."

Of course, his students now play what we'd recognize as the "hipster"
burger game. "Kobe beef," "sourdough bread" and "ethical lab-grown
meat." Lots of fancy features on the product. Or they go the
"super-value" way: "buy one, get one free."

Then Gary Halbert tells his students the thing he wants. The thing that
he's going to have that's going to mean his burger stand is more
successful than theirs -- a hungry crowd.

*A hungry crowd.*

This applies to burgers, and it applies to software product development
too. A hungry crowd is the best bit of news you can possibly get. If the
organization that is paying you to deliver this project really needs it,
that's good. If they really need the project to be delivered, that is a
huge benefit. But even better is if the *users* of the product really
need it to be delivered. This is my own personal experience. Sometimes
an organization is desperate for a piece of software. When they are,
they will tolerate the kind of approaches that we've talked about. They
will allow user research; they will allow early release of working
software. They will even grudgingly allow changes to the idea. Notice I
say "tolerate" and "allow." They will, for a while, support these new
ways of working.

If the crowd is hungry for the software and the organization is hungry
for the software, that's the best combination that you can hope for. The
project will have both push from the organization and pull from
customers and users. And maybe the project's time in the desert won't be
too long.

# Conclusion

The value stream is an idea taken from successful manufacturers. They
make familiar products. These are products that people understand.
Products like cars!

The idea of the value stream can be useful when we're thinking about
software development. But it only helps if we understand three important
things. We need to understand that in a software development project:

-   There is no product value stream: it's the job of the project to
    build it.

-   There is another value stream: the project only exists because an
    idea value stream has attracted support and funding.

-   The idea value stream and the product value stream will fight:
    figuring out how to make the product value stream function will
    almost always mean that the idea value stream needs to be changed.

Seeing software development projects in terms of these values streams
helps us to be smarter. It helps in specific ways.

It explains the behavior of the bosses who backed the idea. This would
otherwise be mystifying. Implementing the value stream for a product is
often threatening to the idea. It undermines the idea. It can be
embarrassing.

But seeing things in terms of two value streams and a gap in between
makes it clear what's going on.

And it encourages the right kind of behavior. It tells you what you're
doing.

You're figuring out how to build value and you're figuring out how to
deliver that value to the people who want it. Many times, you're trying
to do that in a hostile environment. It's a desert. The goodwill of the
people who initially had the idea and the people who initially funded
the idea is often running out. But you don't have any goodwill from
users yet. You don't have anything to give them that they want.

We come back to Joseph Campbell's monomyth(Campbell) -- his idea is that
all stories are essentially the same story. And this is helpful in
understand the arc of optimism and struggle that projects will likely
describe .

But if we really want to deliver these projects, we have to pay
attention. It isn't enough to recognize the general structure of all
projects. We also need to understand the local, specific details.

We need to understand what creating a new software project means. It
means building a new value stream that connects its users and customers
to things that they value. And we need to know from users and customers
what those values are.

But to really understand what those values are, we need detailed local
knowledge of the environment that we're operating in. This is another
thing that is often overlooked when discussing Toyota's approach to
building cars in Japan. Toyota's management had visited the Ford
factories in the United States. What they took away from their visit was
that building cars in Japan would have to be different. The market for
cars in Japan was much smaller than in the USA. At least it was to begin
with. And the market was very cyclical, characterized by boom and bust.
This is part of the reason for the invention of "Just-in-Time." And the
idea that the value stream should be conducted at their rate of need
affects how the value stream is constructed. And it decides how the
original idea for the product is revised and modified.

Local details matter. And that is the precise point of the next chapter
-- Chapter SWAMP.
Chapter SWAMP

> When I would recreate myself, I seek the darkest wood, the thickest
> and most impenetrable and to the citizen, most dismal, swamp.
>
> Henry David Thoreau (Thoreau)

Any project that has the potential to be of value has an element of the
unknown about it. It's like trying to build on a swamp. It's a complex
space, with complex geology, history and ownership. The only way to
progress is to acknowledge its complexity and explore.

Imagine that a property developer has given you money to develop houses
in a swamp. How would you go about doing that? Would you just start
work? Or would you feel the need to do a little bit of exploration
first? Wouldn't it be a good idea to know what's in the swamp?

# Who is interested in the swamp?

What if I told you that there were people who already live in the swamp?
And that there's no way that you're going to be able to build without
upsetting them? They don't want you to build there. They're quite happy
with things just the way they are.

But then again, there are also people who are keen to move into the
swamp when you have built something. They've already given the property
developer money. They want you to build in the swamp fast, so they can
move in.

People who live there and people who want to live there both have
interests in the swamp. But they aren't the only ones. There are people
who care generally about bogs, marshes and wetlands. There are people
interested in building regulations. They will be interested in any kind
of engineering or construction. There will be government. There is
always government. Local government will probably want to levy some kind
of tax. You will probably need planning permission. There are people you
need to talk to about getting a road, a power cable, water mains and a
sewer to come right up to the swamp. Because whatever you're doing in
the swamp, the likelihood is that you'll need to connect to existing
infrastructure.

And beyond government, there might even be global bodies that care about
this swamp. There are people who don't care about you, don't care about
the property developers, all they care about is the swamp. These people
might be eco-activists. They might be political activists. The swamp
might be on a burial ground. It might be the site of a buried temple or
a deposit of rare minerals.

And that's just the people who are interested in the swamp. What about
the plants and animals that are already in the swamp? Maybe there are
new species that will be of interest to science. Maybe there are plants,
unknown to western medicine, but the locals use them as a miracle cure.
What about the geology? Maybe there's oil. Maybe there's gold.

Why am I babbling on about swamps? What does this have to do with
software project management? What's the point I'm trying to make?

My point is that there are a lot of people who have varied interests in
what you're doing. Some are directly associated with the project. Some
of those will directly benefit from it, or possibly they will lose out.
But there are also a lot of other people who are only distantly
associated with your project. When you start, you might not have heard
of them and they might not have heard of you, but you're still important
to each other. If the project is going to succeed you will have to make
some of these people happy, even if you upset others.

Maybe there is something that you can do for those people. You could
change their world for the better. Maybe you can find something they're
willing to pay for. By doing that, maybe you can make the organization
you're working for a fortune.

Equally, it might be a bad idea to annoy some of the people who are
interested in this swamp. Maybe you can't avoid it, but it would still
be good to know that it's going to happen.

This is the point that we're making in this chapter. *Projects are
things that are done in a complex environment.* There are lots of people
who are interested in that environment. And the interests that they have
are extremely varied.

# User research --how to find out about the swamp

I didn't really fully understand this until I worked on one particular
project. This involved working with a woman who did research with users.
I'd worked on other projects that had user researchers. But they hadn't
done research like she did it. She *really* did research with users. As
soon as the project started, she contacted potential users of the
product and arranged meetings. She was running workshops with them,
getting to know them and developing relationships. First, she got to
understand their "pain points." Then she got them to start to sketch,
with pen and paper, graphical user interfaces that might start to
address these points.

Then the designer on the team designed interfaces informed by this
research. They looked real, but at this stage they were still only
models. These were again tested with users. If there were serious
problems, they were modified and then tested again.

This kept going. She hired another researcher. They both did research on
users. They did research with internal users in the organization we were
working for. And they did research with external users, who were the
organization's customers. I worked on this project for over three years.
And the research never stopped. It didn't stop when we had working
software that we could test with users. It didn't stop when we had a
version of the software that had a copy of the users' sensitive data
running on it. This gave them a chance to see what it would be like
using the live product. It certainly didn't stop when we went live.

As I said, I'd been involved in projects that had user researchers
before. But this was the first time that I'd seen a project that was
*led* by user research right from the start. And the project continued
to be informed by user research all the way through development.

I learned a lot from watching this happen. And there were a couple of
things that came as a surprise to me. The first was that if you do this
much research on your users, you won't just find out about your users.
You'll get to know a lot about other people who aren't *exactly* users.
To return to our metaphor, these are people who don't live in the swamp,
but are still interested in what goes on there. We found other
regulatory groups that our users needed to please and that our product
had to support. These were people who, even if they weren't users, were
stakeholders. And they went on our stakeholder map.

This was another important idea that I hadn't come across before. If you
do enough research on your users, you can put together a "stakeholder
map." Do even more research on your users and you can put together not
just a map, but a *"stakeholder ecology."* You start to understand not
only who all the people are who have some interest in the swamp, but
also how these stakeholders *interact.* You see what's really happening
in and around the swamp. You see opportunities to do good and to
succeed. Many of these opportunities would be missed, or discovered too
late, if it weren't for these investigations.

And you also see where there are dangers and tensions. You see where
there are problems. You notice unhappy people and people you might
upset. You find out tricky rules and policies that could cause trouble
for you and the project.

This project was the first time that I really understood the value of
user research. Even though I should have understood it about 15 years
earlier. After all, I had previously worked on a user-centered design
project as a researcher. Maybe the thing was that it wasn't exactly a
software development setting. Maybe I'm just a bit slow. But I hadn't
quite joined the dots.

# User research will probably meet with resistance

"What can a ten-year-old teach me?" I was sitting in an office in
Athens, arguing with a Polish software developer in a room full of
technical guys from all over Europe. None of them seemed to think that
we needed to do any user research on a project that had the phrase
"user-centered design" in its title.

It was a European Union-funded project. The first aim of the project was
to design an application involving tangible user interfaces. These are
interfaces that you can get hold of, pick up and move around. There was
a second aim for the project. We wanted to use the interface to put
together discursive arguments. These are arguments that deal with a
controversial topic and discuss the pros and cons of any particular
take.

My organization was doing the user research for the project. As I said,
"iterative, user-centered design" was in the title of the funding bid.
Still, most of the software developers and search engine experts on the
team were skeptical. They didn't see the point of talking to users,
especially since in this case our users were schoolchildren. Still, we
did it.

And what we found was interesting and -- as I realize now is almost
always the case when you do user research -- unexpected. It pointed out
a potential route toward making a successful product. And this was a
route that, if we hadn't talked to users, we would never have imagined.

We found one thing straight away when we talked to the users. In this
case the users were schoolchildren aged 10--11. We found that they
didn't really need help with the logic of an argument. For example, we
discussed the subject of graffiti. And they understood that there was a
contradiction between statements like:

> *People should be able to express themselves freely*

and

> *It's against the law to paint messages on public buildings*

and

> *There are advertising slogans everywhere*

What the kids did seem to need help with was organizing the material
that they collected. They needed help putting the bits of evidence that
they found into a structure that worked as a persuasive argument.

Rather than guessing what the children needed help with, we let the
ten-year-olds teach us. That was the great thing about doing research in
real schools. But when we started to do that research, we found out
about another concerned party. These were people who didn't live in the
swamp (which in this case was a school). The concerned party was a long
way away from this nice school in Leamington Spa in Warwickshire,
England. Yes, this project involved Athens and Leamington Spa. It was a
pan-European project.

> Even so, this concerned party really cared about what happened there.
> Who was this interested party? It was the government. At the time when
> we did this research, the government insisted that all schools in
> England follow a national curriculum. This meant that pretty much
> every lesson the kids attended had to show that it satisfied a
> specific learning requirement. And this learning requirement was
> written in a centralized government document.

We realized this after some very tense discussions with teachers about
early research sessions. We needed to do two things at the same time. We
needed to design our future research sessions so that they addressed the
curriculum. And we needed to do activities that gave us what we needed
for research.

But it wasn't enough to do these two things. We then needed to make it
obvious to the teachers that that was what we were doing. Why? Because
the teachers were also swamp residents, of course! If we did this, we
made it much easier for the teachers to support us and our research.
Otherwise, the number of sessions we'd be able to run with the children
would be severely limited.

Fortunately, we had a former teacher on our team. This allowed us to
design our research sessions carefully. The former teacher told us what
we needed to do to make the lessons work as research while at the same
time working as part of the national curriculum.

# What happens when you don't do your research

A long time later, I found myself working on a software development
project. This company was successful and highly regarded in one area of
business: mobile phone service provision. In the world of mobile phone
service provision, it had a pretty cool image. It also had a very good
reputation for customer service. In a national poll of popularity of
customer brands, it came second, behind a chain of bakers that sold
sausage rolls. Any company that sells sausage rolls in the UK is going
to be very hard to beat in a popularity competition.

Because of its success, some of the senior people at the mobile phone
company wondered if they could succeed in another area: personal loans.

This team did a lot of work developing a personal loan website. But they
hadn't done any user research. Then, in a meeting with a potential
business partner, the partner mentioned a problem. They pointed out that
any company that lends money needs to be regulated by the financial
authorities.

You might well raise your eyebrows slightly at this. A company that
didn't talk to the regulators before starting up in the money-lending
business? Really? But I think that a substantial part of the reason that
this got missed was that they hadn't done enough research. On this
occasion, that might not strictly have been user research. Maybe this
was more connected with filling out the stakeholder map. There was a
stakeholder who was far away. Again, like in the school research
example, it was the government. And if they weren't kept happy, the
project would be a failure. The people who'd had the idea, and the
people who had funded it, hadn't sufficiently explored the swamp.

My bet is that research with users and mapping of stakeholders would
have uncovered this issue. Something as important as "If you want to be
a money lender you have to fill in a big application form" would have
become clear, very quickly. This project would also have seriously
benefited from more traditional user research. If the project had done
that research, maybe it would have reminded everyone of one of the most
crucial points about lending money. This is the fact that many of the
people who want to borrow money are either expensive or impossible to
lend to. They're bad credit risks. This is of course, part of the reason
why illegal lenders -- loan sharks -- charge such high rates of
interest.

But attempts to charge high rates of interest legally lead to the
discovery of other interested parties. It isn't enough to do research
with users. You also have to find all these other people: regulators,
investors and commentators on the ethics of your industry. The interest
rates needed to make lending to people who are a poor credit risk viable
-- in the UK for a while, some companies charged over 1,000 percent --
come with a set of commentators and critics. Debt counsellors, suicide
prevention campaigners and politicians start to get involved.

To return to the swamp metaphor, these are people who don't live in the
swamp. These are people who live miles from the swamp and will never
visit it. But they still care what goes on in there. And if these people
aren't happy, they can sometimes kill a project stone-dead.

## Giving a project the best chance of success

What am I really saying when I advocate for user research, when I talk
about the environment of a project being a swamp? What I'm saying is
that looking outside of a project at its surroundings has important
benefits. We want to give the project the best chance possible of
success. And one way to do that is to examine the outside influences.
What we can do then is change what we do in light of what we find.

But, like the man who asked rhetorically "What can a ten-year-old teach
me?" lots of people will tell you that user research is a waste of time.
Actually, more often than not, they won't say that. They will agree with
you. Yes, yes, user research is very important, but it just isn't needed
on this project. Or they won't bother making any argument, they just
won't for it in their projects. They won't hire people to do it. Lots of
people will see a software development project as merely that -- a
matter of software development.

If you're managing a team in this kind of situation, you should do "user
research" anyway. If you don't have somebody on the team who's a
dedicated user researcher, you should still do some user research. You
should do "user research" with the people in the team that you have.
Maybe they're not perfect subjects. But they're sentient humans
(mostly), and their responses to user interfaces will still be
interesting and useful.

But they aren't residents, or people who have interests in the swamp.
And if your project is going to have a good chance of success, you
really need to speak to swamp people.

You should still push to talk to real users, or where possible include a
user as a product owner or business analyst. OK, it won't be anywhere
near as good as a full program of user research carried out by
professionals. But one of the main aims of doing this research should be
to find out surprising stuff (you will). Then maybe you can take what
you find back to whoever is paying for the project. Maybe you can show
them what you have found and use it as a case for more user researchers
and stakeholder research.

A criticism of my insistence on user research is what might be called
the "faster horse" objection. Henry Ford is reported to have said:

> "If I'd given the people what they wanted, I'd have given them a
> faster horse."
>
> Henry Ford (attrib.)

When I talk about the swamp, I'm not saying that you should give any of
those people associated with it exactly what they want. What I am saying
is that whatever you do has a much greater chance of success if it's
informed by what they want, hate, fear, hope for and wish for.

I've worked on lots of projects where we either haven't done any user
research or haven't done anywhere near enough. It didn't bother me that
much at the time. It's easy to get wrapped up in the problem as it's
presented and not go "looking for trouble" by exploring the swamp. But
all those projects found trouble anyway. Or found no audience for what
got built. When that happened it was because we just didn't understand,
not so much *what* we were doing, but *where* we were doing it. We
hadn't explored the swamp.

And that's exactly why you should be getting user researchers involved
in projects right at the beginning.

Now that I've seen it really work, I'm convinced that user research is
the best way of reducing the risk on a project. We'll talk specifically
about this approach to reducing risks and shortening odds in (Chapter
BET).

But we've already talked about it a bit in (Chapter STREAM). The whole
business of exploring the swamp with user research is about trying to
connect to user "pull." This means understanding which values we need to
bring together in our value stream. What do we need to assemble so that
we have something our users want. Maybe we can find something that they
*really* want. Maybe, if we're lucky, we can find our own hungry crowd.

But it's still going to be hard to persuade the people who are paying
for the project that they need user researchers. And not just one, and
not just at the beginning. They need people talking to users and
potential users all the way through. And feeding back what they find to
the team.

Making sure that the team is well-informed is important. The best chance
of a project succeeding is when the team is aware of what users and
stakeholders want. The chances get even better if the team can put
working software into users' hands. And things are even better still if
feedback from users starts to guide future iterations. But it isn't an
easy ride.

We can discover the wants and needs of some or all of the interested
parties in the swamp. And when we do, it's very unlikely that they'll
all be compatible. There are often going to be a lot of different
possible solutions that fully or partially satisfy these interests.

# Project management and user research

In Agile methods, the final decision about what goes into the product is
supposed to rest with the product owner. We'll talk about the role of
the product owner a lot more in (Chapter BOSS). The idea is that the
product owner listens to the research and feedback coming in from all
directions. Much of this feedback might be conflicting. So, then it's
the job of the product owner to make good decisions about which bits to
acknowledge and which bits to ignore. It's also their job to sometimes
pick a side in a particular conflict. But of course, the product owner
is just another human being. It's very likely that the decisions that
they make won't be perfect.

So, what's the role of a project manager? In a way, it's your job as a
project manager to make sure that these conflicts happen. You need to
support the user researchers in all their efforts to understand what's
going on in the swamp. And if this doesn't uncoversome conflicts, the
user researchers probably aren't looking hard enough. In complex project
space, there will be some conflicts.

But at the same time, you have another important job as a project
manager: you need to look after the team. You need to reassure your team
about conflicts. The ones that emerge from doing research are good,
healthy conflicts. As a team, you may not get things right initially.
How could you? You're working in a complex space that you're only
starting to understand.

But you don't have to understand everything at the beginning. That is
the power of an iterative process. You can develop and expand your
understanding of the stakeholder map. You can start to understand the
stakeholder ecology as you progress through the project.

At every stage, even if you get something very wrong, further user
research will pick it up and allow you to correct it.

But even if you have the funding and resources to do all the user
research that the project needs, there are other kinds of research --
organizational research -- that you should also do. And one extremely
effective way of doing this kind of research is to talk to your
stakeholders about risks.

# Talk to your stakeholders about risks

Talking to senior stakeholders about risk is an extremely good way of
finding out what's going on in the organization.

For a long time, my opinion of risk registers was that they weren't very
useful. I worked on many projects that had a "risk register." My
experience was that this was a document that got written at the very
beginning of a project and then ignored.

Then, at the start of a new project, the product owner informed me that
the organization insisted that we have a risk register. I told him that
I didn't think that they were a good idea. But he gently insisted and
came back with a suggestion he had found online for an Agile risk
register. This was a board with three columns for high, medium and low
risks. At the outset, a group of us got together: me (the project
manager), the product owner, the product owner's boss and a few
additional senior stakeholders. In a one-off workshop, we came up with a
list of risks. We added them to the board and then scored them.

The idea of the score was that it was a combination of things. First was
the likelihood of a risk happening. Second was the "cost" to the project
of fixing the fallout if it did. We expressed that in days of work for
the team. For example, a full security breach might take the whole team
a month to fix. So, we would give that risk a cost of 20 (working days
for the team). Then we gave each risk a probability, expressed as a
percentage. Collectively, we thought that there was a small but not
insignificant chance of this happening, so we gave it a probability
score of 5. So, the overall score for the risk was 100 (20 x 5).

Once we'd done this for all the risks that we'd identified, we ended up
with a three-column board. One column had high risks (\>1,000 points).
Another had medium risks (500--1,000 points), and the third had low
risks. And we also had a total -- the sum of the scores on all the risks
on the board.

Every two weeks, this same group of people took another look at the risk
board. We talked through the risks from highest to lowest and amended
the scores. We also added any new risks that anyone in the group
identified.

In one sense, none of the discussion about specific risk scores
mattered. But in another sense, it was a lifesaver for the project.
That's because, when we talked about the risks and changes to the risks'
scores, we found things out. News about what was going on in the broader
organization got discussed. Stuff that otherwise we might not have been
officially "told," but was relevant to the project. Often these were
things to do with budgets and deadlines. Sometimes we were being asked
to make text changes. They appeared strange to us. But it turned out
they were being made to pacify distant but influential pressure groups.

# Demonstrate working software

Scrum is the most popular Agile framework. And "show and tell" is one of
its names for a meeting where the team demonstrate working software to
stakeholders. If you're doing user research, it's also an opportunity to
talk about the findings that you got from that research. Invite as
broadly as you can. Invite people from across the organization. And even
include the public, if that's possible. Demonstrate to yourselves and to
anyone who will watch and listen. Talk through the findings of your user
research. And, actually, it doesn't have to be working software.

Walk through the designs that have been created, informed by that user
research. Demonstrate the working software that your team has been
developing over the last few weeks. The development team is often
reluctant to do this, especially if what it's working on doesn't have an
obvious UI. It's still important that it does it.

Just one observation or a comment from a user might let you know
something that's vital for your project. This could be the thing that
you find out that could derail your project -- or make it really shine.
There's a natural tendency to avoid asking, to avoid finding out. But
really, when would you rather know? Sooner, or later?

# Embrace your deadlines

Yeah, I said it. Embrace deadlines as opportunities to negotiate scope
and set software free. But also, you'll find out more about the swamp.
Some bosses see deadlines as opportunities to mess with you. They want
to make you and your team work longer hours while giving themselves time
off from thinking about what a product really needs to do. Some bosses
just see the promise of delivering for a deadline as a way of getting
their own boss off their back. But you don't have to see them like that.
You can see them as powerful, if slightly clumsy, ways of exploring the
stakeholder map. The map of the swamp.

If you do try to release something, who jumps out and tells you that you
can't? Who suddenly appears and tries to take credit? Who objects to you
doing anything on the grounds of security? Are there issues with
accessibility? Does anybody object on the grounds of secrecy? Is anyone
worried about reputation? Which requirement really is a deal-breaker?

That's the thing that, if it's not right, will stop the software from
going live. Software wants to be free. You should want it to be free.
You should do your best to help it. We'll talk more about this in
