(Chapter ESCAPE).

# Exploit the crisis

What if your team releases software that nobody wants to use? What if
your team releases software that nobody is allowed to use because it
doesn't meet regulations? Well, then, you have a crisis. And each crisis
is an opportunity to iterate your software and improve it. One way to
think about this is that sooner or later, all successful software
becomes iterative and incremental. Software listens to feedback from its
users. Or it dies.

This is the point of comparing a software development project with a
development in a swamp. A software development project is, to some
degree, building on uncharted ground. The people funding the project
will rarely talk about it in such terms. It's not clear what's hidden in
the waters of the swamp. It might be untold riches. It might be a
virulent disease. But whatever is found there, lots of people are going
to have an opinion about it. The people who already live there, the
people who want to live there -- they have opinions. They have things
that they value.

The same goes for some people who will never live there. They still have
opinions and interests. This might be because they are in power. It
might be because they want to preserve some aspect of the environment.
There might be some other reason entirely. Sometimes that reason would
be difficult to uncover without research. It would certainly be
difficult to fully understand.

Sometimes, it's only in crisis moments that what's really going on in
the organization reveals itself. Moments like those around deadlines, or
a negative, or surprising response to a demonstration of working
software. These finally make it clear what the people that had the idea
or the people who funded the idea are really trying to do.

# Conclusion

The message of this chapter is that the place pointed to by the dream of
a project is a mysterious and complex one. It's a swamp. And the way to
give your project the best chance of thriving in this mysterious place
is to explore it through research. You figure out what the project needs
to do through user research and frequent releases of working software.

Talk to as many of the interested and potentially interested parties as
you can. Understand how their interests and values mesh -- or conflict.
Find out as much as you can about the lay of the land. And one way to do
this most effectively is to bring into the team "professional
surveyors," i.e., user researchers who can talk to these parties. But
talking to interested parties isn't just the responsibility of user
researchers. It's also your responsibility as project manager. Project
management is about making sure people talk about what they need to talk
about. At least, that's one way of looking at it. And one way of
improving the chances of a project succeeding is by enhancing the
quality of those conversations. Those conversations that you have with
the people who have interests and a stake in the project. These are the
people who own the swamp. Or live in the swamp. Or even though they are
miles away, still care about the swamp.

This is the challenge that we've talked about in this chapter. To go out
into the wilderness. To explore the swamp. To find out what's going on
there, in the hope of building a product that can not only survive but
thrive. But this isn't enough.

Our projects are software development projects. We need to use our
understanding of the swamp to build software. And then we need to put
that working software into the hands of users. Our researchers and our
inquiries can give us a good idea of what our users want. And these
investigations can give us a good idea of how our users will react to
what we build. But until we actually see them using the real software,
we won't know for sure.

When we try to put working software in the hands of users, we'll often
discover a whole extra layer of rules and regulations. We might find a
whole new group of interested parties who try to stop us from putting
working software into the users' hands. And then our new challenge, the
one that we'll talk about in the next chapter, becomes clear.

We need to help software escape.
Chapter ESCAPE -- Working software

> *"Sufficiently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic."*
>
> Arthur C. Clarke

# When will it be ready?

I worked on a project for an organization that was still doing all its
business using paper documents. It processed a lot of paper. The project
was trying to digitize just one of these paper processes. The aim was to
improve it using an electronic document storage system.

The project had a couple of good things going for it. Firstly, it was
using an iterative, incremental, Agile approach. Secondly, it had a
product owner. She was the person responsible for making decisions about
what the product did. And she was a former clerk of the company. That
might sound like a junior role, but in this organization, the clerks had
clout. She knew all the other clerks and she knew their business very
well.

To start with, the project had a tough time. We couldn't get servers.
So, we had nowhere to put the software. The open-source document
management system that we'd chosen wasn't as mature as we thought. We
pushed on through some early design iterations. We dealt with technical
problems. Finally, we got to one "show and tell" where the team had
working software to show.

It was an odd turning point in the project, because that first demo was
so terrible. We tried to demo viewing a collection of documents. The
next step was to click on a document title, and the full document was
supposed to be displayed.

In that first demo, when we clicked on the document link, a window came
up that said, "Do you want to *veiw* \[sic\] your document?" together
with "OK" and "Cancel" buttons. Yes, the dialog window had that spelling
mistake. When the user clicked "OK" an error message appeared.

The look on the product owner's face! We were about half a million
pounds into a two million pound project. And all she had to show was a
misspelled dialog box and an error message. It was a hard time for her.
It was embarrassing for the team.

Two weeks later it was a slightly different story. Now there was a list
of documents. When the document name was clicked, the prompt to open the
document had a button that was spelled correctly. Click the button and
the document was displayed!

The product owner seemed a bit more relaxed.

Not too many "show and tells" after that, she had a question:

> "Can I get this on a laptop so I can show it to the clerks?"

The short answer to that question was "no." Because all of this nearly
half a million pounds worth of software was on developer laptops. We
were in dispute with the client over who should pay for server space. It
hadn't been included in the original bid. The client was arguing the
servers were implied, and that we as suppliers should pay.

But the product owner wanted a version she could take around the country
and show off. And we wanted to give the product owner what she wanted.
It would support us in our negotiations over server space.

Once the software was on her laptop, the product owner went on the road
to show it to her colleagues. The demo still wasn't great. It had bugs.

But the demo to former workmates went well. She could shrug off any user
interface issues or problems. She could give the clerks first sight of
something that could make their lives easier.

The product owner came back with a list of problems she found while
doing the demo. She also had a list of suggestions for features
requested by the clerks. And she had one question: *"When will it be
ready?"*

That was the point that the project changed. It wasn't about delivering
a list of functions anymore. It was about getting this software out to
the clerks. It was about giving them this tiny bit of working software.
The path of what we needed to do rolled out before us. And the people we
were delivering it to were cheering us on. We'd found our hungry crowd.

There was another very interesting thing about getting working software
in front of its potential users. By doing this, we both asked and then
started to answer two important questions: "Can we do this?" and "Who is
going to try to stop us if we do?"

To the first question, the answer at first was "no". We didn't have any
server space. We couldn't deploy a live service.

But suddenly the people who were trying to make us pay for the servers
were in the way of their own colleagues. Now they weren't helping the
company by being tough on costs. They were blocking people in their own
organization. They were stopping them from getting something that could
make their lives easier. The servers appeared, and then money for people
to support the servers appeared. A real rollout of the software started
to happen.

The answer to the second question, "If we try to do this, who will try
to stop us?" was "several people." There was an accessibility guy and a
security guy.

The accessibility guy claimed that there was no way the software could
be released until it met an extra set of requirements. Then, true to
form, the client claimed that we should have known about these
requirements. And of course, we should pay for them.

Then the security guy appeared, as if by magic. He said that the project
should never be allowed to go live until we could prove that it was
secure. He didn't leave us in any doubt about his effectiveness as a
blocker. He refused to tell us what it was we needed to change so that
it would be secure. And of course, the cost of any changes we made would
have to be borne by us rather than the client.

As people who stop projects in their tracks go, these two looked pretty
effective. Both were telling us we couldn't release until we did what
they said. Both were telling us that we had to pay to do what they said.

But neither of these guys were a match for the clerks. There were a lot
of clerks. They'd seen that this software would make their lives easier.
We kept improving the accessibility, but we went live with what we had.
We submitted the software to outside security testing. We addressed the
most serious issues that arose, but we went live with some other, less
serious issues.

# Pivoting from push to pull

Here's what happened in the example above.

*Putting software in front of real users completely changed the
project.*

Why? Because it started to build the product value stream that I talked
about in (Chapter STREAMS). Putting real working software in front of
real users created *pull.* And that completely changed the dynamic (and
control) of the project.

This is how you deliver the impossible. This is how you make a project
that seems impossible, possible. You build something -- it only has to
be a small thing -- and you put it in the hands of users. And at some
point, you start to get pull. From that point on, the dynamic of the
project has completely changed. Your project is starting to shift from
being a dream to being a reality.

How do you do this on *your* project? Well, you can give yourself and
your team a month or so to do some set-up. If after not much more than a
month, you and your team don't have *something* that works, you've
waited too long. Or you've tried to put too much into that first piece
of software. By the end of that first month or so, you need a tiny piece
of working software. A small bit of software that starts to do the thing
that it's supposed to be doing. And you need to be able to show this
software to the people who will ultimately use it. Ideally, you should
let them use it themselves.

# Changing the dynamic

In the beginning of a project, the sole drivers for the functionality in
your software are the stakeholders who originated the project.

And that's how it stays, right up to the point where users find
something in your software that they like, want or need. Up to that
point, you're in "push" mode. In "push" mode, everything is being driven
by the people who originated the project and the people who got it
funded.

Once you have connected with users and shown them the software, things
are different. They have seen things, or even hints of things, that they
want. Now you are in "pull mode." This doesn't necessarily mean that you
should give users everything that they want. But it does mean that the
dynamic of the project has changed.

When we make working software available early, we're giving it an
opportunity to connect with its audience. We're trying to create "pull."
We're being guided by the people who use it, in the direction of
delivering more and more value.

This is a powerful reason for delivering working software early. But it
isn't the only one. There are other very good reasons why we should try
hard to put software in the hands of users as soon as we possibly can.

# The prison break martini principle

Here's a joke.

> "You will never be alone if you always carry the ingredients and
> equipment to make a martini. Even if you think you are completely
> stranded on a desert island. Because the minute that you start to make
> the martini, someone will jump out from behind a palm tree and say:
> 'That's not how you make a martini.'"

Something like this is true of software. You want your software to be in
the environment where it will ultimately be used. That means running on
the computers that it's going to be running on when it's live. You want
it to be accessible to real users. That means using their real data, on
the real software.

But the nearer you get to doing that, the more likely it is someone will
jump out from behind a palm tree. They will tell you that "you're doing
it wrong." They will try to stop you getting working software into the
hands of users.

This is why this chapter is called "Escape". We can imagine the job of
getting working software out into the working environment to be like
trying to escape from a prison camp. There's only one way to find out
for sure what all the traps are out there in no-man's land, and that's
by setting them off.

In the film "The Great Escape," (The Great Escape) Squadron Leader
Bartlett (played by Richard Attenborough), is masterminding a huge
escape attempt for many prisoners. He persuades Captain Hilts (played by
Steve McQueen) -- a loner who's attempting to escape on his own -- to
escape and then get caught. Bartlett asks Hilts to do this so the larger
escape attempt can be informed by precise details of what's just outside
the camp fence. The potential escapees have good maps of Germany. But
they don't know many local details. For example, they don't know the
exact direction of the railway station.

Your team may be in a very similar situation in their attempts to help
their software escape. They may know a great deal about how the internet
works, and how to build applications for the cloud. But the local
details of how to get permissions to actually deploy something, and
where to deploy it, might be a total mystery.

Why am I talking about cocktails? And prison camps? Why am I talking
about those at the same time? I am certainly mixing my metaphors. I'm
doing this because the good way of seeing that we're talking about in
this chapter is a combination of both. It's a combination of an escape
attempt with someone jumping out from behind a palm tree and telling you
that you're doing it wrong.

In software development, most of those things that the man who jumps out
from behind a tree will bring up are called "non-functional
requirements," and these are the things that will stop your software
from escaping. These are requirements that the software needs to meet
but that aren't strictly about the thing that it does.

-   The software needs to be accessible to users with visual
    impairments.

-   The software needs to be secure.

-   The software needs to comply with a bunch of regulations specific to
    a particular industry.

One way to tackle all the rules and regulations that your software might
contravene is to try to take care of them in the specification. This is
the software engineering approach.

We could try to think of all the things that might stop us getting
working software out into the wild. This list would, of course, contain
things that we already know about - "known knowns." And it might also
contain things we know might be an obstacle -- even though we don't have
any details. These are the "known unknowns."

But what you can't put on the list are the things you don't even know
you don't know.

> Thomas Schelling

These are things that are going to stop your software from getting out
into the world. They are things that you don't even know exist. These
are what the US Defense Secretary Donald Rumsfeld famously referred to
as "unknown unknowns."

Trying to help software escape reveals the unknown unknowns. Here are
some real examples from projects that I've worked on.

-   In order for the site to go live, someone senior in the organization
    has to sign to say that they accept the risk. No one in the
    organization is willing to do that.

-   All applications in your field are regulated by a body that you've
    just heard of for the first time. The senior stakeholder was trying
    to keep the project secret from them, but now they've found out
    about it.

-   The live server is set up differently from the test servers. It's
    set up in such a way that at first, it looks like everything is
    fine. But over time, it gets slower, and slower. And slower. Until
    eventually it stops working.

This is a list of unknown unknowns from my own experience. Of course,
it's hard to say what these things will be, because by their nature they
are a surprise. You knew there would be other tripwires out there in
no-man's land, but you didn't know what they would be. And for some of
those things, there really was only one way to find out. Trying to put
out working software is an important way of understanding the
environment that the project lives in.

This is the counterintuitive thing that I'm trying to tell you. You
should try to get working software as near as you can to live. Why?
Because if you do that you'll be surprised. A man will jump out from
behind a tree and tell you why you can't.

By getting your software into the hands of users, you will certainly
hear from those who try to stop you. But you will also get the answers
to a whole bunch of other questions:

-   Does the technology that we've decided to use actually work?

-   Is the organization that we're working with capable and willing to
    pay? Is it ready to pay for the servers, people and set-up that we
    need to deploy this software to live?

-   And, perhaps most importantly, what is the value of this software?
    What does it mean to its users? Does anybody want it? Will anybody
    pay for it?

# The importance of working software

At the turn of the millennium, a bunch of men (they were all men) got
together in a ski lodge in Utah. There they wrote the Agile manifesto.

Manifesto writing isn't normal behavior for a bunch of guys who go to a
conference in a ski lodge. More normal behavior would be to drink too
much, watch some sport and even go skiing.

But the signatories of the Agile manifesto were struggling with a
difficult question:

*"How can we make project management of software development simpler and
more effective?"*

They wanted something that was simple and lightweight that increased the
chances of success. And maybe that's why the Agile manifesto itself
isn't big. It's 68 words long. And it contains four key values. Working
software is the second one.

The thrust of the Agile manifesto is "people first." That's why its very
first value is:

***"Individuals and interactions over processes and tools"***

Good software comes out of conversations between people. That's our
message about user and stakeholder research that we talked about in
(Chapter SWAMP). But right after that important message, the manifesto
talks about working software:\
***"Working software over comprehensive documentation"***

Why? What caused the people who wrote the Agile manifesto to specify
"working software" as one of their four values?

They had had bitter experiences (Hohl). They had worked on projects
where working software had been delayed or had never been delivered.
They had worked on projects that struggled to get going. Months or years
could go by before a specification was agreed and development could
start. Then they'd had to wait more months or years before anyone who
was supposed to use the software got to touch it.

It\'s worth mentioning here the final two values in the Agile manifesto.

***"Customer collaboration over contract negotiation"***

And:

***"Responding to change over following a plan"***

Customer collaboration over contract negotiation is something that we'll
focus on directly in (Chapter COMMIT), and we'll discuss a helpful way
of seeing and understanding all four Agile values in (Chapter SOME). But
responding to change over following a plan is directly relevant to our
current discussion. When you're trying to help software escape, part of
what you're doing is trying to learn about new obstacles.

This is exactly what's going on in "The Great Escape." The would-be
escapees have good general plans, but they're aware that they don't know
crucial details. They don't know the direction of the railway station.
They are trying to send Steve McQueen's character out of the camp, with
the expectation of getting caught but the aim of learning more about
what they don't know. They are planning to respond to change.

Planning to change the plan in response to new information? Why bother
putting that in a manifesto? Is it really that noteworthy or radical?
This seems like an extremely sensible approach both to escaping from
prison and to putting software into the hands of users. It's especially
sensible in a complex environment that we don't fully understand. But
when the Agile manifesto was written, the idea of planning to change
plans *did* sound relatively radical. This was because, at the time, the
most common way of seeing software development projects was as a kind of
engineering.

# Software development isn't software engineering

How do you do a software development project? You write a high-level
requirements document. Then you write a detailed requirements document,
then you write a design document. Then, once there's agreement that
these documents are "done," the developers can start to code at last.

For the first 40 or 50 years of software development, that's how people
thought it should be done. Well, initially, as with any new field,
people did whatever they liked. But then there was a push to become more
organized and respectable. What people were doing started to be called
"software engineering."

In many kinds of engineering, nothing is built before detailed plans
have been finalized and signed off by a senior architect or engineer.
This is what makes the second principle of the Agile manifesto so
revolutionary. It is saying the engineering equivalent of "have a go at
building a bridge and see how you get on." And "as soon as you've got a
prototype bridge, let people have a go at driving over it." This has
been tried as an approach to bridge building! In the early days of
bridge building -- as with any new field --the approach wasn't
formalized. And many bridges collapsed.

As with ad-hoc bridge construction, a lot of early attempts at software
development were a failure. And might that explain the enthusiasm of
software developers for borrowing the respectability -- and perceived
safety -- of civil engineering.

But what the Agile manifesto is saying is that software works
differently. OK, there might be some extremely safety-critical software
that benefits from a strict model, maybe. But most software -- ecommerce
websites, dating apps, games -- isn't like building a bridge. It has a
different logic. It has different needs, different safety levels and a
different potential path to success.

The people who wrote the manifesto had painful experience of what
happens if you don't push for working software. When software only gets
delivered at the end of a project, the user response is often negative.
It's not what the users want. Or it's not what the users want *now.*
They wanted that *several years ago* when the specifications were
written. But the world has changed.

Maybe it wasn't only bad experiences that encouraged the people who
wrote the manifesto to insist on working software. Maybe they'd had some
positive experiences. Maybe they'd seen the magical way that people
respond to early working software.

The understanding that there is a difference between the kind of
engineering that's used in building bridges and the kind of engineering
that's used in software development is made explicit in Ken Schwaber's
book, "Agile Software Development with Scrum" (Schwaber). Defined
process control is the kind of engineering control that is used to build
bridges. Defined process control specifies requirements in detail up
front. It then goes about trying to implement those specifications.

This is what most people would think of as engineering. It measures
success by how exact the match is between those initial specifications
and the final product. But Schwaber points out there is another kind of
process control: empirical process control. Empirical process control is
the kind of control that should be used in environments that are highly
uncertain, where it would be sensible to expect the unexpected.
Empirical process control acknowledges that the outcome can't be
achieved by close definition up front. Rather, it focuses on
transparency, inspection and adaptation as an approach to delivering
*something* that is valuable and effective in a particular complex
environment. This idea that we're working in a complex, uncertain
environment of course echoes our discussion in (Chapter SWAMP). And
we'll focus directly on the importance of transparency, inspection and
adaptation in (Chapter CAR).

But for now, I want to focus on one very important implication of
Schwaber's analysis of the difference between defined process control
and empirical process control. In empirical process control, we need to
try things early on to check our understanding and inform what we do
next. To understand empirical process control is to understand that we
need to learn from early versions and that we need to inspect and adapt.

One particularly important implication for software development is that
looking at a list of features, even asking *users* to look at a list of
features, is very different from asking users to react to working
software. Part of the reason for that is the inherent uncertainty of how
those features will interact with a complex, uncertain environment. But
part of it is something else. Users don't respond in the same way to a
list of features as they do to working software. Working software is
like magic.

# Working software is like magic

Arthur C. Clarke said that sufficiently advanced technology is
indistinguishable from magic. Working software would surely qualify as
this kind of advanced tech. And the way that users respond to working
software is evidence of this.

They respond to it instantly. They respond automatically and with
emotion. When they press a button or click on a picture, they have an
expectation of what will happen. Working software reveals new user
needs. It sets expectations. It evokes delight, joy, frustration and
anger. This is very different from the way people react to a checklist
of functions in a requirements document. Pretty much the only way to
find out how users are going to respond to something is to put actual
working software in their hands.

And when you do that, users will find ways of using it that you didn't
imagine. They'll want it to do more than it currently does, in
directions you didn't foresee. When people like your software, they want
more of it. Or they want the demo they've seen in a sandbox to be real.
They want it on their phones. And they want to use it with their own
real data. Working software helps you discover value and create "pull"
from users. It will give your project the best chance of success. But
this magic can only happen if the software gets into the hands of its
users. And for many reasons, putting working software in the hands of
users encounters strong resistance. It's often a struggle.

# Meet the resistance

Trying to promote software, trying to get it out into the real world
will annoy a lot of people.

And this is an important thing to understand about getting working
software into the world.

*Very few people will like you if you do this, but it is something that
you have to do.*

Who will get annoyed? Here's just a short list. It is by no means
exhaustive.

Let's start with the people who had the original idea. And the people
who funded the original idea. They'll feel very uncomfortable, to put it
mildly. Because it might start to seem obvious that this idea is very
expensive to realize. Why might that happen? Because *all software is
expensive to realize.* Sorry if you weren't aware of that dark secret.

Maybe you start to push working software nearer to real users. Or maybe
you push the working software nearer to using live data. And as you do,
it becomes obvious that there are some superficial problems with it.
Maybe these are things like spelling mistakes or logos that unwittingly
turn out to look stupid or rude. Maybe the users' data looks dumb in the
new format.

Or maybe there are some fundamental problems. You start to get this
software near a live environment and it becomes obvious that this
project isn't such a great idea, or that the technology it relies on
will never perform in the way imagined.

Senior stakeholders have a lot invested in the project. They are linked
into the hierarchy of the organization. Or they are connected to a group
of investors in ways that mean they'll be exposed when the software
finally goes live. It doesn't matter that the only way to be successful
is to release the software. Trying to release the software by going
through various steps of promotion will still upset a lot of people.
Even people who claim that they want the software.

Trying to release software will also upset people on your team. User
researchers might want lots more user research before anything is
released. Designers will probably want an end-to-end flow. They might
want a coherent look and feel for the whole thing before anything is
released.

Developers will tell you that there's no point releasing just a bit of
this thing. You might as well wait until the whole thing is ready.
Testers will tell you that they haven't tested it enough.

This is an important thing to understand about working software. The
business of trying to get it into live, which is absolutely the thing
that you should be doing, is going to annoy a lot of people. In fact,
the only people who might not be annoyed with you trying to push working
software into the hands of users are users themselves.

Users want you to push some new software that makes their lives easier.
Users want you to push some new software that entertains them. Users
want you to push some new software that makes it easier for them to find
a restaurant or find love. They want to find the perfect gift for their
family or find a job. If you can push software to the point where they
can access it, where they can see it and want it, you can create pull.
You can put something in their hands that helps them to do what they
want or need to do. And maybe you'll make them happy.

But what if you don't make them happy? What if they're highly annoyed?
Or what if they're utterly indifferent to the software that you put in
front of them? What if they laugh, snigger, shout, scream, cry, moan or
yawn?

There's still an important reason why it's worth pushing software as far
as you can, as near the outer fence of reality as possible.

The reason that it's worth doing that is because... Well, the reason is
a rhetorical question.

> "When do you want to find out that nobody is interested in this piece
> of software? When do you want to find out that they hate it? Sooner?
> Or later?"

Surely it's better to know the reaction of the users when you are ten
percent of the way through the project? At that point, you've still got
a chance to shift position and change. You've got a chance to try
something else. What if you leave it until later? What if you only find
that the customers hate your software when you are a hundred and ten
percent of the way through? This is a point when you've spent all the
money. It's a point where it's much harder to do anything about the
things that you've discovered.

You're going to annoy a lot of people by pushing working software. But
still, the best chance that you have of pleasing users is by putting
software in their hands, as soon as you can and as often as you can.

# Surprise!

What? This feels a bit abrupt. This section doesn't seem to follow on
directly from the previous section. It's a surprise. And a surprise is
what we're looking for when we let software escape. Yes, paradoxically,
we're *expecting* to be surprised. When we try to push software as far
as we can out into the real world, we will be surprised -- and that is
because software is treated like magic.

This chapter started with a quotation from the science fiction author
Arthur C. Clarke. Advanced technology isn't magic. But in many
situations, it's indistinguishable from magic. The way that we respond
to it certainly makes it seem that way. That is Clarke's idea. And
that's absolutely the case with software. People will react to your
software in ways that you never imagined. They will do things with it
that you didn't expect them to do.

They will react to software in a way that is completely different from
the way they would react to a written specification.

You will find yourself thinking: What was that? What did she just do?
Why did she do that? They'll put things together in ways that you didn't
expect. Users will totally ignore or be unimpressed by parts of the
software that your team struggled with and worked hard to get right. But
at the same time, they'll get enthusiastic about parts of the software
that you didn't think were a big deal.

Part of the point of pushing software as far as you can, pushing it out
into a live environment is to be surprised.

But of course, some of those surprises won't be pleasant. It's like the
prison camp. As you try to get the software out past the fences, you'll
discover more obstacles. Often these obstacles will take the form of
people who appear from nowhere. They'll tell you that you need to comply
with a certain regulation.

Other people will tell you that you must pass an assessment of some
sort, or that you must answer a questionnaire. Questionnaire, you say?
That's no big deal. Oh, no? One of these questionnaires that I
encountered was 500 pages long.

All those kinds of things will happen, and that is part of why you're
trying to get the software as near live as you possibly can. By trying
to promote software, you're aiming to be surprised. But that doesn't
mean that the experience won't be unnerving. And it doesn't mean that a
lot of people connected with the project won't get upset when the
surprises arrive.

# Promotion -- taking things to the next level

Once you've got this tiny little bit of working software, you should be
looking to promote its status in some way. By that, I mean you should be
trying to get the software to be used by more users. Or you should be
setting the software up so that it's using live, or like-live data. Or
you could be making its deployment more automatic so it can run on more
than one machine and in more than one environment.

What's important is that you've got something and it's moving in all the
right directions. Sure, it needs to be progressing in terms of
functionality. But it also needs to be moving from developer
environments, to test environments. And then it needs to be moving onto
the final live environment where it will run. By doing this, you're
*[promoting]{.underline}* the software. You're taking it to the next
level. You're equipping it with what it needs to escape.

And it needs to be moving from being tested by the team, to being tested
by "friendly" users, to being used by complete strangers. By doing this,
you're promoting the software. You're helping it escape and helping it
get into the hands of its users.

If it's a business application, it needs to move from using dummy data,
to using data that looks like live data, to using real data. Again, this
is promotion.

I know nothing about rock climbing. But this seems to me to be a little
bit like if you're climbing a huge rockface. You climb a bit, then you
put in one of those things that holds the rope to the rock. I think
they're called "nuts." Well done climbers for naming those things.

Well, whatever they're called. When this thing, the nut, is in, you tug
on it to make sure it's firm and would hold you if you fell, and then
you can climb a bit more. This is like making sure that software will
run on test servers, and once we're happy with that, moving on to making
sure it will run on live servers.

Yes, delivering software is a bit like that. It's a bit like climbing a
rock face. And it's like escaping a prison camp. And there's a guy
jumping out from behind a palm tree to criticize your martini. I know, I
know, too many metaphors.

# Promotion in four dimensions

Once we've understood the idea of promotion, we can start to understand
that it doesn't just happen in one dimension. Yes, there's the dimension
of "works on my machine" to test environment, like-live environment and
then live environment. But there are other dimensions that we need to
consider. The most obvious is functionality. What we're looking for is
software that starts with a tiny amount of functionality, but which
gradually increases in capability.

This increase in the capability and functionality of the software is
often the focus of measures of progress. Stakeholders, especially the
shouty, demanding ones, often confuse speed and velocity. When they ask
for an increase in velocity, they are often really just wanting an
increase in the speed of development of functionality. The difference
between speed and velocity is something that we'll talk about in
(Chapter ALL) and (Chapter SOME). But it's worth starting to talk about
it here. In physics, "speed" is movement in one direction. A statement
of speed doesn't say which direction anything is traveling. When we hear
a statement like "he was driving at 40mph," we're hoping that he's going
in the same direction as the road.

And in software development, very often the focus of attention is the
rate at which new features get added. But velocity is a collection of
speeds in different directions. When we're trying to put a lander on the
surface of the moon, those directions are horizontal speed and vertical
speed -- x and y -- at least they were in the game that I used to play
on my first computer. Of course, if we were landing on a real moon,
velocity would be in three dimensions.

When we're trying to "land" a project, there are a bunch of different
dimensions that our software needs to be moving in -- at least four, and
probably more.

## Dimension 1 -- Functionality

This is the dimension that most people focus on. And it's often used as
a measure of progress and success. But focusing on delivering
functionality while neglecting the other dimensions is a very dangerous
strategy.

The specific danger is that as the project accrues more and more
functionality, it gets harder and harder to help it "escape" into the
world.

The simple reason for this is that the bigger the software gets, the
more there is to go wrong.

Say we just have one, single bit of functionality. Then maybe we've got
just one or two applications that need to be deployed and configured.
And then we need to test the functionality and the whole deployment in
the new environment. This is a lot of work. But it's possible.

But what if we wait until the application is ten times the size of that
original, small application? By then we've maybe got half a dozen
different bits of the application that need to be deployed on the new
server. And maybe we have as many different bits of configuration. And
testing the whole thing to make sure that it works in the new
environment is now a much bigger job.

That's why it's much better to deploy a small amount of functionality
*all the way* -- to a live, or like-live, environment -- as soon as you
have some tiny bit of functionality working. And then, at each stage,
it's also a good idea to write automated regression tests. And, where
possible, we should also be trying to capture as much as we can of the
state of the server as "infrastructure as code."

That is, we use special software and special configuration files to
capture the exact state of the server and software setup. This means
that we can reliably recreate the whole server from scratch, if we need
to.

If we do this, when the next bit of functionality is added, the work
required is roughly as big as the new bit that got added. It isn't an
amount of work equivalent to reconstructing the whole thing.

## Dimension 2 -- Data

It's fine to start off with dummy data. But at some point, the data
needs to start to look like real data and users need to start seeing the
software with realistic-looking and then real data. At some point they
also need to start seeing their data change. When they press "save" --
they can see they data is saved. We should move to this stage with
users, even if this is in a safe environment where everything can be
rolled back. And then finally, the users need to be able to "play for
keeps": they need to use the software with real data.

The kind of data that you're using needs to be promoted. And that
probably means step changes rather than the continuum that we see with
functionality.

I worked on a project that dealt with sensitive data. It was a project
where the development was informed by user testing all the way through
the process. There were all sorts of legal and procedural difficulties
surrounding getting access to the real data. Which meant that most of
that testing was with "dummy" data.

But experience from other projects meant that we knew we had to test the
software with the users' real data. So, we arranged to test with users,
with their real data. First, we did this in a situation where it was
read-only. Then in a situation where users could make changes to their
real data, but these were "harmless" changes -- they would be wiped out
and rolled back at the end of the test.

Finally, the users got to use their data in a real environment where
changes were retained and really mattered. At each stage, we learned
something new about the software and how the users were using it. Dummy
data is fine for checking that the software works at all and works like
we expect it to work. But when the users looked at their own real data
in the new system, they saw all sorts of things that the old system
didn't show them. Embarrassing things, formatting errors, spelling
mistakes, duplications.

And this stage was also useful for the development team. Titles were
much longer than we thought they would be. Values that we'd been told
were unique turned out not to be unique. "There can only be one head of
department -- ok, yes, but of course in this case, we can see on screen,
there are three heads of department."

The "reversible changes" phase made it possible to try different changes
to the data and see how they looked before finally putting these into
the live system.

## Dimension 3 -- Users

We can start our testing with the developers, and then maybe we ask
people within our organization to use the software. But at some point,
we need the software to be used by "real" users. Maybe at first those
are "friendly" users -- people we know. But then, pretty soon, we need
the software to be used by its target audience.

We need to be pushing our software out along this dimension as far as we
can.

And we need to be doing this for two vital reasons.

The first is because this is the dimension that allows us to connect to
pull -- to the real desire for the software that we're building. This is
genuinely the dimension where the "magic" happens.

The second -- and maybe this is connected to the first -- is that this
is where we'll find the most resistance. Trying to get this software
into the hands of real users is where we'll find the most mines,
tripwires and trained attack dogs. And it's the thing most likely to
cause someone to jump out from behind a tree and tell us that we can't
do that.

## Dimension 4 -- Environment

We want to be promoting software from a developer's local environment,
into a test environment -- there might be several of these -- and then
onto a live environment. Ultimately, what we're looking at is a
repeatable live environment that is actually stored as a server recipe
and a reference to a code repository.

We want to be as far as we possibly can be from the plaintive developer
protest that "it works on my machine."

Showing working software to users is great. What's better is having that
software in a test environment where users can mess around with the
software unsupervised and break it in ways that you'd never thought
possible.\
And then, of course, better than that is a live environment that's using
real data and giving users real value.

What's even better than that is being able to reliably duplicate that
environment. So that nothing, not power cuts, not cyber-attacks and not
the "success disaster" of having millions of users trying to use the
service all at once, will stop the software from being deployable.

# Reputational damage and legal requirements

What's this? We thought we'd managed to get the software out of the
prison camp. But no. Here are some more objections to stop us in our
tracks. Just one final thing. Actually, it's two final things. Two
common objections to the idea of promoting software as far as we
possibly can.

"Reputational damage." Somebody is always going to try to stop you
promoting software by claiming reputational damage. But remember what we
talked about with promotion. Remember that's different from simple
publication. There are different levels of promotion.

By recommending releasing working software, I'm not saying "show this
thing to everybody all at once." Certainly not as a first move. Rather,
I'm saying promote your software. Make it available to a wider group.

Do you have a group of friendly users that you could show this to? Is
there a group of people you trust? How do you frame this? Can you say
this is an *"early alpha,"* we're just getting feedback for this? How
can user researchers use this working software in their research?

Then there's the second thing: legal requirements. I don't want you to
tell me that something is a legal requirement as a way of stopping
software getting promoted and then actually escaping, unless you're
surrounded by a bench (is that the collective noun?) of judges.

I absolutely don't want you to tell me that something is illegal if you
haven't talked to anybody who knows about the law -- a lawyer -- and
you're just saying it to stop the software from being released.

# Putting it all together

Why do we have to reach for so many different ways of seeing? Prison
breaks and men jumping out from behind palm trees? I think there was
also some rock climbing in there. At one point we were hanging by our
"nuts." Is the idea of getting working software into the hands of users
as early as we can, *so* controversial and radical? Is it so hard to
grasp?

There's a reason why we need to keep talking about the importance of
early working software. And there's a reason why we need to keep
reaching for new "ways of seeing" to try to explain it. It's the same
reason why working software is so prominent in the Agile manifesto.

So many people don't want us to do it. Maybe this is out of ignorance.\
They buy into the "defined process control" model of software
development, despite the abundant evidence that this kind of "bridge
engineering" approach just doesn't work for software.

Maybe it's out of fear. Sponsors of the project, stakeholders in the
project and members of the team all have some reason to worry about
releasing the software early. Instinctively, they worry that if the
software isn't perfect the first time that users see it, it will make
them look bad.

But these instincts are exactly what we need to resist if we're going to
turn impossible projects into possible ones -- and deliver them.

We need to gradually develop our software in multiple dimensions. This
is real velocity. We need to be advancing in environment, audience,
reality of data and functionality all at once. I know we've been talking
in terms of a metaphor of escape and tripwires and setting off alarms,
and rock climbing, and al fresco martini drinking. This all sounds wild
and reckless. But it actually highlights an important contradiction.

> The thing that feels safe -- letting the software stay inside the
> organization and not trying to help it escape -- that is the thing
> that's most dangerous to the project.

And the logical consequence of this is:

> The thing that feels risky -- pushing the software as near as you can
> toward freedom -- is the safest thing to do.

There's no doubt that this is a struggle. But if the thing that you're
doing is in any way useful or interesting to the people who will use it,
then something magical will happen. At some point you will start to get
pull. Obviously, you'd like this to be sooner rather than later. But
somewhere along the line, you will start to get demand for the software
from outside the prison camp. And then you will start to get demand for
more functionality from the software. Not from the people who sold the
idea, or the people who funded it -- that's push -- but from the people
who are likely to use it -- magical pull.

# Conclusion

Some projects can't just put their software out on the doorstep and let
anybody pick it up and use it. There are all sorts of security concerns.
There are all sorts of regulatory concerns. But how far can you push
things? So that you can be surprised? So that you can connect with pull?
So that you can pivot from pushing things out into the world to having
them pulled into the world by the genuine needs of your users.

Over the last three chapters I've explained that projects exist in the
difficult space between the idea value stream and the still-to-be-built
product value stream (Chapter STREAM). I've explained the value of
seeing this awkward space as a swamp that needs to be explored (Chapter
STREAM). A complex space with many geographical features and patterns of
interest and ownership that can be explored using user research and
other techniques. Now, in this chapter, I've talked about another way of
finding out about this complex space. We can understand the space by
helping working software to escape into it. We want to help the software
to run free, to run into the arms of its eager users. What stops the
software from running free? When we find that out, we have a list of
things that we need to fix or bypass so that the software can be
successful.

Of course, all this exploration of the swamp with user research and
working software assumes that you, as the project manager, and your team
will do something useful with what you find.

In the next two chapters, (Chapter SHIP) (Chapter CAR), I'll discuss
what happens if you can do this. And then I'll discuss what happens if
you don't.
Chapter SHIP -- Never ignore the pirate ship

> "They come in force, with stealthy stride, our obvious course is now
> -- to hide."
>
> The Sergeant, The Pirates of Penzance -- W.S. Gilbert(Gilbert).

A project needs the project team and its stakeholders to recognize and
tackle its problems. If the team acknowledges the problems, it can fix
the ones that can be fixed and escalate or work around the ones that
can't. But it's a bit more complicated than that. To point out the
problems, the team needs to feel safe. To listen and act on what they
hear, their bosses need to feel safe. But every discussion about bad
news is a potential threat to everyone's status.

Let's say you're working on a project. What happens if you notice a
problem?

Problems should be reported. Problems should be fixed. If the problem
can't be fixed inside the team, it should be reported to the product
owner, to senior stakeholders. It should be reported upward and outward
in the hope of finding someone in the organization who can fix the
problem.

That's the central idea of this chapter. If you or your team discover
problems, you should try to fix them.

Where do a project's problems come from? How do we find them? An
iterative and incremental, Agile approach to software development
provides lots of opportunities to identify problems. If the team is
using a Scrum approach, every day there's a "stand-up" meeting. The team
members talk about what they did the day before and what they're going
to do today. They also talk about any problems they have that are
blocking their progress.

At the end of every sprint in Scrum, there is a retrospective. This is
another problem-spotting opportunity. This is a bigger chunk of time for
the team to reflect on how things are going. It's a chance to call out
anything that might cloud the horizon.

Sometimes problems also show themselves in "show and tell" meetings.
This is the meeting where the team demonstrate working software to
stakeholders.

So that's it. When you or your team find problems, they should call them
out and you and your team should try to fix them. Simple -- right?
Actually, no. For a bunch of reasons that we'll explore in the rest of
this chapter, reporting problems and then getting them fixed is far from
simple.

# Blocking and agreed activity

One of the great things about living in London is that you can sign up
for all sorts of classes. Over the past ten years, I've signed up for
comedy improvisation classes many times.

A big idea in improvisation is to avoid what's called "blocking." For
example, let's say an improvised scene starts with someone knocking on a
door. The scene might go something like this.

> Jen: \[Miming opening a door\]
>
> Ben: Hello! I brought you a cabbage.

In the language of improv, the cabbage is an "offer." It's something
that Ben has come up with for the improvisers to play with. And so, the
right way for Jen to respond is to "accept the offer." In improv this is
often described as "Yes and"-ing the offer.

> Jen: Brilliant! I always wanted to make kimchi.

Or

> Jen: It's "bring your own bottle," Ben, "not bring your own brassica."

Even though one of these responses is "positive" and one is "negative,"
both responses are good, because Jen "accepts" -- says "yes" to the
cabbage and builds on it. What's regarded as a bad response in improv is
a response like this:

> Jen: That's not a cabbage, it's a balloon.

Or

> Jen: Fine, whatever.

In improv terms this is called blocking. Blocking is the opposite of
accepting an idea and running with it. Jen isn't dealing with what Ben's
saying. She's denying it or saying that something completely different
is happening.

Blocking is an important concept in improvisation. Because blocking
kills the story. It's boring for an audience to watch.

And it can take subtle forms. Imagine that there is a group of
performers on stage for an improvised scene. To start the scene, the
actors ask for a suggestion from the audience. Someone in the crowd
calls out, "the deck of a tall ship."

Here\'s what might happen.

*Kevin: \[Putting mimed fake telescope to eye\] Look over there! On the
horizon? There's a ship, and a flag! It's a skull and crossbones*.

Now the other performers have a choice to make. One obvious choice here
is to "yes and," to accept, the pirate ship.

> Janice: Oh my God, the pirates are coming. Haul up the sails, let's
> try to outrun them.
>
> Leena: The cannons! Load the cannons!
>
> Mei: Quick! Hide in the lifeboats!

All these are good selections. Of course, the audience hope that hiding
in the lifeboats won't work but will result in lots of comedy. They
certainly hope that the ship can't outrun the pirates.

Janice, Leena and Mei are "accepting the offer," in the language of
improvisation. But sometimes in this kind of situation, someone makes a
different kind of suggestion. Something like this:

> Nina: Hey! Let's wash the decks!
>
> Oliver, Pam, Quentin, Rana and Shug: Yes! Let's!

Maybe there's a kind of comedy in completely ignoring the obvious
threat. But this is just another, more subtle, form of blocking. And for
the audience, it's boring. Once somebody mentions the pirate ship, the
audience wants to see it. They want to see a chase. They want to see
this crew load a cannon, point it in the right direction and fire it.
But not, of course, before someone has dropped a cannonball on their
toes! They want to see the pirate king (or queen!) and see what they'll
do to the crew members who are hiding in the lifeboats. They want to see
someone walk the plank.

What they don't want to see are multiple improvisers on the stage miming
mopping the floor.

Improvisers who don't have a lot of experience are tempted to do
something like scrub the decks.

Why? Because they want to do something that feels safe. Doing something
that moves the story forward into uncertain territory feels dangerous.
That was the realization of the theatrical innovator Keith Johnstone. He
developed his own style of improvised theater, starting in the 1950s. He
also wrote several books: "Impro" and "Impro for Storytellers"
(Johnstone 1979, Johnstone 1998).

In his books, Johnstone talks about people who are new to improvisation.
He describes them wanting to do safe, repetitive activities. Washing the
decks is an example of this. He refers to this as an "agreed activity."

Agreed activity is a repetitive, often group action that can plausibly
fit within the scene but doesn't move the scene forward. And it doesn't
result in anyone or anything about the story being changed.

He explains that putting people on a stage in front of an audience makes
them scared. And scared people try to make themselves safe. They try to
make themselves safe even if what they're doing is making the group less
safe. Even if it undermines their collective aims. In the case of
improvised theater, that means, even if what they do bores the audience.
An audience who are there -- need it be said -- to be entertained.

When they hear the suggestion -- the "offer" -- of a pirate ship, good
improvisers accept that there's a pirate ship. They make the story
interesting and move it forward by dealing with what that means.

What's fascinating is that novice improvisers seem to "know" that
mopping the decks -- the agreed activity -- is the "right" thing to do.
There isn't a long discussion. It happens in just a few seconds. What
this shows is how good we all are at shying away from things that we
perceive as dangerous.

Johnstone found one important way to help the improvisers avoid this
"agreed activity." He suggests a form of show that has a director who is
watching from the side of the stage. In this format, the director can
speak out and direct the improvisers during the show. The director spots
which suggestions, which "offers," like the pirate ship, will move the
story forward.

Let's say a director is watching a scene where there's been a suggestion
of washing the decks. She might allow the crew a few seconds of deck
washing without advancing the action. But then she will shout "The decks
are clean! The pirate ship is here! Deal with the pirate ship!"

Agreed activity is an understandable response to the need for safety in
improvised theater. But, of course, this behavior isn't restricted to
this kind of uncertain, creative endeavor. In lots of other kinds of
work, once we grasp the concept, we start to see agreed activity. For
example, in software development, we also need to avoid agreed activity.
We want to find a way out of fixed, safe patterns that aren't advancing
the "action." And one effective way of achieving this is through outside
input and encouragement.

But here might be a big difference between improvisation and software
development. In improv, the "audience" wants the improvisers to discover
trouble and deal with it. But in software development -- where is the
audience? What does it want? Who is it? Is it the senior stakeholders?
Has the team ever seen them? Is it the users? Has the team ever seen or
heard from them? Often the response of the "audience" of senior
stakeholders to reports of bad news is the equivalent of booing.
Sometimes there is also hissing and throwing things. Sometimes there are
explicit shouts of "scrub the decks." Sometimes, in software development
projects, it's the director who shouts, "Don't deal with the pirate
ship!"

# The pirate ship paradox

In improvised theater and in software development we need to move the
story forward. We need to call out the pirate ships on the horizon and
deal with their impending approach. But highlighting the problems that a
project throws up can be threatening to the senior people involved in
that project. What if the problem that comes to light is something that
they haven't thought of, or they don't know how to fix? Even worse, what
if the problem is something they do know about. I worked for months on a
project that the client, a bank, knew would have to be regulated by
banking authorities. But they kept it quiet. Why? Because they knew that
the admin involved in dealing with the banking authorities would double
the price of the project. In fact, their last project had been killed by
exactly that kind of bureaucracy. Their strategy the second time round
was to try to ignore the pirate ship. They were not at all pleased when
we figured out that the project would need to be registered and
regulated.

Whether the news is expected or unexpected, in my experience it's very
rare for the response to bad news from senior stakeholders to be
positive and helpful.

And that's why people don\'t raise problems and instead just wash the
decks. This looks like a paradox, doesn't it? To succeed, projects need
to tackle their problems. The bosses are the ones who you might think
would most want the project to succeed. The bosses had the original idea
or work for the people who did. But it's often the bosses who play a key
role in discouraging the reporting of the pirate ships. They discourage
the tackling of the problems that, if they aren't dealt with, will sink
a project. Why on earth would they do that? Well, remember that deck
scrubbing? Scared people try to make themselves safe.

And managers and senior stakeholders are people. Often the problems that
the team uncover threaten their safety. And so, like the improvisers on
the stage, senior people will instinctively do things to try to make
themselves safe. And they'll do these things even when that undermines
the projects that they claim passionately to want and need.

Let's put ourselves in the shoes of someone who has managed to persuade
the board to back her project. As part of the process of arranging the
funding, she has probably overstated the benefits and understated the
risks and costs. Anybody who's ever completed a grant proposal or a
project bid will know something about this. Often these formal processes
encourage exactly this kind of under and overstating.

Maybe the process was informal. Maybe the project was the result of a
few brief discussions with someone senior. Maybe it was a dreaded "golf
course conversation." In that case, it might be that the gap between the
vague, "shiny" idea and the reality of what can actually be done is
huge. Maybe she's decided to "move fast and break things." And one of
the things she's decided to break (as with the guy trying to avoid
banking regulation) is the law!

Is there anything that we can do to resolve this paradox? Completely
resolve it? Probably not, but there are some aspects of it that, if we
understand them better, can allow us to manage it more effectively. In
the rest of this chapter, we'll examine two different ways of looking at
this paradox. We'll see how the ideas of psychological safety and status
can help us understand what's going on. We'll also look at some ways of
managing it, even if we can't completely solve it.

# Psychological safety

In recent years, the concept of "psychological safety" as a way of
understanding the effectiveness of teams has been developed and
popularized by Amy Edmondson (Edmondson). It states in a more formal way
something that Johnstone discovered in his experiments with improvisers.
Safety isn't just about physical safety. We also need psychological
safety, and when we don't have it we will change our position and
behavior until we do, for example, by finding an agreed activity.

Psychological safety proposes that there are four basic levels that sit
one on top of another.

*Level 1 is being allowed in the group -- inclusion safety.*

*Level 2 is being allowed to learn in the group -- learning safety.*

*Level 3 is being allowed to contribute to the group -- contribution
safety.*

*Level 4 is being allowed to challenge what the group is doing --
challenger safety.*

Timothy R. Clark outlines these in his book "The 4 Stages of
Psychological Safety" (Clark). It's important to understand that, in
this view, each stage of psychological safety is built on the stage
below. It's unlikely that anyone will be able to learn in the group if
they aren't included. If we're improvising or if we're in a development
team, it seems that there are a few stages of psychological safety that
we need to achieve. That's before we can comfortably shout "Hey, look, a
pirate ship!" We need to be nearly at the top of this staircase, all the
way up at level 3 or 4.

For Clark, fostering psychological safety in creative teams is about
trying to do two things at the same time. In a problem-solving team, we
need disagreements, we need different ideas. As Clark puts it, we need
to increase intellectual friction. But at the same time, we don\'t want
to spend all our time falling out, storming off and getting upset with
each other. We need to decrease social friction.

This is extremely familiar to me. When I'm working with a team, I often
find myself having to point out that there are disagreements --
"intellectual friction." And I have to reassure the team that this is OK
and doesn't have to result in anyone falling out -- "social friction."
Easier, much easier, said than done.

This tension connects with another idea that Clark discusses, the idea
that psychological safety is about a relationship between "cover" and
"candor." The more reassurance that the leader of a team, and other
senior leaders, can provide, the more they can show that being honest
won't have any negative consequences, the more honest team members will
be. The more cover the leader provides, the more candor she will receive
from her team.

But there are two difficulties I have with the idea of fostering
psychological safety as a full solution to the paradox of "agreed
activity" vs "dealing with the pirate ship." Firstly, I'm skeptical of
the idea that creative problem-solving situations are ever going to be
truly safe. That's because the level of safety in the team, for the
individual, is dynamic, it's either being increased or reduced on an
ongoing basis.

And there is a chicken-and-egg aspect to this. If nobody ever took a
risk without feeling totally safe, nobody would ever find themselves
supported in taking that risk. So, they wouldn't then, in the future,
feel safer, when they encounter the need to take another risk. Here we
can see that psychological safety is dynamic and fragile. It needs to be
continuously shored up and supported.

Secondly, there's someone, or maybe several people, missing from our
story. There is someone else who needs to feel safe: the boss or bosses.
The sponsor of this project and the senior stakeholders who backed her
have their own psychological safety to consider. Yes, increasing
psychological safety for the team makes it more likely that the team
will contribute and challenge. And, as project managers, this is in our
power. We can work together to help the team feel safer, to help them
feel more confident about pointing out problems. And we can work to make
sure that when they do this they are supported, so that the level of
safety is increased.

But what about the boss? Is she part of the team? Or is she part of an
entirely different organizational setup? What is her role in that team?
Is she trying to get her feet under the table and just get "inclusion
safety?" Is she trying to learn the ropes? Or is she really at the point
where she can say to other people in her peer group:

> "My team have identified this problem. It's something all your teams
> must also be experiencing. I\'m surprised no one has mentioned it
> before."

Or

> "I think we made a mistake; we didn't expect this problem and we're
> going to need to spend extra effort to fix it. That's going to mean a
> substantial delay."

For your boss, these might be uncomfortable things to say. What if your
boss doesn't have challenger safety in the group that she's in? If she
isn't at the point where she can say things she needs to say, in
whatever forum she operates in, then we have a problem. We all have a
problem.

# Status transactions and the SNAFU principle

The boss is also in a different team, and in that team the boss isn't
the boss. This helps explain something that I'd known for a long time.
But it was something I hadn't fully understood. Then I read about Robert
Anton Wilson's "SNAFU principle." (Wilson)

> "Adequate communication flows freely between equals. Communication
> between non-equals is warped and distorted by ... Domination and
> Submission rituals perpetuating communication jam."
>
> Robert Anton Wilson, Prometheus Rising.

It's actually worse than Wilson imagines. Communication doesn't even
flow freely between equals. Certainly in senior ranks of an
organization, but also at any level in an organization, where people
might be jostling for position, information isn't flowing freely among
people at the same level.

What does this mean? It means yes, it's right to avoid agreed activity.
Yes, it's right for members of a team to point out problems. It's right
to stop mopping the deck and tackle the pirate ship. But it also means
that what happens as a result is rarely going to be straightforward.
It's going to be vulnerable to being warped and distorted by whatever
power structures the team, the boss and her bosses are involved in.

So, what's to be done? Our argument so far has been that a crucial way
of making seemingly impossible projects possible is by spotting their
problems. We can improve a project's chances by sighting the pirate
ships on the horizon and then either tackling them or making them clear
to the bosses. To do this, we need the team to avoid the temptation of
agreed activity. And to do that, we need to foster psychological safety
in the team. But given our realization of the SNAFU principle, what's
the point? Why bother if all that's going to happen as a result is that
we're going to hand this off to a boss who is too scared to do anything?
What can we do about the "distortion" that Wilson talks about? This is
distortion that results in unhelpful responses. It causes responses that
vary from indifference to actual active aggression.

I really wish there were one simple clear answer to this. I don't think
there is. Part of the answer is to take a different view of this
relationship between you, your team, and the boss. Think carefully about
how communication works between you, your team, the boss and their
senior stakeholders. Especially, focus on how the relationship works
when delivering bad news. A view that helps is to see this "bad news"
conversation is as a status transaction. And Keith Johnstone's writings
on improvisation give us further inspiration.

# Natural-sounding conversations are status transactions

Johnstone had been trying to get the students that he was working with
to have natural-sounding conversations on stage. He'd tried all kinds of
things and hadn\'t managed to get anything to work. Finally, after
another day of struggle, he went home and turned on the TV. There was a
program by the biologist and writer Desmond Morris (Morris,1956-1968).
Morris was talking about a community of chimpanzees. He explained that
pretty much everything that chimps do can be explained as a status
transaction. Who they sit with, who they eat with and of course who they
mate with has a strong status-dimension. When one chimp grooms another,
the chimp that is doing the grooming is affirming that they are lower in
status than the chimp that gets groomed.

The point that Morris made in the TV program was that most of these
status exchanges aren't huge. There are very few fights involved. There
isn't much chest-beating. The status exchanges are tiny, fractional
indications of who is higher and lower-status. Who sits highest on the
tree? Who has their fur picked for fleas first when two near-equals
agree to pick through each other's fur? How these questions are answered
shows the precise status of all the chimps in the group.

Next day, Johnstone went back to his drama studio and asked his students
to improvise scenes. He had only one instruction. Whatever one person
said, the other person should try to lift themselves, or lower
themselves, just slightly, in status with their reply.

Johnstone was delighted with the results. Suddenly, his students were
having conversations that sounded like the way people normally talked.

> "Are you going anywhere nice on your holidays?"
>
> "Tahiti."
>
> "Ooh, that sounds amazing, I'd love to go to Tahiti."
>
> "Well, my husband is working out in the South Pacific, so it's easiest
> if I go out and holiday near where he is. Are you going anywhere
> nice?"
>
> "Ibiza."
>
> "Oh! I love Ibiza. We used to go there all the time before we had
> kids."
>
> "It's my first time."
>
> "Oh, I'm sure you'll love it."

OK -- now read this again. Who is high-status? And what does the
high-status person do to (slightly) lower their status?

Does saying "Tahiti" sound like a definite attempt to elevate status?

Does explaining that this is because that's where your husband is
working make it just slightly less exotic and lower status? The more you
look, the more you can see is going on.

Does this sound like a realistic conversation?

Think of a conversation that you've had recently with a friend or a
colleague. Who was lower-status (just fractionally)? Who was
higher-status?

Now let's come back to the business of bad news and taking problems to
the boss. The difficulty that project managers are always wrestling with
is that bad news is humbling and threatening. A conversation where bad
news is given and received is a status transaction. Keith Johnstone's
and Robert Anton Wilson's insight is that in real life nearly everything
is a status transaction. This makes life difficult for development team
members, project managers and their bosses. But it explains a lot of the
apparently negative and unhelpful behavior that we see from bosses when
they receive bad news. It explains why so many teams recognize the bad
news and then instinctively tell nobody about it. They just agree to mop
the decks.

It also explains why kings shoot messengers, plus why senior
stakeholders react with anger to bad news. They shoot the messenger
because when the messenger spills the bad news, they are radically
lowering the king's status. This is an intolerable situation for the
king. But in that moment, he can easily solve his status problem albeit
temporarily. Sure, the battle is lost. But he can partially recover his
status and show that he's still the king, by having the messenger shot.

Delivering bad news is a status transaction. If we fully understand the
implications of this, it suggests how we might improve. It suggests how
we might better escalate problems to senior stakeholders.

# (Quietly) pointing out the pirate ship

I worked on a project that aimed to replace a complicated website. The
website helped users meet the government regulations for the biotech
industry. The initial deadline for the project had been so laughable --
just eight months after we'd started -- that nobody had really taken it
seriously. But then, without much discussion -- or none that I was party
to as project manager -- a new deadline appeared. It was the summer of
the following year. Eighteen months after we'd started the project. On
the face of it, it was a "reasonable" deadline. But from experience, I
knew that delivering what we needed to deliver by that deadline would be
extremely challenging.

It was a classic "burning platform" deadline -- the contract with the
existing supplier would time out and this supplier was making noises
about charging exorbitant fees to keep the system running beyond that
point. I knew that it would be hard to renegotiate the deadline.
Instead, we needed to renegotiate the scope: limit what we delivered by
the deadline to the absolute minimum possible. But I also knew from
bitter experience that negotiating a reduction in scope would be a very
difficult business. Simply telling the senior stakeholders that they
couldn't have everything that had been "promised" would be difficult.

The team, including the product owner and the tech lead, didn't seem to
think that we needed to change our priorities or look for things that we
could postpone until after the deadline. Looking back now, I realize
this was probably "agreed activity." We were carrying on as we had been
carrying on, even though the parameters of the project had just
radically changed. Later, I found out that both the product owner and
the tech lead did think that we needed to do something but hadn't spoken
up.

But my reaction was "no way!" We're going to have to do something
drastic to make this work. This was a pirate ship!

I called a meeting (this was in a pre-Covid, face-to-face world). I got
the team, including the product owner, to write on index cards all the
"epics" -- the big chunks of work that needed to be done on the project.
We stuck them on the wall.

As a team, we estimated their complexity -- how big they were relative
to each other. Adding up all these complexity scores gave me a total
score for the project.

We arranged the epics on a "roadmap" which was just a series of columns
on a chart -- with the headings "Done," "Now," "Next," "Later," "Long
grass". Every fortnight we'd have a meeting where we updated the level
of the completion of the stories that were in the "Now" column. This
gave me a progress score. I let this run for six weeks or so.

Now I could put together a chart. A "burn-up" chart. This showed the
amount of work that we needed to do by July (that was the deadline). And
every two weeks, it showed our progress. If you drew a line,
extrapolating from the end of the actual progress line to where that
line crossed the "total amount of work" line, that was our prediction of
when all the work would be done. After six weeks of doing this, the
lines were crossing in late November! Four months too late.

This was the point where I called a meeting with the team and the
product owner.

I'm not saying all hell broke loose, but certainly, bits of hell got
free and ran around for a bit.

There were raised voices. I remember someone saying when I mentioned
