that part of my job as project manager was to be pessimistic, "Well,
congratulations on doing your job so well! What are we going to do now?"

I don't have solid proof, but I think that same person went to my boss
and discussed the possibility of getting me fired. And I think that my
boss told that person that what I was doing was exactly what he'd hired
me to do.

# Tackling the pirate ship

I didn't do most of the communication of the bad news to the senior
stakeholder. The product owner and the user researcher had the best
relationship with the senior stakeholder. They talked him through the
problem. They were the ones who agreed to a reorganization of the epics;
they were the ones who agreed the epics that wouldn't be done until
after we went live. And together with the team they came up with a
brilliant way of de-risking the project. They figured out a way of
slicing off the front end -- the form-filling part of the project --
from the data back end. This meant that, if we really had to, we could
go "live" with just this stand-alone part of the system and then
back-fill the connection to the back-end database.

As a team, as a wider team, we reduced scope and brought down the line
that showed what needed to be delivered for the deadline. As a team we
figured out how to reduce the scope inside the epics so that only the
minimum that we could get away with was done for launch.

And the result was that we delivered on time. I saw a pirate ship, I
pointed it out and as a team, we tackled it.

Note that I did almost none of that tackling. Talking this through with
the senior stakeholder was done by the members of the team who had
developed and maintained the best relationships -- the user researcher
and the product owner. Re-ordering the epics was done mostly by the
product owner. The brilliant idea of splitting the front end from the
back end came from the tech lead and other software developers.

I find my own my own actions in this example worth noting. Because I
didn't shout out "Look, a pirate ship!" or, in this case, "There's no
way we'll deliver for that date!" Well, I didn't shout it out the minute
that I noticed it. I waited six weeks. I didn't engineer the
confrontation with the senior stakeholder; I left that to others who
were better at it and had an existing relationship. I didn't really,
directly tell anyone. I put together a chart that showed the problem.
Why, given what we've talked about above -- the importance of pointing
out pirate ships -- did I do such a peculiar thing? Why did I wait?
Well, what made me wait was ...

... experience.

Five years before, I was the project manager on a large project in a
public organization that had a terrible record of failing to deliver
software projects. So bad that it had made the front page of the tabloid
newspapers. If you're responsible for a project in the UK, the front
page of the tabloids is exactly the place that you never want to be.

As I started on the project, I heard that it was already in quite
serious trouble. When I go into projects that are in trouble (and indeed
when I go into projects that aren't), one of the first things I do is to
run a retrospective with the team. What came out of the retrospective
was a long list of problems. As is very often the case with software
development projects, some variation of this dialogue was going on.

> Client: Hurry up! Hurry up! We need this software fast.
>
> Development team: OK, can you tell us what it needs to do?
>
> Client: No. \[pause\] Hurry up! Hurry up!

I also had a suspicion that there was probably about twice as much work
as could realistically be delivered by the deadline. But I didn't yet
have any proof; I was planning on getting to that in the next few weeks.

In that first week, of course, I didn't have much of a relationship with
the product owner; I'd only just met her. I didn't really have sight of
any of the other stakeholders.

In these pre-home-working days, it wasn't unknown, rather than using
software, to keep track of a project using physical index cards on a
wall. We had planned two weeks of work and that was on index cards on
the wall. So, I had what I thought was a good idea. I wrote the problems
that had been mentioned in the retrospective on sticky notes. And I
stuck them on the wall alongside the tasks that we were trying to
complete.

I didn't last the week. The product owner had me shot (figuratively,
thank goodness). When she saw the sticky notes that I'd put on the wall
and read them, she had them removed. Her explanation was that "someone
senior might see them" and get the wrong idea about the project. And
then she had me removed. This was my first engagement working for a new
company, so I nearly got fired. I only just managed to cling on. But I
think I learned my lesson. Yes, you have to point out the pirate ship.
But you have to find a way of doing it that causes the minimum amount of
threat to status. Especially the status of senior people.

# Agreed activity, psychological safety and status transactions

Finally, we can bring these ideas, these ways of seeing together.
"Agreed activity," "psychological safety" and "delivering bad news is a
status transaction" are helpful ways of seeing the difficult business of
spotting, reporting and dealing with bad news. And bringing them
together, we can come up with some positive, helpful suggestions for
anyone who is trying to make a project more possible than impossible.

## Do it in private.

One of the mistakes I made when I was a messenger who got shot was
delivering the bad news in front of an audience. I literally made it the
writing on the wall. In private, the threat to status is reduced. I
should have talked to the product owner privately. That would have given
them far more "outs," far more ways of dealing with the situation
without losing status or face.

Instead, I put up post its with the "pirate ships" that came out of the
retrospective. And broadcasting that bad news got me kicked out.

## Understand the value landscape

As much as you can, understand the landscape. What team is your boss, or
your boss's boss part of? What's going on? Is there a re-org? Is there a
merger? Did another project fail and that means this project is in the
spotlight? Who cares about this project? Who's annoyed it's happening?
Is there a group of users that really want this project to happen? Is
there a group of users that really *don't* want this project to happen?
As we discussed in (Chapter SWAMP), the project is happening in a
complex space. The more you know about this space, the less likely you
are to put your foot in it when you attempt to mention bad news.

## Side by side rather than face to face

Use maps, charts and graphs for side-by-side discussion. This may feel a
bit sneaky, but it works. Don't try to tell the boss anything; show
them. Show the problems that the development team is reporting. Let's
imagine that in the last three retrospectives the team ranked the same
problem as the most important. Make it obvious that this is the thing
that's slowing or stopping progress.

As I did in the example above, create burn-up charts. Given current
progress, when is the project predicted to finish? Well, would you look
at that? Seems it's going to take twice as long as we expected. Huh! Who
would've thought it?

## Do it as part of an existing relationship

If you have no relationship with your bosses, it will be very hard to
give them bad news. The more that boss already trusts you, or some
member of your team, the more chance there is that they will believe
what you're saying and act on it. Even if what you're telling them
doesn't make them feel psychologically safe and lowers their status. And
the best time to improve the relationship is before you're in the
situation of having to discuss an impending pirate ship.

It may be that you aren't the person on your team with the best
relationships with senior stakeholders. Use whoever you have.

## Bring in the opposite number

If all else fails, try bringing in someone of similar status to the boss
who you are trying to talk to. Sometimes you're getting nowhere.
Sometimes, there is no alternative but to bring someone in who is their
"opposite number." Who can look them in the eye on a level status and
explain that there is a problem.

Again, obviously, this works much better if this kind of contact is part
of an existing relationship. If a senior partner in your organization
has been regularly, if not frequently, having a conversation to keep in
touch, it makes it much easier to have a difficult conversation when
it's needed.

## Connect with commitment and consistency.

There is a very flawed idea that project management is about keeping
promises. There are lots of things wrong with this idea and we'll
explore those in (Chapter COMMIT). There aren't many positive aspects of
this kind of view of project management. But one of them is this: when
senior people seek a commitment from you, you should absolutely use that
opportunity to get a commitment from them. Yes, as project manager,
you're going to do your best to commit to the deadline. But can you get
a commitment from them that when problems arise that can't be addressed
by the team, they will listen to you? Can you get a commitment that when
these issues need an escalation, they will do what is required to
escalate them?

## Be comfortable in tension

Be comfortable in tension -- and in maintaining that tension. A mistake
that I made frequently earlier in my career was to expect the reporting
of problems to result in immediate action. Even more ambitiously, I
expected *visible* immediate action.

There may be all kinds of reasons why this isn't possible. There may be
other blockages. There may be other timing issues that you can't see.
These hidden issues mean that simply reporting a problem doesn't mean
that the process of solving it is immediately set in motion. Still, it's
important to keep these problems visible and at the front of senior
people's minds. The psychologist Daniel Kahneman wrote in his book
"Thinking Fast and Slow" (Kahneman) that, *"What you see is all there
is."*

The implication of this is that if you want to change your bosses'
thinking, you need to change what they see.

# Conclusion

If you want a project to succeed, you need to identify its problems. And
solve them.

But there's a complication. Actually, there are at least two
complications. Firstly, members of your team who discover problems will
instinctively avoid reporting them, out of the fear that their bad news
will be received poorly. Secondly, these fears are justified. Senior
stakeholders might well receive the news badly. They might shoot
somebody.

As a project manager, you need to encourage your team to call out
problems when they see them. It's your job to increase psychological
safety within your team. You need to be providing all the cover that you
can for their candor.

You need to be encouraging intellectual friction, reassuring them that
this is OK, while at the same time soothing and reducing emotional
friction. It's also your job to spot "agreed activity" -- places where
the team seem to be working but not making any progress. If there's a
problem there that nobody's talking about, you need to help bring it to
light.

How do you solve the second problem, the difficulty of delivering bad
news to the bosses? If you want to turn a seemingly impossible project
into a possible one, you will have to tackle its problems. A crucial
part of that process is coming up with ways of broaching these problems
with senior stakeholders. We have to ask for their help in solving
problems that can't be solved within the team.

To do this, you need to craft a way of delivering bad news that does not
threaten their status. And to do that you need to be working on your
relationships with them. This means taking advantage of times to build
those relationships when things aren't going badly. And you need the
best picture that you can get of what is going on in their world and in
the wider organization. You need to know what's going on in the swamp
(Chapter SWAMP).

This brings us to a point that we haven't yet discussed directly,
although we have alluded to it. All these strategies only work if you
and your team have the clearest view possible of the problems that
affect the project. Your telescope can't be a pretend telescope. The
lens needs to be clean, and it needs to be properly focused. Making sure
that you have that view is what we talk about in the next chapter,
(Chapter CAR).
CHAPTER CAR

> "Seeking what is true is not seeking what is desirable."
>
> Albert Camus (Camus)

Project management is like driving a car. That's because project
management is about helping the empirical process. It's about learning
from experience. It's about being transparent. We need to keep the
windscreen clean. It's about inspection. It's about looking out through
the windscreen. And it's about adaptation. It's about changing what you
do because of what you see.

At the same time, project management isn't anything like driving a car.
For a start - no one is driving! Well, at least, no single person. And
trying to make the windscreen clean so the people who *are* driving can
make sensible decisions, could get you thrown out of the car.

# Learning from experience

"Empirical" isn't a common word. "Empirical process" is an even less
common phrase. But "empirical process" refers to something we do every
day. In our ordinary lives, we constantly change what we're doing in
response to what we see and experience through our senses. And that's
exactly what "empirical process" means. "Empirical" means relating to
experience in the real world. It's a down-to-earth, practical word.

For years I've run training courses to introduce people to iterative,
incremental, and Agile ideas. And a basic concept in understanding these
ideas is empirical process.

Because "empirical" is a slightly strange word, I get people to look up
the word and to discuss what it means. Then we do an exercise. This
exercise often involves Lego.

Using a variation on an activity developed by my friend and colleague
Steve Forbes, I give the team a bunch of Lego models. I ask them to
estimate how many of the models they can build in a short, fixed space
of time. These short, fixed amounts of time are called "iterations," in
Agile.

Almost always, at the end of the first iteration, the team realize that
they can't do as many models in the time as they first thought.

So, this forces the teams to do something different, based on their new
experience. Either they reduce their estimates of the number of models
they can build, or they simplify the models. At the end of the exercise,
I come back to this word "empirical." Empirical means based on
experience.

That is the key point that I'm trying to get across in this exercise.
Learning from experience is important. Modifying what you do in light of
what happens is a big part of improving and controlling your
performance.

But this exercise doesn't always communicate what I hoped it would. In
the feedback from one training course, in answer to the question "What
is the most valuable thing you've learned from this course?" I got:

> "The importance of imperial progress."

This is evidence, I suppose, of just how tricky this concept is. But
there is a way of breaking down the empirical process into
easier-to-understand bits. We can break it down into three constituent
parts, sometimes called the three pillars of empirical process. They are
transparency, inspection and adaptation (Schwaber, 2002).

## Driving the car, transparency, inspection and adaptation

Imagine that you're driving a car. Ok, you've got the car started, and
you've figured out how to make it go forward and backward. And how to
make it slow down and stop!

The next thing you need is to be able to see out of the windscreen. If
the windscreen is covered in mud, or being washed with buckets of rain
or frosted up with ice, that's bad.

You can't drive under those conditions. What you're missing is
*transparency.* You *need* transparency***.*** And it isn't just looking
forward that's important. You'd like to see behind you through your
mirrors. So, the wing mirrors need to be there, and the back windscreen,
as well as the front, needs to be clear. When you're driving you need
transparency in multiple directions. All-round transparency would be
great. And if you don't have it, things can go wrong very fast.

But transparency isn't enough. If you're driving, it isn't enough for
the windscreen to be clear. You need to look out of it. You need to look
out of the windscreen and check your mirrors. If instead you're checking
messages on your phone or trying to brush scalding hot coffee out of
your lap, that's no good. Transparency by itself isn't enough. You also
need *inspection.*

We're focusing here on the visual aspects of driving. But we all know
that when you're driving, you're also really checking with your other
senses. That's inspection too. Of course, inspection is, strictly
speaking, a visual thing. But, when we're driving, it's also more
generally paying attention. If you feel an unusual vibration accompanied
by a dull thudding, there's a good chance that you'll slow down. If you
smell petrol, or burning, you'll probably stop. If you hear police
sirens, you'll check in your rearview mirror and see if the police seem
to be interested in you. If they are, you'll probably speed up -- if
you're in the middle of committing a bank robbery. And that dull
thudding is probably someone you have tied up in the boot! But under
more normal circumstances, you'll slow down and stop.

So, I hope we're agreed, inspection isn't just a visual thing. It's
paying attention with more than just one sense.

Talking about slowing down and speeding up brings us to the third pillar
of empirical process: *adaptation.* What we're doing when we slow down,
is adapting. That's also what we're doing when we speed up because we
see the road is clear. It's also what we do when we swerve to avoid
something or put the fog lights on when it's foggy. Adaptation is when
we change what we're doing because of what we sense, what we see, hear
and feel.

Notice how, for these aspects of empirical control to work, they have to
be arranged correctly. They are dependent on each other. We can't drive
successfully without transparency. If the windscreen were covered in
mud, we would not see the things that we needed to avoid. We can't do
empirical control without inspection. If we didn't look up from our
texting, if we didn't stop playing with the radio, we wouldn't see the
thing that we were about to hit. We wouldn't feel the need to swerve or
slow down. And finally, we wouldn't be slowing down or swerving if it
weren't for adaptation. When we're driving, we instinctively change what
we're doing because of what we see, hear, smell and feel. These things,
when they're put together, are empirical process.

More full disclosure: I've crashed two cars. Actually, there was also a
third incident in a car park, but nobody admitted liability. So, I'm not
claiming that I'm an expert on driving. Neither, I'm guessing, would the
person who was driving the car I reversed into in the car park. But I am
an expert on what these principles of empirical process mean for
software development teams.

## Empirical process for software development

OK, we've talked about cars. But what does any of this actually mean for
managing software development projects? Well, let's start with
transparency. What does transparency mean for software development
teams? I'm afraid it means meetings. There are at least three meetings
that add transparency into the software development process, and of
course, by doing that, they facilitate inspection and adaptation.

## The stand-up

As we mentioned in the previous chapter, the stand-up is a short meeting
that all the members of the development team have every working day.
Each member of the team says what they did the previous working day and
what they're going to do today and highlights anything that's blocking
them.

The stand-up has all three pillars of the empirical process. It has
*transparency.* Members of the team are telling each other what they're
doing and pointing out any problems that they're having.

It has *inspection.* Well, it has inspection provided the other team
members are looking up from whatever they are doing and paying
attention. And also, of course, provided that the updates are honest.
The team members can only inspect what they see (and hear). Perhaps
someone has been struggling for three days on something that was
supposed to be a "two-minute" job. Well then, it would be great for the
team to know about that. But maybe the person who's struggling won't
want to say.

And the stand-up is also a crucial opportunity for *adaptation.* When
they hear about their teammates' progress, or lack of progress, it's
natural for the team members to change their behavior in response to
what they hear. The team adapts by focusing on problems and fixing them.
Or it adapts by understanding that the problem can't be fixed right now
(or ever) and routing round it.

The stand-up is a fantastic opportunity for team members to let each
other know what's going on: transparency. But only if it's combined with
inspection. As mentioned above, this isn't just limited to seeing or
hearing. It's a combination of, at a minimum, both those senses. We can
see from facial expressions and body language and we can hear from tone
of voice what's going on with each member of the team.

And it's also an opportunity for adaptation. If something's wrong, if
something's stuck, the stand-up is an opportunity to find that out and
then decide as a team to do something to solve the problem.

Of course, teams can get through hundreds and hundreds of stand-ups
without getting any of these benefits.

And this is bad for the project. Very bad. If people aren't transparent,
and don't inspect, nothing works properly. To come back to transparency
and inspection as part of what's needed to drive the car, if team
members aren't transparent and they don't inspect what's going on around
them, the project can easily end up upside down in a ditch.

Members of the team need to pay attention in the stand-up. This
invaluable communication needs to happen. Changes of direction as a
result of new information, getting help from other team members to solve
a problem -- these also need to happen.

As a project manager, you need to try to spot when this good stuff isn't
taking place. There will be one person who always wants to use the
stand-up to talk about anything but what they did the day before and
what they're going to do today. This person might be someone who does
nothing. Or this person could be the most valuable member of the team.
It's not really an indication of either. But they want to talk about
their cat's illnesses and feel free to do so. If you don't have a quiet
word with them and help them to focus on the work in hand, your world
will be filled with half-dead mice, cat vomit and worse.

And there will be someone else who gives the same update every day.
Maybe because they're seriously struggling with what they're trying to
do. And there will be someone else whose update is inaudible, or who
refuses to turn their camera on, or both. It's your job to cajole these
people into being a bit more forthcoming about what they're doing, maybe
admitting that they have a problem, or into turning on their camera and
speaking up.

And there will be people who don't actually belong in your stand-up but
who turn up anyway. Why? There could be lots of reasons. Because they've
heard your project is cool. Because they've heard your project is in
trouble. Because they don't have much to do (more common than you'd
think). Because they've heard that your project is about to become
successful and so now they want to be associated with it. Or because
they're the chairman of the board of this multinational company and they
can do what the hell they like (this really happened to me).

Whatever the reason, they're there. But if they shouldn't be there --
and especially if their presence is going to inhibit the members of the
team from being transparent about what's going on with the project --
it's your job as project manager to make sure that they aren't there.
Fortunately, the chairman of the board only came to my stand-up once. If
he'd found it so fascinating that he decided to come every day, I don't
know what I'd have done.

But one thing was for sure, nobody was being transparent about anything
that had gone wrong when he was in the room. There was zero
transparency. Nobody could "see out of the windscreen" in that meeting,
because there was the powerful, blinding light from the monster truck of
the big boss shining in.

## Retrospective

Another meeting that implements the three pillars of empirical process
is the retrospective. In retrospectives, the team looks in the rearview
mirror. They look at the road behind them and talk about what progress
they have made down it. They have time to reflect on the things that
blocked their path. They get to talk about how they could have dealt
with the obstructions more easily. What things have they got the hang of
now? What knowledge have they gained that means they might be able to
tackle obstacles faster in the future? They also get to talk about the
things that they still have no idea how to handle at all. These are the
obstacles that are still in the way. These are the lighting, weather and
road conditions that are still slowing them down.

They also tend to dance around disagreements that are crippling the
team. Or they say nothing because they don't feel safe speaking up. Or
they whisper an idea that will actually save the project, but they do it
so quietly that no one hears. Or someone makes a suggestion that will
make the project far more valuable than anybody imagined, and it gets
missed, because the person who's talking isn't the person who normally
dominates the conversation.

Again, as a project manager, it's your job to encourage transparency.
And part of that needs to be paying attention to how comfortable members
of the team are speaking out in retrospectives. One way of doing this is
as a "safety check." Asking the team on a scale of 1-5: 1 being not safe
at all and 5 being totally confident about speaking in that group. The
way that I do this is by asking members of the team to write their
number on bits of paper -- anonymously. Then we take the average. If the
average is below an agreed number, like 3, we stop the retrospective. If
there are individual scores below 3, I suggest that those people might
want to approach me privately and explain why they don't feel safe
talking in the retrospective.

My experience is that putting some focus and attention onto the safety
of speaking up is often all it takes for members of the team to feel
comfortable enough to speak up.

The other thing that the project manager needs to do is point out
conflict. There may be disagreements in the team about how to adapt,
about how to change or about how to react to new information that's
emerged as a result of transparency and inspection. In my experience,
most development team members are conflict-averse. So, if there's
disagreement in the team, the result very often isn't conflict. It's
silence. As a project manager, it's your job to spot these silences --
and that can be very tricky. But one way to look at this is that each of
these silent disagreements is an adaptation that isn't happening. This
is like having two passengers in the car who spot an object that they
think the car is going to crash into. But they say nothing, because they
know they'll disagree about what to do to stop the crash.

My strategy in these situations is to identify the disagreement. At
least if we can identify the disagreement over how to solve a problem,
then we have acknowledged the problem. And we likely have at least two
different suggestions as to how to approach solving it.

## Show and tell

The final Agile meeting, which implements the pillars of the empirical
process, is the show and tell. I'm not sure what the reason is, but this
is a meeting that goes by a lot of different names, all of which mean
roughly the same thing. "Sprint demo" or "showcase" are other names.

The show and tell is a regular demonstration of working software that
shows exactly the real progress that the team is making. This isn't
quite the "putting working software in the hands of users" that I was
advocating in (Chapter ESCAPE). But it is an opportunity to get software
*nearer* to actual users. And get genuine feedback. And of course, that
can really be a problem.

There are some other aspects of showing working software that can cause
difficulties. By regularly demonstrating working software in show and
tells, you reveal the *actual* progress of the project. And very often,
this is much slower than anyone, especially the senior stakeholders,
would like it to be.

# Mr. Two

The first Agile software development project that I worked on was for a
publishing company. When I joined the project, it had been going for
over a year and had cost many millions of pounds.

A few months before I joined, there had been a show and tell meeting.
This was an international company, and it had offices in London and in
New York.

This is the story that I heard about that meeting. One of the project
managers said that he was going to share project progress. This was
progress that had been made through "the backlog." The backlog is the
Agile way of saying the list of all the requirements for the project.
It's basically the project's "to-do list."

The project manager had a presentation with some slides. And there was a
bit of messing about setting up the presentation -- and then making sure
that it was shared with the office in New York. Finally, everything was
set up and the project manager could move to the slide that showed the
progress of the project. He clicked on to the slide. In the middle of
the slide was a giant "2".

I don't know how dramatic he was at this point. I don't know if he asked
anybody in the room or on the phone if they could guess what the two
meant? Two weeks to finish? Two months? Two years? In fact, the two was
percent. Next to the giant two was a tiny % sign. Progress on the
project, after six months and many millions of pounds, was two percent
of all the work that was on the to-do list.

This is brutal transparency. And it probably came out of desperation.
Having worked on that project, I can guess that someone had told the
bosses in New York that the project would be finished by a certain date.
Nobody had mentioned to the bosses the problems that were being found.
Problems that were slowing progress to a crawl. Also, my guess was that
nobody had said "no" to the bosses. Ever. And that was why the list of
requirements was tens of times as long as could ever be delivered.

The main result of that number "2" was the project manager was never
allowed to speak at a show and tell again. The bosses in New York hired
a "senior project manager" to communicate progress across the Atlantic.
After that, guess what? The news was always good!

We discuss people like this, who are put in their role specifically to
hide bad news and only communicate a rosy view of projects as "baby
seals," in (Chapter NOT BET). But, at that moment, in that show and tell
meeting, nobody was around to obscure the bad news about the project.

This is what one friend of mine said about this meeting:

> "I remember an explosion of anger over the phone where the entire team
> (50+ people) were dressed down and told to get on with it or heads
> will roll."

But what does it tell us? What does it tell us about using driving the
car as a way of seeing software development project management? What
does it tell us about empirical process, transparency, inspection and
adaptation? And in the end, what does that tell us about delivering the
impossible?

# Maybe software development isn't like driving a car

The idea of this book is that looking at things from different points of
view can make us smart. And the idea of this chapter is that looking at
controlling a project as if it's like driving a car can make us smart.
How? By encouraging us to foster the three pillars of the empirical
process: transparency, inspection and adaptation.

But are the wheels already coming off this way of seeing? Is it already
breaking down? Yes, bits of it are. Transparency, inspection and
adaptation are very important. And thinking of them in terms of driving
a car is useful.

But the story of Mr. Two shows us that there are ways in which a project
isn't like driving a car, or at least there are aspects of a project
where driving a car isn't the way of seeing that makes us the smartest.

Of course, people go on impossible, or very difficult, car journeys. But
most car journeys aren't like that. Most car journeys get completed and
are largely uneventful. And I say that even as someone who has driven in
traffic in central Athens.

Mr. Two was trying to be transparent. Which is as important for driving
a project as it is for driving a car. His idea, although he might not
have made this explicit, was that by doing that, by telling the truth,
by encouraging the bosses to inspect what was *actually going on,* they
would adapt as a result.

Of course, Mr. Two doesn't just spring from nowhere. He has a past. By
the time he turns up in our story, he has become strident in his
attempts to get through to senior people. He's failed to communicate
what he's seen from inspection. He knows that there needs to be
adaptation, that something needs to change. And the result is that he
becomes a figure of myth. The "hero" in a story that other project
managers tell each other as a warning.

And what does this story tell us exactly? It tells us three things:

1.  Most car journeys succeed -- many projects don't (I'll back that up
    with figures).

2.  No one person is driving -- adaptation happens -- or doesn't happen
    -- in a distributed way across the hierarchy. This makes adaptation
    far more complicated.

3.  People often get into trouble for either establishing, or increasing
    transparency, or for reporting what they see as a result of
    inspection.

Let's take these three differences in turn. By doing this, we can see
how trying to see a project as if it's like driving a car can be
helpful, even when what it shows us is that managing a project isn't
really like driving a car at all.

## Difference from driving #1 -- Most car journeys succeed 

Here's one of the main reasons that a substantial number of projects
don't succeed. I'm going to put on my magic fortune teller's hat and my
magician's cloak with stars stitched onto it by my mum. And I'm going to
look into your project. Mm! Ah! Argh! Actually, I don't need a funny hat
and a cloak, and I don't need the strange noises. If you have a project,
and this project has yet to deliver its first live release and that
release has a deadline, I already know one thing about it.

*Your project is supposed to deliver N times more work than can possibly
be delivered by the deadline.*

Just like the project above that Mr. Two was struggling with. What's the
value of "N"? On a "good" project, N is 2 or 3. This figure is taken
from my own experience. I don't think I've ever seen N to be lower than
2.

And I've seen lots of projects where N is 7 or 8. On the project that I
just told you about, where the project manager brought up the "2" on the
screen, N was somewhere between 25 and 50!

But this is from my own, anecdotal experience. Is there any wider
research that I could use to back this up? Well, the Standish report
(Johnson) has been reporting for decades that most software development
projects aren't a success. The 2020 report found that around 30% of
projects met all their goals. Around 20% were canceled. The rest -- 50%
-- were "challenged." What is the Standish definition of "challenged"?

> *"Was completed and operational but over budget, over the time
> estimate, and/or offers fewer features and functions than originally
> specified."*

Well, that sounds a lot like 50% of projects had an N that was bigger,
maybe much bigger, than 1.

But maybe I'm wrong about your project. Maybe you're reading a book
called "Delivering the Impossible" out of idle curiosity. You wonder
what it would be like to work on one of those projects that seems
impossible. But *your* project? *Your* project is fine! Maybe.

If your project isn't fine, what are you going to do about it? If you
have ten times more work in your backlog than you can possibly deliver,
what do you do?

Telling it like it is, as the project manager in the story tried to,
doesn't seem to work that well. In that case, it resulted in someone
being hired specifically to stop the bosses in New York being told the
truth.

Someone senior might well have promised something that is impossible to
deliver. Delivering anything near that amount of functionality might be
a difficult process.

Getting everyone to admit that only a fraction of what's in the backlog
can make it into a first release can be an even more difficult process.
Although doing just that can be the thing that saves a project. But it
might require careful handling, more careful than that shown by Mr. Two.
Diplomacy, tact, careful handling and an understanding of the unique
politics of a particular organization may well be required, as we'll
discuss in the next chapter (Chapter SHIP).

## Difference #2 -- who's driving this thing?

It was my first project manager stress dream. I haven't had as many of
these as you might expect. But I had this one in the first few weeks of
my first job with "project manager" in the title.

The dream was that I was sitting in the back seat of a car. And so was
my boss! No one was sitting in the driving seat. Actually, the driving
seat was in front of me, so it was possible that there was a driver. But
I couldn't see who it was. I started to go about the physical
contortions needed to climb from the back seat into the front seat, the
front passenger seat.

I haven't thought about this dream much in 20 years. Although at the
time, I realized that it was something about control.

But thinking about it now, and in light of our current use of "driving a
car" as a way of seeing, I find it fascinating.

Me and my boss, we're in the back seat. But the car (the project) is
moving. I can't see who's driving. And the thought occurs to me only
now. Who was driving the car?

This dream highlights something that doesn't work about using the car as
a way of seeing for projects. Something that partly explains Mr. Two's
antics. Did you notice something strange about this way of seeing? Did
you notice a distinct lack of "main character energy" surrounding the
project manager? Yes, it's your job to enable and maintain transparency.
It's your job to encourage inspection. It's your job to encourage
adaptation. But it isn't your job to decide how to adapt.

*As the project manager, you're not driving the car*.

Of course, there are many project managers who think they *are* driving
the car. And there are many project managers who think they *should be*
driving the car. But in projects of any complexity, they are both wrong.
Adaptive decisions about what the project should be doing -- what goes
in the software -- should be being made by a product owner. Adaptive
decisions about how to address technical problems should be being made
by the technically knowledgeable members of the team.

As a project manager, you're trying to create and maintain the
conditions that allow the car to be driven. And also, of course, as a
project manager, you're responsible for telling everybody if, when you
clamber into the passenger seat, you realize that no one is driving the
car! Facilitating the "driving" that needs to happen across multiple
levels is something that you need to do as a project manager. And it
doesn't really match what's going on when someone is driving a real car.

In (Chapter ESCAPE) we talked about a product owner who saved a project
by connecting the software to the people who wanted to use it. To some
extent, she was driving the car. But there were other people who were
trying to drive it in a different direction. There were the people who
were arguing over costs that weren't covered in the contract. And
essentially threatening to crash the project in the process. What saved
that project was that she prioritized working software over arguments
about who was going to pay for servers and about who should pay for work
to make the software accessible.

None of this was going on in Mr. Two's project. Why? Because the product
owner on Mr. Two's project was a mild-mannered lady who couldn't say no
to any demands for functionality, and who couldn't do anything to
connect the software to its actual users. In the language of my stress
dream, she was in the driving seat.

*But she wasn't driving!*

Having a product owner who is empowered and has the resilience to make
decisions about priorities is a major factor in allowing a project to be
"driven." Connecting to users and to the pull that we talked about in
(Chapter ESCAPE) is another.

Both of these things were lacking in Mr. Two's project. Someone needs to
be able to say "No" to initial requests for functionality. As the
project progresses, they also (and of course, this is harder) need to be
able to say "No" to functionality that previously they have said "Yes"
to. This goes against one of the most fundamental principles of human
behavior -- that we should keep our promises. And we deal with it in
detail in (Chapter COMMIT).

But a big difference between a product owner and a car is that the
product owner can't drive all by themselves. The product owner can't do
all the adaptation that's needed on a software development project. Some
of it has to happen inside the development team. And sometimes, it
*would* be good to have the chairman of the board at the stand-up,
because some decisions do need to be made at board level. This is a big
difference between driving a car and controlling a project. When I say
no one is driving, what I really mean is that no *one person* is driving
all by themselves. Adaptation needs to happen at lots of different
levels inside the organization.

## Difference #3 -- Enabling the empirical process gets you in trouble

Making it clear what's going on -- transparency. Paying attention to
what's going on -- inspection. Changing, or suggesting that changes need
to happen because of what can be seen -- adaptation. All these things
can get you in trouble on a project. Doing this kind of thing can result
in you being silenced or disciplined. It can even get you fired. And
this seems to be a big difference compared with driving a car.

We wouldn't expect anyone driving in a car to get into trouble for
daring to look out of the windscreen. OK, maybe if they were being
kidnapped.

And it might seem unlikely anyone would ever get in trouble when sitting
in the passenger seat of a car for pointing out that the car was going
to hit something and suggesting that the driver take evasive action. But
actually, when it comes to spotting trouble and suggesting some
adaptation, maybe driving is more like project management than it first
seems. Especially if we broaden the definition of "driving" to include
planes on the tarmac of a runway.

Although the details still aren't clear, it's thought that a copilot's
failure to point out to his captain not what could be seen through the
windscreen, but what had been heard on the radio, contributed to the
deadliest crash in aviation history: the Tenerife Airport Disaster
(Faith). And this in turn led to a specific set of practices being added
to training to counteract the difficulties of speaking out in front of
superiors: Crew Resource Management (Kanki).

# Conclusion

The main idea in this book is that the right way of seeing can make us
smart. The extension of that idea is that, in some circumstances, seeing
things in the wrong way can make us dumb. But one thing that we haven't
explored so much is how ways of seeing may make us even smarter when it
becomes obvious how they don't work, as well as how they do. Sometimes,
ways of seeing can make us smarter by letting us see when they work and
when they break down.

The US Military's Red Team Handbook is a manual that helps strategic
thinkers challenge assumptions and explore alternative perspectives
(University). One small part of this is an exercise called "Analogy
Suitability Analysis." This concludes by asking four pithy questions,
which I think we can usefully ask about this way of seeing.

-   Is the analogy valid?

-   Is the analogy relevant?

-   How is the analogy useful?

-   How is the analogy dangerous or unbeneficial?

I started out telling you that managing a project is like driving a car.
And in some ways, it is. But in some other ways, it isn't.

I think this analogy is valid, relevant and useful because it can be
used to explain the concept of empirical process.

Managing a project is like creating the conditions in the car that allow
it to be driven. ***Transparency**,* clean windscreens and unbroken
mirrors. ***Inspection**,* encouraging your team to look through those
windscreens and check in the mirrors. And all of this is so that we can
change what we do because of what we see. It's about creating the right
kind of environment for ***adaptation.***

Facilitating transparency, inspection and adaptation is a way of giving
your project the best chances of delivering.

It's your job as a project manager to facilitate those things. People
will try to cover the windscreen in all kinds of rubbish. It's your job,
however unpleasant, to wipe it off. People will avoid looking out of the
windshield. It's your job to remind them that they need to look out
there. To remind them that if they don't do that they'll crash!

But are there any ways in which this analogy, this way of seeing, is
"dangerous, or unbeneficial"?

Yes -- there are at least three.

First, there's an expectation with a car journey that it will succeed.
That we'll get where we want to go. The research, over decades now,
indicates that we can't expect this kind of success rate for software
development projects.

Secondly, the car analogy might create the impression that one person is
at the wheel. But in a software development project, multiple people
need to be involved in driving. Technical decisions need to be made at
the technical level. Business decisions about the functionality of the
product need to be made at a "business level." Exactly where that level
is inside a hierarchy may be very difficult to determine.

Most software development projects happen inside a hierarchy. And there
are many adaptations that are the "real driving" of the project that
need to happen. The speeding up and slowing down, the changes of
direction, the slamming on of the brakes -- all of these things need to
happen in different places across the hierarchy. No one single person is
driving. No one person can do it all. And the people who do make these
adaptations need to communicate with each other. If you think you're
driving by yourself on a software development project -- well, maybe you
are. You probably aren't. And you definitely shouldn't be.

And there's a third and final reason why thinking about projects in
terms of driving a car can be dangerous or unbeneficial. Keeping the
windshield clean can get you into trouble. We can now see that beyond
recognizing the importance of transparency, inspection and adaptation,
seeing projects as like driving a car is very much like seeing projects
as being about pointing out the pirate ship (Chapter SHIP). Pointing out
things that are dangerous that you can see through the windshield (or
the telescope) can get you fired. This is what we saw with the story of
Mr. Two. He didn't get fired. He did get neutralized.

Why Mr. Two got in trouble has deep roots. Essentially, project
management is about turning ideas into reality. And the resistance that
Mr. Two encountered is a basic and instinctive refusal to deal with the
difference between these two things: ideas and reality. Most of what
we're going to touch on in the second half of this book -- in chapters
(COMMIT, SOME and BOSS) -- will focus on this. And then, the concluding
chapter (CONCLUSION) will try to come up with a more general theoretical
explanation of why this happens and what we can do about it. But before
that, we're going to focus on another way of seeing one of the big
differences between driving a project and driving a car: most car
journeys succeed. As we saw from the Standish report (Johnson), many
projects fail or don't make it to where they want to go. Most car
journeys are a reasonably sure thing. All projects are bets (Chapter
BET).
Chapter BET

> "Intelligence means making bets"
>
> Gerd Gigerenzer (Gigerenzer)

Every project is a bet. In software development projects we end up
playing a series of games to cover up how risky the bet really is. On
many projects there's a pretense that there is no bet. The project is
guaranteed to succeed. There is no risk at all.

But if we can accept that projects *are* bets, then there are some
things we can do. We can dramatically increase the chances of the bet
paying off.

In this chapter, I'm going to show the power of seeing every project as
a bet. And I'm going to show what that means for how we need to behave
and how we should expect others to behave.

Remember what I'm doing in this book. I'm showing you different ways of
looking at projects, ways that maybe you haven't thought of before.
These ways of seeing make you smarter. These are ways of seeing projects
that increase your project's chances of success. They can make
impossible projects possible.

They can also show you when the chances of success on a project are very
low. And when they are low, and you can see that, you have a choice. You
can walk away, or you can seriously revise your expectations. We'll talk
about that more in the following chapter (Chapter NOT BET).

The way of seeing that I'll be using in this chapter is the idea that
the decision to fund any project is a bet. This might not be something
that many people associated with the project want to admit. And because
they don't want to admit it, making the bet sets off a whole bunch of
other games. Games that the people who made the bet have to play. And,
if a project is going to have any chance of succeeding, we as project
managers are going to have to play even more games to shorten the odds.
Or, in certain cases, encourage an even bigger bet, to save the project.

# What is a bet?

A bet is a decision taken in uncertain circumstances. There's an
understanding that there is the potential for a reward. But at the same
time, there is also a risk. There is also a chance of failure. And for
me, I think that is also a pretty good description of a project. That's
what it is. A project is a game of chance. It has some similarities to
betting on a hand in poker, betting on a turn of the roulette wheel, or
betting on a horse. There's a chance of a payoff, but there's also a
substantial chance of losing everything.

Looking at projects in this way helps us understand some things. If we
didn't know that a project was a bet, some people's behavior would be
bewildering. And it's also a comfort in those times when things don't go
well. Sometimes the dice come up "snake eyes." Often the third ace that
you were hoping you would be dealt doesn't appear in your poker hand.
Sometimes your project just stalls or gets canceled.

Because here's a truth that I hope doesn't come as a surprise.

> Not all projects succeed.

Your project might not succeed. Why?

1.  Because you're no good at your job?

2.  Because your team is useless?

3.  Because the odds of the project succeeding were ridiculously long to
    start with?

It's probably not 1 and, anyway, you're improving those odds by reading
this book. Very rarely, it can be 2 -- the team. But a lot of the time
it's 3. The project was a bet. There were non-zero chances that it would
fail. And it did.

If you understand that projects are bets, then you can do the right
thing. You can do the right thing even in confusing, irrational and
emotional circumstances. You can help your stakeholders avoid risks and
take advantage of opportunities.

## What happened at the beginning?

As project managers, we're almost never involved when the decision is
made to kick off the project. We have to piece together the thinking,
the reason for the funding of the project after the fact. Sometimes it
can feel like an accident investigation.

Was the reason for the new project that the old system was unpopular and
unmaintainable? If so, then built into the project is a bet. It's a bet
that the same organization, with possibly the same people, will be able
to do a better job this time than they did the last.

Did this project get started because there was some budget that needed
using up? Well then, there's a bet that something useful can be done
before the money runs out. Or maybe a bet that someone can find some
more money.

Or it's a wild bet, with no real expectation of any payoff. Maybe this
is a brilliant opportunity to try something new. Extreme programming --
an Agile method -- supposedly came out of just such an opportunity. A
project that nobody expected to succeed (Beck).

Knowing the kind of bet our project is making can help us to understand
the games that are being played. It can help us understand what we need
to do, what games we need to play. And it might explain why others
appear to be behaving so strangely.

# Game number one -- sooner or later someone has to say "f\*\*k it."

The economist Herb Simon coined the term "bounded rationality." (Simon)

Bounded rationality is the idea that rationality is limited. It has to
be. If it weren't, our reasoning would run the danger of going on
forever. Real people have to bound their rationality. They have to make
a decision that's good enough. It is satisfactory. This isn't
necessarily the same as the optimal decision.

Of course, we would like to be rational. Or we imagine that we would
like to be. Certainly, we would like to be *seen* as rational. The
people who fund projects would like to be seen as rational. They would
like to say that they gave full consideration to all relevant
information. They'd like to think that they did all of that. Then they
made the rational decision to greenlight a project.

But there's a problem. The world is big. "All" the information is a lot
of things. And deciding exactly what is "relevant" and what isn't is an
even bigger problem. Once you start gathering information, it's really
hard to know when to stop. It's a bit like pulling a loose thread on
your favorite pullover.

This is called "analysis paralysis." And it is a common problem in
software development projects. In reality, a project needs a rule, a
"finishing function" to decide when to stop. Otherwise, analysis can go
on forever. Well, maybe not forever. But analysis can certainly go on
long enough that, whatever the project was about, it becomes irrelevant.
I've worked on projects where the only "finishing function" was that the
budget for analysis had run out.

Getting stuck in an analysis phase or indeed any other phase is
something that we've seen before. It's falling back to doing something
that's "safe." Well, it's doing something that *feels* safe. Even though
getting stuck in any phase will eventually kill the project. This is of
course, our old enemy, agreed activity from (Chapter SHIP).

As an Agile coach, my coaching often really consisted of one word. On
some projects it was "start" -- start doing development, stop waiting
for the analysis to be finished. On some projects it was "release." Stop
waiting for all the software to be perfect.

What was I doing in these situations? I was encouraging whoever was in
charge to finally shrug their shoulders and say "f\*\*k it." When I was
asked for my opinion, and sometimes when I wasn't, I was saying "release
it" or "start development." And both of these bits of advice actually
boil down to saying: "F\*\*k it! Take the bet."

So, at some point, if anything is ever going to happen, someone has to
draw a "bound" around the analysis. No matter how careful and
considerate they are, at some point

> Someone needs to shrug their shoulders and say "f\*\*k it."

We need someone to do this. But in general, people don't want to appear
reckless or irrational. So, the fact that a project has been kicked off
with a shrug and an expletive sets off three other games.

# Game number two -- HIWTYL (heads I win, tails you lose)

One of the best product owners that I ever worked with was rarely around
when the project was in trouble. But when we had something that worked,
software that she could show to her users, she turned up.

She was a strong advocate for the software when she was demonstrating to
potential users. But most of the time, to keep track of what was going
on with development, she sent a deputy. When she did turn up to see the
development team, she mainly looked disappointed. Disappointed that we
hadn't solved whatever the current problem was. And of course,
disappointed, that we weren't delivering fast enough.

She wasn't overbearing or too demanding. She never complained that the
team weren't working nights. But if the project had trouble -- she
wasn't around.

Finding a good product owner can be a tricky business. And for a long
time, I struggled with the idea that she *had* been a good product
owner. She'd done a very good job for the project and the product, but
she'd been slightly, slightly what?

Flaky?

Dodgy?

Dishonorable?

Could I really blame her for making sure that she was never around when
there was trouble?

It was only later that I understood that she was playing a game that had
a name: HIWTYL (heads I win, tails you lose).

This game is discussed by Venkatesh Rao in his book "The Gervais
Principle." (Rao). Rao explains one thing that will make it more likely
that someone, at some point, says "f\*\*k it." They will take the chance
when they know they will be able to take credit for the bet of the
project and accept its winnings. But only if they are comfortable that
they can be nowhere to be seen if the project is a failure.

We might think of this as being like T. S. Eliot's "mystery cat"
Macavity. When you look for him, he isn't there (Eliot). Especially if
there's any sign of trouble.

Isn't this outrageous behavior? It certainly seems so. But maybe you
should wait before you start saddling up your high horse. Before you
gallop away with a feeling of moral superiority. There is something that
you might want to bear in mind. If it weren't for the HIWTYL principle,
we'd simply have fewer projects. Far fewer people would ever have the
courage/recklessness/entrepreneurial vision to say "f\*\*k it."

And so, far fewer projects would ever get kicked off. And
"entrepreneurial vision" is a clue to a second reason.

HIWTYL is written into the laws of all western countries. And without it
most commerce wouldn't exist. HIWTYL is what the "limited" means in
"limited company."

Buckminster Fuller was an inventor and visionary. In his book "Grunch of
Giants" (Fuller), he explains the need for limited companies. He does so
in terms of the medieval trading city of Venice. Merchants wanted to
send ships off around the world, they wanted backers to invest in their
"argosies." The promise was that the ships would return full of gold and
spices. But of course, sometimes, the ships didn't come back at all.

Shipping is a business with inherent risk. When a fleet of ships doesn't
come back, who should be responsible? Who should take the hit? Should
the merchant who took on all that investment still owe money to his
investors? Should he personally go bankrupt? Or should he be able to
shrug his shoulders and declare his *company* bankrupt? The company
being a thing separate from himself and his own personal wealth? If he
could do that, then he would be free to start again with a new company,
looking for new investors.

There may not be complete agreement on what the answer to this should
be. And there might not be a lot of love -- or indeed investors -- for
someone whose companies seem to go bust rather too frequently. But
western society has agreed on an answer.

Limited companies are a thing. And so is HIWTYL -- not being around when
the company, or any particular venture, fails. And also, crucially of
course, taking advantage of the benefits of things going well. These
things are written into the way that our modern world does business. We
might not think that this is how the world should work. But it's how it
does work.

But of course, people are rarely going to be this candid about what's
going on in a project. The people who kicked off the project aren't
going to be clear that at some point, they drew a ring around the
benefits and risks and said "f\*\*k it." They aren't likely to mention
they are playing HIWTYL. Nobody is going to say that they will only be
around if the project succeeds.

What about everybody else involved with a project? What about members of
the project team? What about the project's managers? Are they being
entirely honest about a project being a bet? About its risk of success
and failure? No. They're not.

# Game number three -- the hiding hand

The economist Albert Hirschman had many jobs in a long career. He was
born in Germany and was a young, Jewish man at the beginning of the
World War II. He escaped from Germany and fought for both the French and
US armies during the war. When it was over, he acted as an interpreter
for Nazis accused of war crimes.

Later, he was involved in the management and monitoring of projects
funded by the World Bank in Asia and South America. Hirschman noticed
that often the projects were fundamentally wrong-headed. Their goals
were unrealistic. Predicted benefits were not likely.

But there was always support for these projects. There was always
enthusiasm from the people involved. Nobody pointed out the obvious
problems. Nobody raised questions about the benefits that were promised.
Nobody raised an eyebrow at projected timelines.

He called this phenomenon the "hiding hand." This is a reference to the
idea discussed by the pioneering economist ­Adam Smith of the "guiding
hand" (Hirschman). Smith's idea is that there is a force which
supposedly works to set prices in a market. There is no one person who
sets prices. A mysterious hand seems to nudge them either higher or
lower.

Hirschman saw something similar on projects he observed. Almost
magically, the people involved in the project agreed to keep quiet about
its problems. This was not an explicit oath of silence. It was an
emergent behavior. A "hiding hand" muffled and concealed their
objections. At least until the project was up and running and it would
be much more difficult to cancel.

I have experienced this myself multiple times. A banking project that I
worked on was impossible. It was missing a key transaction database.
This transaction database had been talked about in the company for
years. But the project to build it had somehow never got off the ground.
Our project just wouldn't work without this dependency. Nobody working
directly on the project ever mentioned this. As coaches working to help
the project succeed, we discovered it almost by accident. By the time
that we did, it was too late. Our project was fully funded and started
-- even though it had no chance of success.

We've already talked about something similar to the "hiding hand." In
(Chapter SHIP), we explained that members of the team instinctively
avoid bad news. They avoid reporting it. They seem not to notice it. Or
they simply don't acknowledge it. They avoid conflict. They don't need
to talk about doing this avoidance. They do it instinctively. We called
this "agreed activity."

Hirschman was directly distributing funds. He was there to encourage
economic growth. He realized that lots of different groups of people
take part in the hiding hand because they want the project to start.

The team want to get paid; they want the project to start. The person
who said "f\*\*k it" is making a bet; *they* want the project to start!
Lots of different people outside the team are also colluding. They're
covering up any issues that might delay the project; *they* also want
the project to start!

What Hirschman could see clearly was that a project might be a high-risk
bet with low odds of success. Still, if that bet involves paying a team,
the team won't stand in its way. Everybody involved is incentivized to
pretend that the project isn't a bet, it *isn't* a risk. The project is
declared rational. People agree it is sensible. They pretend it is
deliverable. And this insight also explains why everybody involved with
the project agrees to play out the next game.

# Game number four -- project management theater

# 

People who kick off projects want lots of projects to be happening. Just
like the merchants who want to send out lots of ships from Venice. This
gives them the best chance of having some of their "ships come in." Some
of these projects will succeed and they will do their level best to take
the credit for them. And HIWTYL means that they don't care too much
about the rest. If things go wrong, they won't be there.

And the people who work on projects want -- well, they want to work. So,
another game gets played. This game pretends that we need to follow a
project management methodology. One particular methodology (whichever
one is the current favorite) is the only one that will work. It pretends
that if we do this, we are guaranteed success. Of course, like all
supernatural rituals, it only works if it's conducted perfectly. It only
works if it's conducted by anointed priests. And if it doesn't work, it
isn't the fault of the Voodoo. It's the fault of the people performing
the ritual. They didn't do it right.

This is what one flavor of project management theater looks like:

There should be Agile ceremonies. There needs to be a special language
with special names for everything. No one can be hired without project
management qualifications. Qualifications in a particular flavor of
methodology are the only useful ones. They might even be matched to a
particular numbered version. They go out of date and need renewing after
a number of years.

I was once asked to "coach" a Scrum Master who was "performing poorly."
The perception was that the way that he was running stand-ups and
planning might be causing problems. I attended a few stand-ups. I had a
chat with the Scrum Master. We followed that up with an estimation
session with the team. We managed to unearth the real problem with the
project. The project had -- by our estimate -- SEVEN TIMES too much
work. It had seven times as much work as could possibly be done by the
deadline. And the deadline couldn't be moved. It was a deadline set by
the government. This is the dreaded N that we've discussed previously in
(Chapter CAR). The way this guy was running stand-ups was hardly the
issue.

When I talk about "project management theater," I'm not criticizing the
use of Agile methods. I've talked all the way through this book -- and
I'll talk more -- about the vital importance of Agile ceremonies. I
really believe that iterative and incremental approaches are the only
way to deliver software development projects.

Rather, what I'm saying is that we should understand that project
management methods can be misused. One of the ways this happens is that
they are used to cover up the fact that what we're doing is a risky
endeavor, i.e., we're trying to deliver seven times as much work as we
possibly can in the available time.

The project is a bet and neither group -- not the people who made the
bet nor the team members who are implementing it -- wants to admit this.
Nobody really wants to admit this.

As the author and poker player Annie Duke (Duke) tells us in her book
"Thinking in Bets":

> "We are uncomfortable with the idea that luck plays a significant role
> in our lives."

So, we might be a little comforted by the next two games that I discuss.
Because in these two games, we have an opportunity to improve the odds
of the bet that was made on the project. And, maybe just a little,
reduce the role that luck can play.

# Game number five -- shortening the odds

The next two games I talk about are different from the first four. These
are games that, as a project manager, you get a say in. You can play
them to improve the chances of a project being a success.

The claim I made in the first half of this chapter is that, if a project
gets started, someone has made a bet. Someone has shrugged their
shoulders and said "f\*\*k it!"

And when they do, that will almost automatically kick off the games
we've talked about. They play HIWTYL (heads I win, tails you lose), the
hiding hand and project management theater.

As a project manager, I don't think you have any choice but to play
these games.

Trying to stop people playing these games just annoys them. And these
are often senior people that it's not worth annoying. But there are
other games that you can play. Other games you *should* be playing that
can increase a project's chance of success.

The first of these games is to work with your team to break the big bet
of the project into smaller, lower-stakes bets. But you can also make
these bets much more likely to pay off, by cheating -- well if it were
poker, it would be cheating. Relax, I know we've talked about the queasy
moral status of HIWTYL, the hiding hand and project management theater.
But what I'm suggesting here isn't even slightly dodgy. It's just user
research.

Incremental delivery and user research. These are solid, wholesome
things that you can do to give your projects the best chance of success.

## Break the big bet into smaller bets

If you are working using Agile, then you will already be familiar with
the concept of making frequent, small releases. Now we've talked about
projects in terms of bets, it's easy to see that these small releases
are also bets.

Releasing software in small chunks reduces the risk of the big bet that
is being made. Testing those small chunks with users makes it clear to
everyone what works and what doesn't. This is the strategy that we
talked about in (Chapter ESCAPE). As we discussed in (Chapter ESCAPE),
releasing working software to users isn't an all or nothing thing.

Release of working software, using live data, to the people who will
ultimately be using it is the goal. But there are lots of stages on the
way to this destination which are also useful.

Releasing to a group of test users, using test data, is still better
than not releasing at all. Releasing to test servers is an important
advance. These can be servers that are like the live servers. Even if
they aren't completely the same, they can be the same in important ways.
And this is much better than letting the software stay, untested, on
local machines.

Releasing just some features, even if they are a tiny subset, still
reduces the odds of failure. Getting those features all the way to live
tells us a ton of stuff that we'd otherwise not know.

Each of these moves nearer to live software being used by real live
users with their own data is a small bet. Crucially, even if it doesn't
pay off, the damage that we've done is also small. And we can learn from
that bet, so our next one is better-informed.

But there is another way that small bets can pay off.

This is what has happened on many projects that I've worked on. The team
does an initial investigation. It finds something interesting. There's a
small part of the whole project that could be delivered in a short space
of time. And it could deliver a lot of value.

For example, I worked on a mortgage project as a coach. We did some user
research. It showed there was one question that customers really wanted
answered.

Actually, it was two questions:

1.  Can I have a mortgage?

2.  How much will it cost?

The answers to both these questions were provided by the proposed
system. But only after filling in a lengthy questionnaire. This
questionnaire was also wired into back-end systems for credit checking.
It was also connected to the system that would eventually release the
money!

In the original design, the only way for a customer to find out if they
could get a mortgage was to complete an application. They needed to go
all the way through the formal process to get an answer

We discussed it with the team. We realized that we could deliver an "in
principle" mortgage calculator. This calculator could answer these
important questions. Can I have a mortgage? How much will it cost? And
this could be delivered independently of the "back end." The system
could answer these questions based on a small number of initial details.
It didn't need to start a formal mortgage application.

This changed the dynamic of the project. It put something useful in the
hands of users early on. It told the development team a ton of useful
information. The team got a small service working. And in doing so, they
found out a lot about the difficulties of getting software onto test and
live servers. This is the strategy of connecting to pull that we were
talking about in (Chapter ESCAPE).

It gave the project a "win" in its early stages. This brought with it
further support and funding for the more difficult parts of the project
that followed.

## The sure thing

If you have enough inside information, then a bet isn't really a bet,
it's starting to get near the dream of all gamblers -- a sure thing.

You may have realized what we're doing here. We're breaking the big bet
into multiple small bets informed by what we already know about our
users. This is the kind of exploration of the complex space that we've
already talked about in (Chapter SWAMP) and (Chapter ESCAPE). If we're
playing "proper" poker, part of the game is to guess what cards the
other players have in their hands. But this is where the value of user
research on a project shines through.

When we initially release small amounts of working software to real
users, of course there is still some risk. But it's a small bet, and
it's informed by the initial research that we've done with users. Any
further releases that we make are now more informed. They're informed by
user research. And they're informed by the feedback that we got from the
last bet, the last small release of working software.

We start to learn more about our users. We understand more about the
value of what we're building. We start to know more about the
environment we're trying to build it in.

This means that the more small releases we make, the more user research
we do, the more what we're doing starts to feel like cheating! It's not
a bet anymore. It's a sure thing. We're sufficiently informed about
what's in other players' hands. Sounds great right? Unfortunately, it's
not always that simple.

# Game number six -- upping the bet -- deal or no deal?

It's a good idea to make small bets using working software. It reduces
project risk. Informing these bets with user research reduces the risks
and shortens the odds even more. Sometimes these techniques can improve
the odds up to the point where it hardly seems like a bet at all.
Sometimes. But other times these "small bets" reveal something quite
different.

As a result of our investigations, we might start to realize something.
The bet that the project made isn't big *enough.* More needs to be
spent, more time needs to be taken. More must be risked if we're to give
the project any chance of success.

Often the way this reveals itself is on a project where the focus has
been technology and functionality. But now, as the technology starts to
be used, something becomes obvious. To give the project any chance of
success, it needs additional resources. A bigger bet needs to be made.
Again, there's a direct analogy here with poker. At some point in a
poker game, the only way that you're going to win is by making a bigger
bet, by raising the stakes.

What kinds of things might we find that make us think we need to up the
stakes on a project?

Here are some examples.

**Moderation.** What if we have an application that involves users
creating content? Early releases of this application show it will only
really work if it has a moderation team. When the moderation team
doesn't get involved, the site collapses into trolling and legal
problems.

**More content.** What about an application that sells high-quality
content? Like a streaming service? The high-quality content of the app
is the thing that's selling it. Now it has an audience, the need for
content is huge. Where's that content going to come from? Who's going to
pay for it?

**Bigger servers.** The project is a wild success, but a result of this
is that every hour or so, the servers collapse because too many people
are using the service. The original architecture didn't imagine this
level of use. When engagement with the service goes beyond a certain
point, it takes off exponentially. The current system has no way of
dealing with this -- other than falling over.

We should definitely be doing user research and we should be breaking
the project up into smaller bets. But as we do this, we can uncover lots
of different kinds of extra needs. We can find extra opportunities. And
sometimes we find that to make this bet pay off, the project needs to up
the stakes.

## Projects aren't like poker

In poker, there are specific points when the player can up the stakes.
These points are clear. Also, in poker it's clear that deciding when to
up the stakes -- and when not to -- is an important part of the way that
the game is played.

Unfortunately, in software projects, these things are not clear.

Let's remember that the games of HIWTYL, hiding hand and project
management theater are actively conspiring to cover up that a project is
a bet *at all*. Sadly, there's a side-effect to these games. It makes it
more difficult to justify the helpful games that we've been
recommending. If we do start building in small increments, releasing
software and doing user research, this makes it clear that there is some
risk involved.

This is a contradiction. Covering up that a project is a bet also
obscures the points where, if the stakes aren't increased, the bet of
the project will be lost. Work might carry on, the project still looks
alive, but it isn't. Unless the extra bet is made and the stakes are
upped, it's lost. Almost always, upping the stakes looks like an
admission of failure of some kind. It's a difficult decision to make and
it's a difficult decision to explain.

This is a crucial point in a project. If they can see the opportunity,
the project manager can have a big influence on the project's chances of
success. I said that I'd show you ways to make seemingly impossible
projects possible. Here we have an obvious thing that we can do to turn
a project from impossible to possible.

We need to be able to spot the points where the stakes need to be
increased. Then, if we can make a good case for actually increasing
them, we can really make a project a success.

## Upping the bet

Here's the thing about these decision points. We've got to a stage where
there is a choice to up the bet. If this happens, we can give the
project a good chance of success. If this choice isn't made, we might as
