well stop wasting everybody's time. This can feel tricky. But we should
remind ourselves of this important fact.\
\
*This is a much better bet than the original decision to start the
project.*

Let's say that we've done all the work that we talked about in game
number five -- the odds-shortening game. We've pushed working software
early and we've done our user research. So now, we have a good idea
about what the users value and what will work technically. This is one
of those points where we can really make a difference on a project.

Most of the time, whether to up the stakes isn't our decision. It's a
decision for a senior stakeholder who has control over resources. We
aren't doing the betting.

But we can put a case to the people who can increase the stakes. We can
give them the best information we have in support of upping the bet.

I recently worked on a project that, on the face of it, was a simple
website. But the content of the site was a collection of complex
technical documents.

We developed the software in small bits. At every stage, we encouraged
users of the site to use what we had developed.

Slowly it became clear that, for the project to have any chance of
success, the client needed to up the stakes. They needed a whole team of
specialists to curate these technical documents. The project
neededpeople who really understood the field. These documents weren't
going to look after themselves.

But sadly, we didn't see it; nor, I think, did the client, or not until
it was too late. We didn't have time to put together a strong case for
increasing the investment, for upping the bet.

This meant that the project was just allowed to run out of money. It
never really had a chance to be a success. Technology wasn't enough.
High-quality content wasn't enough. For the site to be a success, the
bet needed to be increased. The site needed a dedicated editorial team
to look after the content. And that never arrived.

### Deal or No Deal?

In the TV show "Deal or No Deal" (Deal or No Deal), a contestant has a
mystery box in front of them. It is one of 21. When they open the box,
there will be an amount of prize money inside. That prize money is
between £0.01 and £250,000. By opening some of the other 20 boxes, the
contestant has to decide whether to open their own box. Their box might
contain big money, or next to nothing. Or maybe they don't want to open
their box. Maybe they want to take the deal.

What deal? Each round of the gameshow consists of opening one of the
boxes. After some of the rounds, the contestant receives a phone call
from "the Banker." This call offers a sum of money. While there is still
a chance that the box sitting in front of the contestant contains big
money, the offers from the Banker will be higher. But with each opening
of the boxes, the odds change. The game for the contestants is to decide
when to accept the "deal" from the Banker. Or when to press on and get
to the point where they can open their own box. But the real challenge
for the contestants is to not let their emotions get in the way. This
can be difficult, because their chances of winning "big money" seem to
lurch from optimistic to pessimistic as each box is opened.

Does this remind you of anything? It should. As we progress through a
project, the odds of its success can seem to rise and fall dramatically.
Let's say we're playing the game of trying to shorten the odds that we
just discussed. We're exploring the space of the project by doing user
research, by releasing small amounts of working software. What we're
doing is "opening boxes." We're revealing the odds of the final project
paying off.

But here's a crucial difference. The Banker never calls. But sometimes,
you have to call the Banker! You, or someone on your project, has to go
and ask for some more money. Someone who is a stakeholder on your
project has to identify when the project needs more support. They need
to see that the bet needs to be increased to make the project pay off.
They need to ring the Banker.

### How to structure projects around making bets

The UK government spends a lot of money on software projects. So, by the
argument of this chapter, it must make a lot of bets. In one way, as a
taxpayer, this doesn't really bear thinking about. But of course, in
another, if bets are being made, I want them to be as well-informed as
they can possibly be. In the UK there is a body called the Government
Digital Service. It is responsible for government projects in the UK. To
make its software development project bets, it uses its own service
manual. And this has a section dedicated to Agile delivery (Government).

This service manual seems to be aware that projects are bets. It breaks
its projects into four stages (actually 5; there's a final stage of
"retirement"). These first four stages look a lot like rounds of
betting. The four stages are:

1.  Discovery. Spend a small amount of money and time. Investigate the
    problem space. Do user and stakeholder research.

2.  Alpha. Develop an initial prototype and test it with users.

3.  Beta. Iterate on the prototype.

4.  Live. Go live with the service.

Set up this way, a project looks a lot more like a poker game. The
"players" are the sponsors of the projects. They get several chances to
decide whether to stop or keep going. They can up the stakes, keep the
stakes the same or cut their losses. And at the end of each stage,
there's a service review, where the sponsors can make their decision.
Should they carry on? Or should they quit?

Of course, such an approach isn't without its own problems. The short
time and fixed-cost nature of the discovery makes it very tempting for
suppliers to take short cuts. That's my experience. For example, do
"desk research" rather than user research. Or do "user research" only
with the people who are paying them to do the discovery rather than with
actual users of the service. This can very quickly turn into an exercise
in telling those people what they want to hear.

Opinions seem to vary about the Alpha "prototype." Should it include
working software? I absolutely think it should -- for all the reasons
that we talked about in (Chapter ESCAPE). The most common reason that
I've seen people push for no working software in Alpha is, in my
opinion, a bad reason. The team wants to avoid conflict. The designers,
user researchers and developers probably don't agree on some things. But
bringing these disagreements to the surface early reduces the risk for
the project. Producing working software often shows up these conflicts.
And in the most helpful way -- backed by feedback from users.

If there's no software development done in the Alpha stage, that just
turns Beta into a bigger bet.

Another way to make Beta less of a big bet, even if some development was
done in Alpha, is to split it into "private Beta" and "public Beta."

Private Beta is a stage where the software is "live" on some kind of
server that can be accessed. That may be only within the organization.
It may be only available to certain select people within the
organization who have an account. But at least it's up and running
somewhere and interacting with users.

Public Beta is really where we wanted to be all along. Because in public
Beta we're fully connecting with users. In public Beta, these are a
wider group than they were in Private Beta. Still, it might not be a
huge group. But now, we're connecting with pull and we're drastically
shortening the project's odds.

Finally, from what I've heard, most government projects using this
framework don't make it to "Live."

That doesn't mean that the services that these projects deliver aren't
online and being used by thousands of people. Rather, it means that the
paperwork to get through the final service review to get to "Live"
status is just too onerous. It often involves the owner of the service
signing to confirm that they take on all the risks associated with the
project. Remember -- this is probably the person who said "f\*\*k it,"
in the first place. And if they're any good at playing "HIWTYL," that's
the last thing they're ever going to do.

### Scoping the odds: form and the planning fallacy

Why do horses in a horse race have certain odds associated with them?
Partly, it's because of their "form." Form is the history of how a
particular horse has run in previous races. We can take how a horse has
performed in previous races as a reasonable prediction of how it will
run in the future.

When I read chapter 23 of "Thinking Fast and Slow" by Daniel Kahneman
(Kahneman), the scales dropped from my eyes. Chapter 23 is about
something called the "planning fallacy."

*The planning fallacy is a particular kind of psychological flaw* that
we all suffer from. This is called a "cognitive bias."

Let's say you give someone a list of things to do. Then you ask them to
estimate how long it will take to finish the things on that list. Their
estimate will be near the ***most optimistic possible value.***

We simply can't imagine things going wrong. And it isn't just beginners
and amateurs who make this mistake. Old hands aren't much better.
Professionals make the same mistake.

Kahneman tells the story of being involved in a writing project for a
school textbook in Israel. After three years, there was a meeting of all
the people involved in writing the book. Kahneman made a suggestion. He
asked members of the team to write down on a sticky note how much longer
they thought the project would take. The average of these estimates was
four years!

Kahneman asked the most senior member of the group -- a guy called
Seymore -- how long projects like this typically took. Seymore thought
for a minute, and then said, "projects like this typically take ten
years."

"Actually," he said, "a lot of projects don't take the full ten years --
about half of them get canceled or abandoned before that."

Kahneman could feel the depression washing over the team. He had an
idea. "Is there any reason why we might get our project done more
quickly? Are we a more able team?"

Seymore thought for a minute and then looked a bit embarrassed. No, he
said, the team was a bit light on experience.

So, what did Kahneman do? Having found out that the project he was on
for three years was likely to take either another seven years, on
average, or fail. What did he do? Surely it would have been rational at
that point to walk away!? But he didn't.

Just because he's a Nobel prize winner doesn't mean that he wasn't
susceptible to the same biases as the rest of us. And sure enough. The
project was never finished.

So Kahneman didn't manage to take advantage of what might be one of the
most useful questions that we can ask about our project that isn't
actually about our project.

*How have other projects that have tried to do similar things fared in
this environment?*

That is a very good guide to how your project will turn out. It's a
reliable indication of what the odds are for the bet that's been made on
the project.

# Conclusion

All projects are bets. The only way that any project is going to get off
the ground is if someone says "f\*\*k it!" Somebody draws a bound around
the rationality, the pros and cons of a project, shrugs and decides to
take a risk.

And when they do that a bunch of other games -- HIWTYL, the hiding hand
and project management theater -- get going. As a project manager,
there's almost nothing you can do to influence that. But what you *can*
do is explore the odds by encouraging your team to do research with
users. You can increase the odds of success. You can do this by
incrementally releasing working software.

This business of exploring the odds will be a lot like the emotional
rollercoaster of playing "Deal Or No Deal." At some points, it will look
likely that the project will pay off big. At some other points it will
look like the project will either not pay off at all or will pay much
less than initially imagined.

This isn't a part of "Deal Or No Deal" but maybe it would make a very
interesting game. At some point it becomes obvious that the only way
that the bet of a project is going to pay off is if the stakes are
upped. It becomes obvious that if the bet isn't increased the project
won't pay off at all.

*Someone is going to have to phone the Banker!*

This is a second way in which a project manager can positively influence
the betting that's going on in a project. They can make clear these
changes in the perceived odds of the project. They can point out when
the stakes need to be increased. Of course, this can be extremely
difficult. Because, as with "Deal Or No Deal," each change in odds
brings with it a change in emotions.

But what if we find out something else? We're doing our user research
and releasing working software. We're reassessing the project's chances.
What if as we're doing this, we come to realize that the project doesn't
have any chance *at all*. What, as project managers, should we do when
we come to realize that not all projects *are* bets? Some projects are
certain failures. These certain failures are what we'll talk about in
the next chapter (Chapter NOT BET).
Chapter NOT BET

Most projects are bets. They have a chance of success, and they have a
chance of failure. In those projects, there are often obvious things
that can be done to improve the odds of their success.\
\
But some projects *aren't* bets. Because of the way that they are
constructed, they have no chance of success. They are surefire failures.
As project managers, it's important for our own success, safety and
sanity to understand this. There might still be things we can do to help
these projects. But it may well be that they are doomed from the start.

# Wall of shame

I suppose I did it out of desperation. I used the back of the filing
cabinet next to my desk. It faced the corridor. I wrote in the biggest
letters I could on a piece of paper "Wi-fi wall of shame."

I was working on a program called the "DQ program." The aim of the
program was to digitize a complex, multi-step process end-to-end that
had been run on paper for hundreds of years. It didn't start in the days
of the Pharoah and papyrus, but it did start with writing on vellum! The
teams working on the program filled an entire floor of an office block
in south London. We all had desks. We all had chairs. The lights were
on. But the wi-fi connection was weak and feeble. Repeated complaints
and escalations didn't seem to have resulted in any improvement. So, I
thought I'd try something a bit different. I said to the members of my
team, every time you have a problem with the wi-fi, every time that you
can't do your work because of the wi-fi, write the date and time on a
sticky note and stick it on the wall of shame.

The wall of shame filled up *fast.* Maybe a week later, the wi-fi
seriously improved. I never knew who saw the wall, or what they did to
make the wi-fi work. It felt good to remove one of the problems on my
list. At least the DQ program wasn't going to fail because of poor
wi-fi. But the DQ program was probably still going to fail because it
was what we'll call a "nonsense accumulator."

# The Archbishop of Canterbury is alive and well

Here's a joke. Bob is on holiday in Rome. He visits the Vatican. Through
a series of unlikely accidents, he discovers that the pope is dead. But
it's Easter weekend and the church is covering up the big news until
after the weekend's celebrations. Bob tells his friend Dave to put a big
bet on at the bookies for the pope to die. It's a sure thing. Bob and
Dave agree that they'll split the winnings! The Tuesday after Easter,
the Vatican makes the announcement, which comes as no surprise to Bob
and Dave. The pope is dead.

Bob goes round to Dave's to get his share of the winnings. Dave looks
miserable.

"Sorry Bob. We lost the bet."

"What?" says Bob. "How could we have lost the bet?"

"I got carried away," says Dave. "I put the money on an accumulator for
the Archbishop of Canterbury to kick the bucket as well."

What's an "accumulator?" An accumulator is a bet on multiple horses --
or in the world of jokes, multiple religious men in funny hats. The
winnings from one horse (if it wins) are bet on the next horse. A tiny
bet accumulates into a large payoff. So, betting on the pope/archbishop
double offered a payoff that Dave couldn't resist. Although of course
the increase in the odds of it happening turned a dream bet into a
certain loss.

For a long time, I'd noticed that some projects get foiled, their
chances of success flattened by the addition of extra unnecessary risk.
But I didn't have a way of explaining it to other people. Now, using
betting as a way of seeing, I do. I have a way of explaining it. Some
projects are bets. Some projects are risky bets. And then some projects
-- notionally they are bets -- but they're like a bet that the pope, the
poor old Archbishop of Canterbury AND the Dalai Lama will all shuffle
off this mortal coil on the same Easter weekend. They aren't really
bets, they are *nonsense accumulators.*

# *Nonsense accumulators*

What I mean by a nonsense accumulator is this. Someone has made a bet.
This is not necessarily a sure thing like secret knowledge of the pope's
death. But it is a reasonable bet. Someone has taken a bet like that and
then they've turned it into an impossible bet. They've turned it into a
nonsense accumulator by combining it with a series of other bets --
crazy, long-shot bets.

For example, most projects have one bet that has moderately long odds.
That bet is something like this:

> "We can deliver this project with the team that we have."

Nearly all projects have a second bet, also with reasonably long odds.

> "We can deliver something that will be valuable to our organization
> and its customers."

On nearly all projects, these bets have to combine with a bet about
delivering within a certain timeframe, at a certain cost and to a
reasonable quality. Many people will recognize these three aspects of a
project -- time, cost and quality -- as the three sides of what's known
as the "iron triangle" of project management (Atkinson). The idea is
that any change to one of these three values -- let's go with our old
favorite, an aggressive deadline -- will affect the other two aspects of
a project, namely its cost and quality. When we start to think about
these three variables, we realize pretty quickly that there are values
for those variables that would make the chances of the project
succeeding quite high. And there are values that would make it really
unlikely to succeed. If the deadline is too crazy, there isn't really
any amount of money that you could spend that would give the project
good odds of succeeding. If the allowable cost is too low, quality and
timescale are going to suffer.

For the purposes of our discussion here, we don't have to worry too much
more about the iron triangle right now. All we need to really understand
is that, taken together, there will be specific odds of a project being
delivered for a certain time, at a certain cost and at a certain
quality.

So , the chance of the project being delivered on time, on budget and to
a certain standard of quality, the chance of it being valuable and the
chance of it being doable with the team we have -- these odds combine.
They multiply, to give the overall chance of the project being a
success. And if any one of these bets doesn't succeed, neither does the
project. Projects are accumulator bets. They rely on a bunch of
different bets paying off.

Imagine that we give these kinds of odds for the main "bets" that affect
any project.

> Delivering something with the team that we have: 2-1\
> Delivering something valuable to customers: 2-1
>
> Delivering the project on time, at a reasonable cost and quality: 3-1.

Because odds multiply, this gives the project a 1 in 12 chance of
success -- that's about 8 percent.

But what if in addition, we're asked to try to deliver this project
without talking to users? This might lengthen the odds substantially.

> Delivering the project without talking to users: 10-1.

That's odds of 120-1. Now we're down to a 0.8 percent chance of success.
And what if we're also asked to deliver the project without access to
the basic infrastructure that's required: test servers, live servers and
basic internet access?

> Delivering a project without adequate access to wi-fi: 50-1.

These additional "long-shot bets" have turned this into a project with
odds of 6000-1 -- that's around a 0.017 percent chance of success.

We could argue about any of these odds. But the general principle is
valid. Somewhere along this journey, these extra unnecessary obstacles
that were added to the project made it much less likely that it would be
a success.

What's particularly frustrating is that many of these obstacles are
things that could be fixed. The wi-fi could be fixed. Users can be
involved in testing. Environments can be provisioned. Software can be
released frequently.

In his classic book on screenwriting, Robert McKee (McKee) says:

*"We do not wish to see a character suffer due to stupidity, masochism,
or self-inflicted wounds."\
\
*But many of the obstacles that we see on projects are exactly that:
self-inflicted wounds. Long-shot bets that nobody should have ever
heaped onto the initial sensible bets. Extra bets on the deaths of
additional religious figures. Nonsense accumulators.

# Game 7 -- Bricks without straw

> "And Pharaoh commanded the same day the taskmasters of the people, and
> their officers, saying,
>
> Ye shall no more give the people straw to make brick."
>
> The Bible, King James Version, Exodus 5:6--7 (The Holy Bible)

Projects start out as bets. But there comes a point where crazy long
odds are being heaped on crazy long odds and the project becomes a
"nonsense accumulator." In the joke, Dave bets on the nonsense
accumulator because he's an idiot. People behaving like idiots in jokes
is funny. In real life? It's not so funny.

When you and your team are being asked to deliver something that's
impossible, it can feel like the story in the Bible where Pharaoh asks
the Israelites to make bricks without straw. Which -- as far as I can
grasp with a primitive knowledge of 13^th^ century BCE brick making --
is impossible. Pharaoh has a reason for what he's doing. The Israelites
want their freedom, and he wants them to remain his slaves. And the
Israelites know this. It's a toxic situation, but everybody knows what's
happening and why.

Very often, as a project manager, you're suckered into playing the
"bricks without straw" game, and you have absolutely no idea why.

The DQ program that I mentioned earlier had a whole list of "bricks
without straw" problems. And like almost all projects, it had an
"aggressive" deadline.

But the teams on the program were expected to meet that aggressive
deadline (bet #1) using extremely poor internet access (bet #2). The
team was supposed to demonstrate working software on a live server. But
it had yet to get access to the test and then the live servers (bet #3).
It seemed that every step on the way to getting code to the live server
was blocked.

All code had to pass through an automatic code checker. The code checker
had a default set of rules, some of which didn't apply to the code that
the team was writing. As a result, the code checker would never "go
green" (bet #4). It would never indicate that the code was OK to be
moved to the next stage. We needed to change the file that contained the
rules. But we couldn't find anyone with authority to make the change.

The deadlines were aggressive. When are deadlines ever anything else? So
(if we could get code through the code checker), we needed to start
getting code onto the test servers. Then we needed to start working out
a path to promote it to the live servers. Doing this would allow the
exploration that we've already talked about in (Chapter ESCAPE).

The ops team, the people who were responsible for the live servers,
didn't appear to have any experience of working using an iterative and
incremental approach (bet #5). This ops team was used to having an
application delivered to it as one big chunk of code or as change
requests accompanied by supporting documentation. A *lot* of supporting
documentation. According to the ops team, each change we made was going
to require 22 separate documents.

At one point, the suggestion was that these documents would be required
for every "user story." That is, for every small change in
functionality.

We were stuck.

We weren't going to meet the "aggressive" deadline.

We couldn't even get off the starting line.

We reported these issues as loudly and as widely as we could.

Apart from the wi-fi wall of shame, we made almost no progress.

And so, like the Israelites with Pharaoh, we were stuck playing a game
of "bricks without straw." Being asked to do something that we couldn't
do. But unlike the Israelites and Pharoah, we didn't know why these
obstacles were being put in our path. We didn't know why extra nonsense
bets were being added to the accumulator.

Of course, we used empirical processes, transparency, inspection and
adaptation. And we tried to make these multiple obstacles clear to the
people who we thought needed to hear. We tried to get through to senior
stakeholders.

And that's when we encountered the baby seal and his eagerness to
discuss spoons.

# Game 8 -- The baby seal and the big bad dog

> Garry: "And now for the first item on the agenda: spoons."
>
> Bev: "Really? That's the most important thing for us to talk about?
> You're going to talk about spoons?"
>
> Garry: "Well, it was on the agenda last week and I've got an update."
>
> Bev: \[Angrily rising from the table\] "I can't believe this, I'm
> leaving."

This was a scene that played out one morning on the DQ program. The
project managers had spent several months trying to communicate to
senior people about our "bricks without straw" problems. The response of
the project's bosses had been to appoint a "senior project manager" and
to tell us that we should communicate all our problems to him.

I sighed. I was every bit as furious and frustrated as Bev, and it could
have just as easily been me who lost my temper. The program lead had
given us a baby seal.

What's a baby seal? I wouldn't have known if I hadn't experienced it on
another project. This was the project where Mr Two gave the famous
presentation that we talked about in (Chapter CAR.)

Let's say you're on a project and the news is bad, the news is very bad.
The people charged with delivering the project, the project managers,
are starting to shout quite loudly. They are behaving like Bev. They're
trying to communicate to senior management that the news is bad. The
response of senior management is to stop listening.

But the way that they stop listening is quite sophisticated. They
appoint someone -- often with an important sounding title, such as
"senior project manager." Then they say, "all communication needs to go
through the senior project manager." This is a strategy that you might
remember was used with Mr. Two, who we discussed in (Chapter CAR).

Here's the thing. The "senior project manager" is the gentlest,
mildest-mannered person that you're ever going to meet. They have big,
cute eyes and a gentle expression. Like a baby seal. You can't club the
baby seal! Look at those big, cute eyes! The baby seal is completely
conflict-averse. They are all soft edges. Arguing with them is like
shouting at fog or punching pocket lint. They're never going to tell the
senior stakeholders that there are problems with the project. The entire
reason that they are there is to prevent that from happening.

Of course, some senior managers don't have the finesse that's required
to appoint a baby seal. Rather than finding a gentle soul to completely
muffle the sounds of trouble, they buy a big aggressive dog. At the
slightest sniff of bad news, the dog growls and snarls and even tries to
bite. The dog makes anyone who tries it regret their attempts to tell
the truth.

Weirdly, in my experience, this is a less "successful" strategy than the
baby seal for preventing the reporting of bad news. It doesn't stop news
getting through as effectively. There are only so many times that
someone can threaten to fire you. And the big bad dog is unlikely to
have the authority to fire you. If they manage to arrange that, too many
questions get asked. Even "Mr. Two" from (Chapter CAR) didn't get fired.

You might suggest that this is just another version of the "hiding hand"
that we talked about in game number three (Chapter BET). But the real
game now is to get past the seal or the dog. This can be a Kafkaesque
experience.

But here's the important thing. There's an opportunity here. Just like
there is in game five -- shortening the odds. And in game six -- upping
the bet (Chapter BET). Here is a point where you *can* make a
difference.

Behind the silent conspiracy of the hiding hand, the bells and whistles
of project management theater, beyond the gentle honking of the baby
seal or the snarling of the attack dogs, there's the truth. Or at least
your version of it, your team's version of it. There's a crazy bet that
the project could stop making.

It's important that you are clear that the game that you're playing, the
problem you're solving, is getting to this truth.

This is not for its own sake, but so that you can use it to make the
project more likely to succeed and less of a crazy bet. And when you're
clear about this, there are some relatively straightforward things that
you can do.

There's an obvious solution to the nonsense accumulator -- don't bet on
the poor old Arch Bish of C to die! There's an obvious solution to the
"bricks without straw" problem. The clue is in the problem.\
\
Straw!\
\
And for many projects there's very often a similar, obvious solution, or
several obvious solutions.

Get access to the live servers.

Do some user research.

Plug in the wi-fi.

These things are things that we've already discussed in the previous
chapter (Chapter BET). They are ways of working to bring down the odds
on the nonsense accumulator until they get nearer to a sensible bet,
*even if that bet is a long shot*.

Your mission, should you choose to accept it, is to make these crazy
bets and these "bricks without straw" problems clear to the people who
need to know about them. And that will probably mean figuring out how to
bypass the baby seals and the big bad dogs. That's why I was behaving so
strangely when I created the wi-fi wall of shame. I was trying to get
around the baby seal.

The way I've just described this task, it can sound overwhelming. And
it's worth understanding that when we are in this situation, we're
literally facing *impossible odds.* What we're trying to do is find one
bit of the nonsense accumulator and remove that bet. We're trying to
improve the odds of the project to the point where it's just a long
shot. We're trying to change impossible odds into possible odds.

# Wrestling the bull

I don't remember where I heard this story. A journalist is interviewing
a bullfighter. The journalist asks the bullfighter if he has a rigorous
regime of physical workouts, running, weights and cardio to make sure
that he's fit enough to fight the bull. The bullfighter smiles*.*

> *"I think you misunderstand. I do not wrestle the bull."*

My point is that fighting with seals and dogs, making nonsense
accumulators into sensible bets, this might sound overwhelming. But
there is a little bit of good news. You don't have to deal with all the
craziness at once. You don't have to wrestle the bull.

The matador has a team that makes several passes to weaken the bull
before, finally he (or she, there are female matadors) tries to kill it
as cleanly as possible. The bull has already been weakened and wounded.
There's no wrestling involved.

And it's these earlier passes, weakening the "big angry bull" of the
nonsense bet, slowly changing it into something that can be managed,
that are the ones we need to look for.

To move away from a brutally cruel way of seeing, which I slightly
regret mentioning, the lesson of the matador is that there are other
ways of slaying this enormous, enraged beast. Maybe we can get help.
Maybe we can prepare the scene. We can ask ourselves, which crazy bets
can we stop the project from making? At this point I'm going to draw
from two ideas from "Lean" thinking -- the approach behind the success
of Toyota that we talked about in (Chapter STREAMS). These are two
connected ideas.\
\
*"What is it?"* and *the "Theory of constraints."*

# What is it?

In his book "The Toyota Production System," Taiichi Ohno (Ohno) says
that one should always be asking oneself, "What is it?" What is the real
problem that you are solving? If you're making cars, are you really
making cars? Is that really the problem you're solving? Let's say you
decide the problem you're really solving is making cars *at the rate of
need* -- rather than just making cars. This was one of the most
important sophistications that Toyota came to understand. And from it
came ideas that changed the world, now known as Lean thinking and
just-in-time manufacturing (Womack).

Changing the description of the problem means that Toyota ended up with
a very different, and much more elegant, solution. This involves the
notion of parts being delivered "just in time." It involves focusing
management thinking on maintaining a flow of cars at a steady pace
through the production line.

And this might be a useful question to ask ourselves when we're on a
project.

What is the "real" problem that we're solving?

We thought we were in the business of managing software development. But
really, are we in the business of "publicizing wi-fi connectivity
problems?" Or are we in the business of avoiding embarrassing the
company that couldn't provide servers and hired ops people who don't
understand iterative development?

Are we in the business of negotiating a route to putting software on a
live server that doesn't require a sheaf of 22 documents for every
change? Do we need to be diplomatic? Or do we need to be the opposite of
diplomatic? Do we need to shout our problems out loud? Should we
literally paste them all over the walls? Or do we need to be creative to
find a solution that meets multiple constraints? The answer of course is
that it depends. It depends on what's the most pressing problem and
what's the best way of addressing it.

# The theory of constraints

And that brings us to the second concept that we can borrow from Lean --
the theory of constraints -- ably explained in his book "The Goal" by
Eli Goldratt (Goldratt). What is the most pressing problem? The theory
of constraints states that if a number of processes are arranged
linearly, the output from one process is the input into the next
process. There is only one point at any given time which is the limiting
factor. There will be one point along the process flow that is currently
the bottleneck. This is the point that we can precisely and surgically
attack. We don't need to wrestle the bull. We can strangle it.

On the DQ project, there was an obvious constraint. Before we could do
*anything*, we needed fast and reliable wi-fi. Without that, we couldn't
do anything. As one of my team vividly put it,

*"to make internetz you need internetz."*

Creating a "wi-fi" wall of shame worked. I don't know who talked to
whom, but it worked. The wi-fi got better. This allowed us to focus on
the code checking program, and then when we got the rules for *that*
changed, this revealed, in its full awfulness, the dire situation with
provision of servers.

And here we see both the good news and the bad news. Solving the problem
that is the current constraint works to shorten the odds on the nonsense
accumulator. But of course, this just means that something else has now
become the constraint. The problem has moved somewhere else. And now it
may be insoluble, or very difficult to locate. Yes, you've made progress
with one crazy bet. And the only way to save the project is to do that.
But that doesn't mean you'll be able to have any effect on the next one.

One of the ways that you could see my struggles on the DQ project is as
a series of attempts to pull the constraint -- the thing that limited
the speed of development and progress -- into our team where we could
control it.

Sorting out the issues with the wi-fi and the code checking sent the
constraint somewhere we couldn't see it.

Problems with provision of servers didn't seem to be getting any better.
Discussions around streamlining the process to allow release of code to
these servers, should they ever appear, had ground to a halt. At a
quarterly "all hands" meeting, Bev and I counted three actual LIES that
were told by senior management.

And, to quote a famous phrase often used by the News of the World
tabloid newspaper when covering saucy topics like orgies and brothels --
that is the point where "I made my excuses and left." The DQ project was
a nonsense accumulator, and I'd had enough of their nonsense.

# Game 9 -- Should you stay or should you go?

> \"Let go, or be dragged.\" -- Zen proverb

Your project isn't a brave, adventurous bet. It's a nonsense
accumulator. It has been made impossible because of not just one, but
several "bricks without straw" problems. You've done your best to call
out these problems. And that's resulted in the installation of a baby
seal who refuses to engage with the real issues and makes sure that
senior people only hear good news. Or a big, aggressive dog has been set
to guard over you. Whenever you try to broach the difficult issues that
the project is facing, he snarls, barks and threatens to bite until you
stop.

So, what now? The promise of this book is that, if we can look at
projects in the right way, we can vastly improve their chances of
success. Sometimes projects *are* impossible. They can't be delivered.
They combine just too many crazy bets.

OK, maybe we *can* locate the current constraint. Maybe we can help the
project to stop making at least one crazy bet. Maybe we can turn it into
a possible project. But on some projects, we can't. All the crazy bets
are somewhere beyond our control. The project is going to fail. No one
is going to help us tackle the bull, nobody did anything to weaken it.
It is going to run us over.

And this is where it is important to remember one thing. One very
important aspect of projects and poker is that you can throw in your
hand and walk away. You don't have to stay. Letting go is better than
being dragged. Letting go gives you a chance to find another project,
possibly one with no "bricks without straw" problems, with no nonsense
accumulators. A project with good odds of success that you can work to
improve.\
\
**Game X -- Looking glass poker**

When we end up in a meeting that involves an aggressive argument about
spoons, we might start to ask ourselves some questions. What game are we
playing? Is there a game in which we are being *played*?

Is it a game that we might call *"looking glass poker?"*

After all the games that we've enumerated in this chapter and (Chapter
BET), we could call this game 10. But we might also, with a nod to Roman
numerals, call it game X. It's not just another game. It's more of a
mystery.

As we've already seen in (Chapter CAR), sometimes it's instructive to
think about how the analogies, the metaphors, the ways of seeing that
we're using *don't* fit, rather than the way that they do. If project
management is like poker, it's very strange poker.

The people who are making the bets -- the project's sponsors -- aren't
at the table. The people who are at the table playing the hands (the
development teams) aren't making the bets. The sponsors are dependent on
the development team to tell them how things are going, whether they are
winning or losing, whether they need to up their stakes to have any
chance of winning, or whether they need to adjust their expectations and
accept that the payoff won't be anywhere near as big as they hoped.

Of course, this dependence on the dev team causes large amounts of
resentment, fear and distrust among the sponsors. But before we feel too
sorry for the sponsors, we might remember that in many projects they are
asking the dev team to play with the lights out! Without cards!

Things get even crazier when we realize that any money that the team
does win won't be shared around in bonuses, won't even go back to the
company, but will be instantly placed on a bet on the death of the
Archbishop of Canterbury or some other nonsense accumulator-type bet.

Projects get crazy. Projects get crazy like this. Because projects are
multiple gambles, multiple games that are going on simultaneously. Some
of those games are being played using us as chips! We're working on a
project and the project is a bet, the result of someone saying "f\*\*k
it!" We're a pawn in that game. Sometimes the only way we can control
what's going on is by walking away.

# Conclusion

This chapter and the previous one (Chapter BET) are clearly closely
connected. They are two sides of the same "heads I win, tails you lose"
coin. The previous chapter is about projects that are bets. This chapter
is about things so risky that they aren't bets. They are dead certs.
Sure things. Certain failures.

In the previous chapter (Chapter BET), I argued that all projects are
bets. There wouldn't be any projects unless at some point someone,
somewhere decided to accept the non-zero odds of the project failing and
say "f\*\*k it!".

For your health, success and sanity as a project manager, it's important
to understand what that means. There is always a non-zero chance that
the project will fail. And *it won't be your fault.*

Either explicitly or implicitly, almost all the people involved in
projects know this. As a result, they play a bunch of games. The people
who made the bet play a game where they try to be around when the
project is going well and paying off, and try to make sure they're not
around when things are going badly. We call this game *HIWTYL* (heads I
win, tails you lose).

The people who made the bet *and* the people who are doing the work
conspire together to hide problems that would stop the project
happening. A magical *hiding hand* keeps projects going when they
probably should be canceled. This is because the bosses want to make the
bet and the workers want work.

And everybody pretends that if only we follow a certain project
management method in exactly the right way and everyone involved is
properly "qualified," the project is guaranteed to be a success. We use
*project management theater* to create the impression that even the
craziest projects might succeed if only we can conduct our stand-up
meetings correctly and word our user stories the right way.

As a project manager, you can rail against all of this as much as you
want. But you're very unlikely to change how any of it works.

What you *can* do is try to *shorten the odds* of the bet. How? Through
the twin tactics of *user research and frequent delivery* of working
software into the hands of users. By doing this you will start to
understand the real odds of the project and its likely payoff.

Sometimes the payoff will be way more than expected. Sometimes there
won't be *any payoff*. It's a bet, remember. There's a non-zero chance
that it will fail all by itself.

*Sometimes it will become obvious that the only way to make the project
payoff is to "up the stakes."*

It's your job (along with the product owner) to spot these changes in
the odds and communicate the options. You need to offer the project's
stakeholders the chance to change their bet or fold, stop -- give up.

Ok so these are the ways as a project manager that you can help the
project: user research, working software, communication of changes in
the odds. Sounds relatively simple, no?

No.

Because, for reasons that stakeholders mostly keep to themselves, they
often turn reasonable long-shot bets into *"nonsense accumulators."*
Nearly every project combines the bet that the software will be valuable
and useful and the bet that it can be delivered on time. But often these
reasonable bets are combined with other bets that are just silly. Bets
that substantially increase the chances of failure without increasing
the payoff. The bet that the project can be done without talking to
users. The bet that it can be done without the necessary tech resources,
or without the necessary legal permissions. Or without wi-fi!

When this happens, the combined odds of the project skyrocket. The
chances of any success approach zero. It's like the biblical story of
the Israelites being asked to make *bricks without straw.* It often
feels that, like Pharoah - someone in charge - is deliberately trying to
make the project fail.

And that's what we've talked about in this chapter. Sometimes projects
aren't bets. Sometimes they're just surefire failures.

Then your job as a project manager is to communicate that the odds got
crazy. The odds went from steep to impossible.

You're being asked to deliver this software without talking to users.

You're being asked to deliver this software without servers to test it
on. Without live servers to release it to.

You're being asked to make internetz without internetz.

Often the people who are asking you to deliver bricks without straw will
try to stop you telling them that that's what they're asking for.
They'll put a *baby seal* in between you and them, so no bad news will
reach them. Or they'll put a *big, bad dog* in the way -- one that barks
and growls until you shut up.

Now it's your job to somehow get around these weird beasts. But in the
end, it's also your responsibility to yourself to spot when you can't do
that. Sometimes the sponsors of the project don't agree that they need
to up the stakes to give the project any chance of succeeding.
Sometimes, you just can't fix the nonsense accumulator. You can't find
any straw for the bricks.

Now something else is at risk -- *your* reputation, your physical health
and maybe your sanity. Like all good poker players, you need to decide
*when to stay and when to go.* You need to know when to throw down your
cards and sprint for the door. Like the euphemistic News of the World
reporters, you need to know when to make your excuses and leave the
brothel.

There is a lot going on. Whatever position we're playing in the project
-- project sponsor, project manager, team member -- the more we look at
it, the more we see that this isn't really like real poker. And it isn't
much like betting on a horse. It's poker as described by a surrealist
like Lewis Carroll. It's "looking glass poker", where the people who
make the bets don't see the cards and the people who see the cards don't
make the bets. For reasons rarely explained, the people who are making
the bets sometimes seem to be working hard to make sure those bets never
pay off or, as in the joke we discussed, they hook the outcome of the
project onto a bet that several religious leaders will pop their
ceremonial clogs.

OK. So, we've sketched how complex, risky and bewildering the bets of
projects and the games being played can get. How would you feel now
about making a commitment to deliver it on a particular day, at a
particular cost with a particular set of functions? Could you *ever*
make a sensible commitment -- or a promise -- in these kinds of
circumstances? Should you do that? If you can't do that, what should you
do? That's what we'll talk about next in (Chapter COMMIT).
**Part 2 -- Unhelpful ways of seeing**

There are some ways of looking at projects which are deeply unhelpful.
If they aren't countered, they will result in serious problems for a
project. Unfortunately, these are often regarded as the most "intuitive"
ways of seeing a project.

The next chapter is chapter COMMIT. Almost everybody sees project
management as about keeping promises and honoring commitments. What gets
lost in this way of seeing is that a project also needs to be valuable.
And each project's values will be unique. The project's values are a set
of things that are distinct from being "on time" and "to budget." If we
can take a step back from the pressure that we feel from commitment and
consistency, sometimes we can see what these values are. We can see what
a project is really about. And then we can give ourselves the time and
space to deliver something valuable on a commitment that often seems
impossible.

After chapter COMMIT comes chapter ALL. This is the most complex chapter
in the book. For some readers, it might also be the most powerful. It
explains why there is so much focus on doing "everything" and "exactly
what it says on the tin" in project management and why that causes so
much trouble. Then it explains how doing the opposite -- delivering
something that is *not* an exact match -- is the successful way to
deliver in seemingly impossible circumstances.

Like the final level in many video games, the final chapter is Chapter
BOSS. We try to look at a project from the boss's point of view. We
explain the strong temptation to see a project as being about getting
everything right and making no mistakes. When things go wrong, bosses
can see this as embarrassing and problematic -- bad news. This chapter
encourages bosses to see "bad news" as what it is: data. This is data
that a boss needs a team to make sense of. It's data that can be
structured into information. And then that information can lead to new
knowledge. And that new knowledge can be used to make the project more
successful than was ever anticipated.

Chapter COMMIT -- commitment and consistency --project management as
keeping a promise

> "A foolish consistency is the hobgoblin of little minds."
>
> Ralph Waldo Emerson

We want our actions to match what we say. We want to be consistent. We
want to keep our promises and honor our commitments. But when we're
doing something complex like project management, we need to understand
what that means. If we don't, we do a bad job. We disappoint the
project's owners and users. And we make ourselves feel bad in the
process. We need to understand how commitment and consistency work. Then
we can deliver a project that's valuable to its owners and its users,
even if it isn't what was initially promised.

# I'm telling you that you have to commit

I thought to myself, "These are exactly the kinds of situations where
you're not supposed to get emotional."

I was getting emotional. My mouth was dry. I tried to swallow to talk.
Johnny was talking.

> "I'm telling you that you have to commit to being live by the end of
> the month."

It was the Cash project. And Johnny was the CEO. He was giving me a
direct order. I knew we wouldn't be ready by the end of the month.

We didn't have control over some things that had to happen before we
could go live. Legal would have to take a last look. They always took a
month. And my experience was there were always other new things that
popped up. Sure, the software might be ready. That didn't mean the
project would be ready.

*"I'm sorry, I can't promise we'll be live by the end of the month."*

*"I'm telling you that you have to commit to that."*

*"I'm sorry, I can't."*

*"Well, what I'm **going** to hear is that we **are** going to be ready
by the end of the month."*

He was very angry. I could tell. Maybe his mouth was as dry as mine.

We weren't ready by the end of the month. Legal held us up. And then
another thing that we weren't expecting.

What was really going on? My boss had a meeting with his board of
directors at the end of the month. He wanted to be able to tell them the
project was live. I knew it wouldn't be. He probably knew this as well.
He was angry because I was refusing to do what he thought was the main
thing a project manager should do. I was refusing to make a promise. I
was refusing to commit to a date.

# Isn't project management about keeping promises?

I run project management training courses. At the start of the first
day, I do an exercise.

People get into groups. I give each group a pad of flipchart paper and
some pens and ask them to draw a "stick person." I ask them to give that
person a happy face. And then I ask them to write down words and phrases
that they associate with a "happy" project. That's a project that is
going well.

Almost always, *the first thing* written down is:

> *"On time and to budget."*

Do you know what's almost *never* written down? I might have seen it
twice in ten years. "Valuable to its customers." Do you know what's
written even less often? I\'ve seen it once in those ten years. "Makes
money." Yes, "to budget" -- restricting spending money -- that gets
written down all the time. But making money? Nobody thinks of that!

This exercise shows something project managers already know:

> Project management is about keeping promises.

People think it's about being on time and keeping to budget. They almost
always forget that to be a success, *the project must be valuable to
someone.* Maybe it could even make money!

Focusing on a measure of performance which isn't the *real* measure of
performance is a recognized psychological flaw. It's called surrogation
(Black). This is what people are doing when they think about "on time"
and "to budget." The values and benefits of a project are difficult
things to assess or track. So, the promises of budget and time become a
surrogate.

> When people talk about project management, they think about "on time"
> and "to budget." And that means keeping a promise.

# The beautiful overcoat

The writer Alan Bennett (Bennett) tells a story about his mother -- Mam.
A female friend of the family got a job in the offices of the publisher
Faber and Faber. She later married one of the editors, a successful
poet.

Mam meets this woman on the street in Leeds. The woman is with her poet
husband. Mam describes the gentleman to Bennett. A well-dressed man with
an American accent. Bennett realizes that Mam has just met T. S. Eliot!
One of the world's most famous poets.

He explains to Mam that the man she met is a very famous poet who won a
Nobel prize for literature.

Mam says:

*"I'm not surprised. It was a beautiful overcoat."*\
\
The joke here of course is the idea that the quality of T.S. Eliot's
overcoat could have anything to do with him winning a Nobel prize!

What Mam is doing is using quality of clothes as a surrogate. She
doesn't understand the quality of poetry. But she knows about overcoats.

We all do this with things that we don't understand. If we are shopping
for a used car, should we care about the cleanliness of the interiors?
Surely what matters is what's under the bonnet? Cleanliness of the
interior isn't a good guide to mechanical condition. Just as the quality
of overcoat is no guide to the quality of poet.

So why do used car showrooms put so much emphasis on maintaining the
cleanliness of their cars' interiors?

Because evaluating the technical stuff is hard. It is beyond most
people's abilities. That doesn't mean they don't know that it's
valuable. It does mean that most people don't know how to *evaluate*
it*.* Cleanliness of seats and carpets acts as a surrogate.

On time and to budget, in a project, is being used in the same way as
the "lovely overcoat" on the poet and the clean carpets in a used car.
It's being used as a surrogate.

Focusing on commitments around time and budget *seems* clear and
effective. It's efficient in terms of time taken and thinking required.

And for these reasons, we are not going to stop bosses trying to extract
commitments. A lot of the time, those commitments will be to deliver for
a deadline. But if we're going to have any chance of delivering things
that are valuable, we can't leave it at that. We need to understand the
power of commitment and consistency. But we also need to understand its
dangers, limitations and possible misuses.

# The power of commitment and consistency

The title of this book is "Delivering the Impossible." So, here are some
seemingly impossible tasks. "Commitment and consistency" is so powerful
that it has been used to deliver all of them.

1.  You're in marketing for the Big Toy Company. Your task is to
    manipulate parents. They've spent a fortune on toys at Christmas.
    You need to get them out again in January to buy even more toys.

2.  You're the donations manager for the Good Deeds Charity. It's your
    job to get people to volunteer more time to collect for your
    charity.

3.  You're a Chinese Red Army interrogator. You're fighting the Korean
    War. Orders are to get American prisoners of war to publicly
    criticize their country. Then they must praise the Communist regime.

4.  You're in the marketing department of the YUMEE breakfast cereal
    company. You have to get new customers and keep existing customers
    loyal to your brand.

5.  You're the director of the Department of Forms. Your career and
    bonus depend on delivering the new forms system by the end of the
    year. You know the deadline is "ambitious" because there's about
    three times as much work as there is time to do it.

Some of these things sound more impossible than others. But there is a
principle of human behavior that can be used to achieve these tasks. It
can be ruthlessly exploited to turn these impossible tasks into possible
ones. This is the principle of *commitment and consistency.*

How do you get people to volunteer more time to charity? Two weeks
before the phone call where you ask them to volunteer, you make another
call. You ask a simple question: "Do you think charity is a good thing?"
Most people say "yes!" But once someone says out loud they think charity
is good, something changes. They've made a statement. Now they need to
be consistent with it. They're far more likely to be consistent with
their own words. They're more likely to volunteer when asked in a
follow-up call.

How do you make parents spend money on toys in January? If you're the
Big Toy Company, you can hype a particular toy -- a robot dog. You run
adverts and product placements before Christmas. Kids ask for it. Kids
are promised it.

Then in gift-buying season you engineer a shortage of robot dogs. The
parents are stuck. They *have to* buy their kids another Big Toy Company
toy for Christmas.

In January parents have to go out again and buy the robot dog! They
promised. And who wants to be the parent who doesn't keep the promises
they make to their children?

Robert Cialdini wrote a book on how salespeople and others manipulate us
into doing things we don't want to do (Cialdini). They use common
principles of human behavior to do this. He calls these people
"compliance professionals."

How do you get people to buy and then keep buying your product? Run a
competition. Ask the entrants to write why they love your product. Ask
them to do it *in their own handwriting.* This is a quirk of the
principle of commitment and consistency. If people can be made to say
things or write them down, they convince themselves they believe them.

How do you get American soldiers who are being held in a Chinese
prisoner of war (POW) camp to criticize their own country? You use very
similar tactics. And we'll talk about them later in this chapter.

But first, let's take a moment to understand why this principle is so
compelling. And then let's understand why this compulsion is a problem
for project management.

# The origins of commitment and consistency

As human beings, we like to keep our promises. When we say we're going
to do something, we like to honor the commitment. Also, we want to
appear consistent. If we tell people that we're vegetarian, we don\'t
want to be caught eating a chicken burger the size of our head.

This is, of course, mostly a good thing. It's a good thing when people
do what they say they\'re going to do. It's a good thing when we behave
in a way that fits with what we say we believe. But why?

Why were commitment and consistency so important in earlier human
societies? We can offer an explanation in terms of evolutionary
psychology. We can't say exactly what happened. But we can imagine that
when social groups were small, being unreliable or unpredictable would
have been bad. Certainly, being *caught* would be bad. Keeping your
promises made it more likely that you would survive. This made it more
likely you'd be in a society where you could have children.

This is the basic idea of evolution. The traits that are advantageous
from the point of view of having children get passed on. We can imagine
that people who were seen to be noncommittal and inconsistent were
shunned.

When we think of evolutionary explanations, we don't think of marketing
departments. We don't think about toy robot dogs. But the oldest objects
found that are thought to have been toys are more than 10,000 years old.

Maybe there was a brief blissful period of a few thousand years. It was
a time before marketing departments. Before people were working day and
night to manipulate our promises. Maybe.

# Project management is an inherently uncertain business

"Commitment and consistency" is a problem for software development
projects. This is because software development is an inherently
uncertain business. We can understand why by revisiting three ways of
seeing projects we've already discussed: streams (Chapter STREAM),
swamps (Chapter SWAMP) and bets (Chapter BET).

**Streams.** Software development project management isn't about
delivering value that we already understand. It's about *discovering*
value. It's not about working existing value streams. It's about
constructing new value streams. Finding new value is an inherently
uncertain process. Making promises and commitments about what we'll find
and when is very difficult. Actually, making the promises is easy --
it's delivering on them that's difficult.

**Swamps**. Project management doesn't happen in simple environments. It
happens in complex environments. It happens in environments where we
don't fully understand what's going on. It's possible that these are
(for us) unknowable environments. Perhaps too much is happening and
changing too quickly. Perhaps a lot of what is going on is being
deliberately hidden from us. What this means is that we're hardly ever
in a good position to promise anything.

**Bets**. Projects are bets. As a leader, it's part of your job, at
least sometimes, to shrug your shoulders and say "f\*\*k it!" And then
you need to cover up what you've just done. Then you need to pretend it
isn't a bet at all. What does that mean for anybody trying to deliver
that bet for you? How does that fit with the idea that we should be
consistent with what we say and be ready to honor our commitments? It
doesn't!

Bearing all this in mind, why on earth would we ever be tempted to make
*any* commitment? And knowing what we know, why would we then try to be
consistent about a commitment? Why would we feel bad about not honoring
the ones that we have made?

The reality is that we have to make commitments. Because if we don't
make them, we don't get work. And that means that most of software
development project management happens after we've made a commitment. A
commitment that we know we probably shouldn't have made and can't keep.

# Commitments R Us

Remember the warm-up exercise we described at the beginning of the
chapter? What do people write down when asked to describe a project
which is going well? They write down "on time" and "to budget." We're in
a business where people ***expect*** commitments. If we're not prepared
to make commitments, we're not likely to get much business.

Even if, as a jaded and grizzled project manager, you know that making
any kind of commitment is a bad idea. Even if you know there's a poor
chance of keeping any commitments because of streams, swamps and bets,
you still end up making them. Because if you don't, either the project
doesn't happen, or it doesn't get given to you and your team, company or
organization.

Earlier in this chapter we discussed five impossible tasks:

1.  Sell toys straight after Christmas.

```{=html}
<!-- -->
```
6.  Persuade volunteers to collect money for charity.

7.  Get American prisoners of war to criticize their own country and
    government.

8.  Make sure that people keep buying your cereal.

9.  Deliver an online form-filling project, even though there is more
    than three times more work than can be done in the time.

One of these manipulations using commitment and consistency involved me.
I was the sucker.

> Dear reader, I worked in that Department of Forms.

How did the director of that project get me and my team to commit to a
deadline? He simply announced that was the deadline, and that *he'd*
committed to it!

There's a strong irony here. "Commitment and consistency" is supposedly
about being the kind of person who is honest, decent and fair. But
people who are manipulating us into making commitments often aren't
being fair.

Even at the breakfast table we can be manipulated using commitment and
consistency. That's unfair. We're hardly awake. We read the details of a
competition on the back of the cereal packet. We enter the competition.
We write a slogan in our own handwriting saying why we like YUMEE
breakfast cereal. I doubt anywhere we will find a warning that says:

> "Warning! You are potentially becoming the victim of powerful mind
> control techniques. Coming up with a reason for eating YUMEE Sweetie
> Sweet Choco Flakes and writing it in your own handwriting may result
> in your manipulation."

But here's the difference between the manipulation that I fell foul of
and the others in this list. Delivering three times more work than is
possible in the time simply isn't possible. But me and my team had
somehow been maneuvered into "promising" it. So how did we go about
delivering it?

Well, we didn't deliver an impossible thing, because that would have
been impossible. Rather, we delivered a *possible* thing. We found the
third of that work that absolutely had to be delivered for the deadline.
We found out what was really needed rather than what was notionally
promised. And this is a theme we'll explore in the rest of this chapter.
But it wasn't a pleasant experience. We'd been the victim of what Robert
Cialdini calls "commitment and consistency Jujitsu."

# Commitment and consistency Jujitsu

Cialdini calls them "compliance professionals." They are the people who
use the principles of human behavior against us. They use things such as
commitment and consistency to get us to do what *they* want us to do.

And they aren't playing fair. They are manipulating people. Sometimes
this is for commercial reasons. Sometimes it is for ideological reasons.
Either way, they're messing with us. They are using ***"commitment and
consistency Jujitsu."***

Why were the Chinese so motivated to get American prisoners of war to
criticize their own country? Well, part of it was that the Americans
weren't fighting exactly fair either. They were claiming that soldiers
from China and Korea didn't need to be returned to their homes when the
war ended. Why would they want to go back to Communism? They could stay
in the United States and be "free."

This was part of the reason why the Chinese were so desperate to get US
soldiers to say good things about China. And of course, the sound of
American soldiers on the radio reciting the faults of their homeland was
powerful. It couldn't help but threaten the morale of American soldiers
who were still fighting.

How did the Chinese do it?

The interrogators would start by asking if the United States was
perfect. Anyone would probably admit there were problems with their home
country. But in this context, this was a huge mistake. Once the soldier
had admitted his country was less than perfect, he would be asked to
elaborate. Initially that would be verbally, but then he would be asked
to write it down in his own handwriting. This is a very serious step in
the wrong direction for the soldier. The interrogator, of course, was an
expert in persuasion. He would use the dynamic of commitment and
consistency to maneuver the soldier to where he wanted him to be.

> "You said this didn't you? Don't you say what you believe? Then why
> would you have a problem writing it down?"

Let's say that you refuse to admit any criticisms. How could the
interrogators make progress? Well, they can ask you to read out *someone
else's* criticisms. Then they ask you to write those criticisms down.

Those aren't your criticisms. But you heard them in your own voice. And
now they're in your handwriting.

You've written them down. Whether these are your thoughts or not. Now
you can see them in your own handwriting.

Remember the competition on the back of the cereal packet? Writing
things down in your own handwriting starts to convince you that you
believe them. Writing down that you like YUMEE Sweetie Sweetie Choco
Flakes has an effect. It has the effect of persuading you that you like
them.

Now, back in the POW camp, the interrogators suggest that you read out
what you've copied down over the public address system. As you read it
out, you see that it's in your own handwriting. At every stage of this
process, the in-built desires of the soldier are being used against
them. They *want* to be consistent with what they've said. They *want*
to honor their commitments. The interrogator is using a powerful human
driver. They're using it to make the prisoner do something that they
absolutely do not want to do. They are triggering powerful forces that
work without conscious thought.

Think you'd be different? Well, this is the conclusion of American
psychologists reviewing returning troops from the Korean War:

> "Nearly all American prisoners in the Chinese camps are said to have
> collaborated with the enemy in one form or another."
>
> (Cialdini)

Thinking that *we'll* be different, that *we'll* be immune to biases, is
just another kind of bias! It's called the bias blind spot (Pronin).

I wish the news were better. But it isn't. The interrogators in the
Korean War were professionals. But perhaps more upsetting is the fact
that crude and amateur attempts still work.

Here are some real-life examples. These are commitment and consistency
tricks. But these are beginner, amateur moves. They still worked on me!
They made me feel bad and have certainly made me and my team behave in
ways that they otherwise wouldn't.

***You committed to A. And embedded inside A, although we didn't know it
at the time, were B, C and D. So, when you committed to A, you also
committed to B, C and D.***

***I committed to it, so you committed to it.***

***Somebody committed to it in Parliament. Because they committed to it,
you committed to it.***

***I am ordering you to commit to this!***

# Mr. Chairman

It was a small company with fewer than a dozen people working on as many
projects. I\'d been brought in as project manager. We were having a
meeting on the phone. We were at the start of a new project.

It was the kind of meeting I did not like. No agenda. Free-form and
rambling.

But it was with the chairman of the company. And this project was his
baby, so I didn\'t feel I could take over and run the meeting.

The meeting ran long, as meetings with no specific goal tend to. But it
looked like we were wrapping up. I thought the meeting was going to stop
when the chairman said "Right!" But then he continued.

"Let's go round the table. I want you to promise the team that your
actions will be done in time for the next meeting."

*Promise*? I could hear the other members of the team in the phone
conference. They were solemnly committing to do their actions. These had
been captured and written down with team members' names against them.
What was I going to do when he got around to me?

I knew that the actions with my name against them were vague. I knew I
could claim they were done by the next meeting -- or give some good
excuse why they weren't. At the same time, I knew something else:

> I knew that some members of the team were committing to things they
> shouldn't commit to.

Either they had no chance of doing them by the next time we met, or they
had no idea if they could do them or not. They weren't committing
because they thought they could do the tasks. They were committing to
avoid a difficult situation. They were being manipulated.

And there were some things I\'d been hearing about Mr. Chairman. About
how he was working with the rest of the team. He worked from home most
of the time and got up at 4 am! I had heard some worrying things. He'd
been getting some of the younger members of the team to have meetings at
5 am. He had also been encouraging people to work through the night to
meet his deadlines.

> "You've committed to doing something! Well, why don't you work through
> the night to make sure it's done?"

This is getting a bit too close to:

> "You wrote down this criticism of your country? Why don't you read it
> out?"

When my turn came, I said,

> "I\'m sorry, I can't commit to doing my actions. I'll do my best. But
> those things are just too uncertain."

I was saying this because it was true. But I was also saying it for the
sake of the other people in the meeting. I wanted to say to them "you
don't have to be bullied into committing to things you're not sure you
can deliver."

What happened? Mr. Chairman put the phone down! He hung up on everybody.

A bunch of things were going on at the same time to make this go so
badly. We'd only just started working together. The chairman wasn't to
know I'd just read a book about commitment and consistency! He wasn't to
know that I regard it as part of my job to make sure the team works at a
sustainable pace. And part of that is making sure that, most of the
time, they work normal office hours.

I think the chairman was using an approach that had worked for him in
the past. He would get people who worked for him to make commitments
that they couldn't honor. Then, when they didn't keep them, they would
be more pliable. More likely to do his bidding because they felt bad
about not being able to honor their impossible commitments. More likely
to work through the night. He was far from being a white belt in
commitment and consistency Jujitsu. And I was trying out my new-found
resistance to commitment and consistency.

It was a powerful lesson for me on the tactics of "compliance
professionals." And it showed me how explosive the results of
countermeasures can be.

At least the chairman was trying to get us to hang ourselves with our
*own* words. He had skills. I've seen many other similar situations
since then. The boss tries to use the power of commitment and
consistency. *Even when no commitment has been made!* What's even more
upsetting is that it often works.

So how do you counter the Chinese Army-style interrogation tactics?

Fortunately, dealing with this level of compliance professional isn't
our job. But we certainly should know enough about the principle of
commitment and consistency to spot more amateurish attempts when they're
being used on us. And we need some strategies for dealing with them.

In the last two chapters, we established that projects are bets. And
part of being involved safely in the bet of a project is knowing that
you can walk away. As soon as you see that there's no chance of the bet
paying off, that's exactly what you should do. And that's exactly what I
did in this situation, in the face of manipulation by Mr. Chairman. I
walked.

When you can see that you've been maneuvered into a promise that you
just can't keep, that's certainly one option. But it isn't the only one.
There are other things you can do.

# Dealing with the train drunk

The martial arts expert Terry Dobson tells a story (Dobson). He's on a
train in Japan and there's a drunk passenger making a complete nuisance
of himself. The American starts to think that, at last, he can use the
martial art that he's been learning. He'll be able to flatten this jerk.
And he'll be justified in doing it.

He's getting quite excited at the prospect. But before he gets up out of
his seat, an old man in a kimono starts talking to the drunk.

He asks the man who's causing trouble what he's been drinking.

The drunk says he's been drinking sake and follows this with some
insults.

The old man ignores the insults. He is enthusiastic on the subject of
sake. He tells the train drunk that he loves to warm up some sake and
then sit with his wife in the garden, sipping it.

The old man is smiling. The drunk's fists start to unclench. The old man
asks if the drunk has a wife. The drunk collapses onto a seat and
confesses that he doesn't have a wife, or any money or any place to
sleep. And he's very ashamed of himself. He starts to sob. The old man
in the kimono comforts him.

Perhaps even more ashamed of himself than the drunk is Terry Dobson.

> "And all of a sudden I felt ashamed. I felt more dirty in my clean
> clothes and my make-this-world-safe-for-democracy righteousness than
> the laborer would ever be."

Not all conflicts are dealt with so deftly. But what we do see in this
example is someone doing a sensible, clever thing. Actually, it's two
sensible, clever things. Maybe the man in the kimono is a martial arts
guru; maybe he's just a man who's tried fighting drunks on trains and
seen how that ends.

> The first clever thing that he's doing is side-stepping his initial
> impulse.

That initial impulse might be to respond to the train drunk with
aggression. This is Terry Dobson's instinct. Or the impulse might be to
respond with fear -- which was probably the response of the rest of the
people on the train. By avoiding that initial reaction, he gives himself
the space to do a second clever thing. Something that is ultimately more
effective and more humane.

> The second clever thing is moving from a potentially damaging conflict
> toward having a chat.

And almost the instant that he does that, our understanding of the
story, and Terry Dobson's view of the situation, completely changes.

# \$10m or the hostage gets it

*"Give us \$10 million or we shoot the hostages."*

*"Promise to deliver by Christmas or you're off the project."*

Both these statements are primitive threats. One is a threat of
violence. The other is a threat of withdrawal of funding combined with
the thumbscrew of a commitment.

Both statements look like they have no winning options.

We don't want the hostages to die. But paying \$10 million will ruin
whoever has to pay it and set a dangerous precedent.

Of course we want to keep the team working on the project, but we also
don't want the chaos of committing to an impossible deadline.

The drunk on the train is implicitly saying something similar.

"Sit tight-lipped while I run drunkenly around the train menacing
commuters or be prepared to fight me."

We don't want to witness this awful drunk bothering people, but we also
don't want to get into a fight. OK, maybe Terry Dobson fancied the
fight.

But the way that the man in the kimono dealt with the drunk gives us a
clue as to how we might act in these impossible situations. The man in
the kimono guesses something. His guess is that *there's a good chance
that this display of drunken bad behavior is about something else.* And
he realizes that the way to find out what is through a conversation.

# A good night out in Port-au-Prince

Chris Voss wrote a book about negotiation called "Never Split the
Difference" (Voss). In it he talks about his experiences negotiating
with kidnappers in, among other places, Haiti. When Voss worked at the
FBI, various political and economic developments made Haiti a hard place
to live. And those hard circumstances lead to a rash of kidnappings. It
