was Voss's job to help the Haitian police to negotiate with these
kidnappers.

Initial demands for money from the kidnappers were sky-high. They were
way beyond the means of the kidnap victims' families. For example, an
initial demand might be \$100,000.

Voss's strategy was to ask open questions. For example: "How do I know
they're OK?" This is a way of making the kidnappers think. And it's also
a way of keeping the conversation going without needing to make any
concessions. Voss would also ask "How do you expect me to do that?" He
would combine this with a strategy to move the ransom toward a price
that he knew the families could pay.

Voss got experience of multiple hostage negotiations in Haiti. And he
noticed something odd. There were far more kidnappings on a Monday
morning. That was the most popular day of the week. And the kidnappers
seemed to get keener and keener to resolve the negotiations by Friday.
What was going on? Why were kidnappers keeping business hours?

Voss's Haitian colleagues explained it to him. The kidnappers really
wanted to go out on a Saturday night. If they got the ransom by Friday,
that's what it would get spent on. This brought Voss to a crucial
realization. The real ransom was just a few thousand dollars -- the
price of a good night out in Haiti.

# That's the deadline and there's no more money!

Let's retreat from the front line of kidnapping in Haiti for a moment
and remember that this is a book about delivering software projects.
What does this mean for projects? It means that keeping the conversation
going and asking open questions is as important, if not more important,
than meeting a particular deadline.

Recently I was on a project that delivered successfully. We were
initially told that there was no room for movement on either the budget,
or the deadline for go live -- it had to be on time and to budget!

Our strategy wasn't to argue with this. But instead, we did other
things. Like the man in the kimono on the train, we side-stepped the
initial emotional reaction to the obvious situation. In his case this
was a menacing drunk. In our case, this was an impossible budget and
deadline.

Then, like the man in the kimono we had a conversation. We talked. What
did we talk about? Well, certainly we talked about the project. I had a
weekly hourly meeting with the guy in charge of the project. The product
manager had another meeting each week with him.

Yes, we chatted about the project and budgets and deadlines. But we also
had a very good idea of where we were, how we were doing in the project.
We arranged the big items that we needed to do on the project from left
to right on a timeline. This allowed us to ask the kind of open
questions that Voss says we should ask.

*"We're here (pointing at the timeline). What do you think we should do
next? How do you think we can meet this deadline?"*

Regular discussions also gave us a far better understanding of the
project. We started to understand the real considerations beyond the
idea of being on time and to budget.

For example, the customer spilled that another supplier, whose opinion
they trusted, had refused to bid for the project. The supplier had told
them that the project couldn't be done for the budget. And they were
right! The final cost of the project was about twice the initial
estimate. The product owner talked to other senior people associated
with the project. The user researchers talked to the people who would
use the software internally in the organization. And we learned that
internally, the organization was nowhere near being ready to start using
the new service. We also learned that there was more money to be spent,
if it was clear that the project could deliver.

The budget? It wasn't the budget. The deadline? It wasn't the deadline.
The commitment? No one could have kept it.

# Black swans

We might be starting to get just a whiff now of how we might deal with
commitment and consistency issues.

Commitment and consistency are powerful human urges. Most people think
that's just what project management is about. But that doesn't
necessarily mean that we're doomed to feel bad or be mistreated because
we promised things that we can't, or don't, deliver.

We can discover the specific details of the project that we're working
on. We can discover what's really going on.

Voss uses a special term for the details that are revealed as part of
the discussions in a negotiation. These are the important details that
move beyond the surface demand and reveal the real values of the
kidnappers. He calls them "black swans."

The real ransom for a kidnapping in Haiti is the cost of a good night
out in Port-au-Prince. This is a "black swan." This is a term that he
takes from the writer Nassim Nicholas Taleb's (Taleb) book "Black Swan."
Although, as Voss points out, the term has been used for several hundred
years. And it's been used to mean something that's either impossible, or
highly unlikely.

There was a point, in the western world, when everyone thought a black
swan was impossible. Then explorers found them in Australia. That must
have been quite a day.

Voss's use of the term is slightly different. By "black swans" he means
the unique characteristics of a situation. Or, as in Haiti, a particular
group of situations.

This is a hugely useful idea for us. Because it gives us a new way of
seeing the whole brutal dance of hostage situations, *and* commitment
and consistency in project management. Because we can see a lot of the
demands for commitment and consistency as something like hostage
situations. Something like "Promise me these things, or your contract
gets it."

And if we can make this shift in our way of seeing, we can get better at
our jobs; we can get better at knowing what we're doing.

Because what we're doing is trying to use the negotiation process to go
behind the initial threats or demands for commitment. We're trying to
use discussions around commitment and consistency as a way of
discovering the "black swans." These are the unique aspects of this
situation that tell us what we really need to deliver.

This is what the man in the kimono was doing when he talked to the train
drunk. He ignored the surface aggression and found out what was really
going on, the "black swan."

# Frankie and Johnny

We started this chapter with me in a tense conversation with Johnny. He
was demanding that I promise a project would be delivered by a certain
date. And, when I refused, he told me that I *had* promised that the
project would be delivered by that date!

But what was *really* going on? What were the real black swans in this
situation? I put them together later. I think there were two. The first
was that Johnny had an upcoming meeting with his board of directors. The
board had given him a moderately large amount of money -- several
million pounds -- nearly two years earlier, and he had nothing yet to
show for it. Most of the delay had been his mistake: he'd fallen a
little too hard for the idea of "move fast and break things."

One of the things he'd broken was the banking laws! There'd been a
six-month delay while he and his top team had made the government
application to be allowed to operate. But here was the black swan. He
wanted to go to the board meeting and make his own commitment. He wanted
to promise them that the site would be live by the new year. And the way
he wanted to get that commitment was to tell them *I* had promised that
the site would be live by the new year.

But there was another black swan. Despite the football table and pool
table and free cereals and fruit for breakfast, despite the cool image,
the company was a very macho work environment. Part of the way that the
machismo revealed itself was in dramatic shows of "strength." The bosses
would show they were in control by ordering a team to work late, or even
all through the night, to meet a deadline. Johnny's main competitor at
the company -- let's call him Frankie -- had managed to ruin or
substantially damage half a dozen projects using this "management
style."

And I knew this. Because I'd worked on a bunch of those projects. They
were filled with bad code, written in the middle of the night. And when
I'd asked for an explanation of why, somewhere in the answer would be a
story about something being done to meet a deadline decreed by Frankie.

Johnny wanted to tell the directors that the project would be done
before the new year. And to do that, he needed me to tell him the
project would be finished by then. He knew as well as I did that Legal
would hold everything up.

But Johnny also wanted to do the whole decreeing the deadline thing.
He'd seen his rival Frankie do it (Frankie and Johnny weren't lovers;
they were bitter rivals) and he wanted to do it too. He wanted to tell
me when the deadline would be. He wanted to see the team working through
the night.

It's my fault that this got handled so badly. I should have investigated
far enough that I understood the "black swan" of the board meeting. And
the deadline decree. I should have resisted the panic at being forced to
commit to something that I clearly couldn't have committed to. And then
I should have asked more questions, to find out what was really going
on.

What I should have done is just say "yes" sure, it will be done before
the new year.

And I should have done it with sufficient seriousness, and with an
adequately painful look. I should have given him his climactic moment. I
should have made a commitment that he knew and I knew neither of us was
going to keep. What kind of promise would that be exactly? To really get
our heads around this we're going to borrow some terminology and invent
our own. We're going to discuss foolish commitments, low-balling and
vranyo promises.

# How to make a promise, when you really, really have to

> *"A foolish consistency is the hobgoblin of little minds."*

This quotation starts this chapter. Cialdini points out the quotation is
often shortened to *"consistency is the hobgoblin..."*

That single word "foolish" points out an important distinction. And it's
one that's lost if we don't use the longer quote. There is good,
sensible non-foolish consistency.

But we're so sensitive to this notion of doing what we say we're going
to do that we get exploited. And when we're being exploited and doing
things we *didn't* want to do, this is the opposite kind of consistency.
This is the *foolish* kind of consistency.

What if we know that we've been forced to promise to deliver something
by a particular date? And we know that this was in the face of high
uncertainty? Wouldn't we be *foolish* to beat ourselves up when it turns
out that we can't deliver? And wouldn't it be even meaner and crazier to
give our team a hard time about not honoring this commitment?

This distinction between "wise" and "foolish" consistency gets us part
of the way to a solution to the problem of being forced into a
commitment.

If you know it's foolish, then you haven't committed to it! It's like
finding yourself reading out objections to living in the United States
if you're a POW. OK, they're in your handwriting. OK, let's not deny the
powerful urge to believe that if you wrote them, you believed them. But
you didn't. This was commitment and consistency Jujitsu. It was a
foolish commitment. You don't have to keep to it. And, nearly always,
the people who made you make this commitment know this.

# Low-balling, Saddam Hussein and vranyo

What should we do when we're being forced to make a foolish commitment?
An idea that we can borrow from Cialdini can get us all the way to
understanding how we need to act in these circumstances. And then an
idea from Soviet Russia can make us, possibly not comfortable with what
we've done, but comfortable with being uncomfortable. Finally, we can
combine that with some wisdom from a very unlikely source, who can give
us an idea of what we are *really* doing when we're making promises
about projects.

Cialdini gives the example of "low-balling." Low-balling, according to
Cialdini, is making a fake commitment to get a real commitment. This is
something that he learned from car salesmen. The salesman makes the
customer an offer he knows is competitive relative to the offers other
dealers are making. The customer sees this is a good offer and goes for
it. But wait! What's this? Turns out that the salesmen has just talked
to his boss! His boss has looked at the contract, and there's some
problem. The salesman forgot that the Carlos Fandango whitewall tires
are £500 extra, as they're no longer on offer.

Of course, the way this whole charade is supposed to work is that the
customer has gone for the deal. Maybe *he's* written something in his
own handwriting! The car salesman has extracted a commitment. Now it's
very difficult for the customer to back out.

For further support for this idea, of making promises that we know we
can't keep, we're going to seek support from, as I said, a very unlikely
source: Saddam Hussein.

> "What is this politics? You say you're going to do one thing, you
> intend to do another, you end up doing quite another thing entirely."

This sounds like an eerily accurate portrait of politics. But also,
possibly, a description of the approach we need to take to commitment
and consistency in project management.

What might leave us with some smattering of comfort for our conscience
is that what we intend to do by reluctantly giving a commitment, even
when we know it to be foolish, is to keep the conversation going.
Because we know this is the only way we can close the deal and give the
customer what they want, need and value rather than what they're asking
for.

And there's a difference between low-balling -- which is making a
dishonest commitment to get a commitment in return -- and what we're
doing. In the low-balling example that Cialdini discusses, we can
imagine that the person buying the car is an innocent party. The
customer really thinks they can get the white wall tires as part of the
deal! But in the kind of foolish commitments that we're expected to
honor in project management, this isn't the case. The people who are
forcing us into these commitments know that they're foolish commitments.
We know that they're foolish commitments. And they know that we know
that they're foolish commitments. But still, on project after project,
we seem to have to make this foolish commitment before we can progress
any further.

This concept of being dishonest in a way that deceives nobody is
something that the Russians have a word for: "vranyo."

In an article for the online magazine "The Conversation," Neil Bermel
Professor of Russian and Slavonic Studies at the University of Sheffield
(Bermel) defines "vranyo" like this:

*"You know I'm lying, and I know that you know, and you know that I know
that you know, but I go ahead with a straight face, and you nod
seriously and take notes."*

This is a situation that we seem to need to get to before we can start
the serious conversations to unearth the "black swans" -- the
fundamental aspects of the project that need to happen for it to have
value.

And this is also important to remember when we are forced into making a
"low-ball" foolish commitment. Unlike the car salesman, we're not making
this fake commitment to bilk our customers. We're doing it to buy us
time to find out what's really going on so that we can deliver something
that's valuable.

# Putting it all together

We've been literally all over the map trying to put together a way of
responding to the challenge of commitment and consistency. From a train
in Japan to a prison camp in Korea, a palace in Bagdad and to Soviet
Russia. And then to those most brutal of locations for ruthless mind
control: the breakfast table and a toy store in our local shopping mall.

You might be thinking,

*"OK, I'll only make non-foolish commitments on my software development
projects."*

But let's be totally clear here. You *can almost never* make non-foolish
commitments about software projects. Any commitment about software
development that you make probably is going to be a bit foolish. And so,
when we make these commitments, it's setting the stage for playing
Saddam Hussein-style political poker. You're saying one thing, but if
you're totally honest, you intend to do another. And you know in your
heart of hearts that you'll end up doing something different from both.

What you'd really love to do when you're tricked into a foolish
commitment is to call it out. Point out the fact that you and your team
have been maneuvered into a foolish commitment. But we know from my
experience with Mr. Chairman that this tends to result in explosions and
irretrievable breakdowns in relationships. Pointing out the foolishness
of some commitments is sometimes OK. It's great as a way of getting rid
of some irritating person who rings you up and asks you if charity is a
good thing. But on a project, there's a danger that it will completely
ruin your relationship with a client.

And so, we're probably going to end up making a "vranyo commitment" What
we might be able to get for our commitment, if we know what we're doing,
is a commitment in return. A commitment to at least listen to us when we
encounter problems and to keep talking to us throughout the project
engagement.

A commitment to a conversation.

Because we know that we're going to need to use the conversation to find
out what's really going on in the project. We're going to use the
conversation to try to discover the project's unique features -- its
black swans. We need to know this project's precise values. Is this a
commitment that is needed to please the board of directors? Is it a
macho "make it so" type of thing? For this project, what is the
equivalent of the price of a good night out in Port-au-Prince?

So, here's part of the good news about the correct attitude to
commitment and consistency. It starts to make itself clear when we've
got over the fact that we might be forced to make foolish commitments
that we know we won't be able to keep. Because what we do need to do is
to find out as much as we can about the project. And that draws on the
practices of empirical process that we've talked about all the way
through the book. Focus on what's really going on. This is what we were
talking about when we discussed streams, swamps, prison escapes and
driving the car. It's what we talked about when we imagined treating
projects as bets. All these ways of seeing can be boiled down to this:
we need to work in a way that explores and understands the world of the
project. We need to be prepared to change what we do in response to what
we see in it.

# Conclusion

It doesn't matter what we say or do: people are going to think that
project management is about being on time and to budget. People are
going to think that project management is about keeping promises.

Keeping promises taps into one of the most powerful human urges: an urge
to honor our commitments and to be consistent with what we've said in
the past.

For project managers this is bad. Because most of the time they
shouldn't -- in all honesty, if honesty were an important thing -- be
committing to *anything*. But very often project managers won't get the
work at all, or the project won't start, unless they've provided some
kind of commitment. And so, nearly always, these are foolish
commitments. We know that they're foolish. And the people who are
forcing us to make them know that they're foolish. And they know that we
know that they're foolish. They are "vranyo" commitments.

Sometimes they're foolish because they're just over-optimistic and
forget the complexities of real-life projects.

Sometimes they're foolish because we've been forced into making promises
we know we can't keep. There's N times as much work as we can possibly
do in the time.

You're going to have to make foolish promises, vranyo commitments.

But we need to understand that that's when the negotiation *starts*.

Everything we do in the project -- user research, pushing to get
software to escape into the hands of users, meeting deadlines and *not*
meeting deadlines --will give you information about the "black swans,"
the unique characteristics of what needs to be delivered to really make
the project a success.

But there are also other tactics that we can take directly from
negotiation. Open questions:

"How can I commit to that?"

"How can I know that Legal won't hold this up for a month?"

"Can you tell me a little more about why you need this by new year?"

The more open and general -- and continuing -- the conversation is, the
more we're moving away from the brutal manipulation of commitment and
consistency. This is becoming the man in the kimono on the train. It's
avoiding being Terry Dobson. It's talking to the drunk rather than
fighting him.

What we are doing is a curious kind of interrogation. We're trying to
get people to tell us things that might simultaneously be good and bad
for them. In the Chris Voss example, he's trying to get the kidnappers
to tell him something that means they won't get the 100,000 dollars they
asked for. But at the same time, they might get the 4,000 dollars that
would give them a wild night out in Port-au-Prince. And that's what they
really want.

In the situation with Johnny, we were trying to unearth the real reason
why he wanted the commitment to a release on a particular date. The real
reason wasn't because he wanted the project to go live on that date. It
was so he could tell his board. He was scared of the board of directors.

And he wanted to decree the deadline so that he could appear to the big
bosses like Frankie -- his colleague and rival. He wanted to be able to
walk up to a team, point at them and tell them that they had to deliver
by a certain date. "Make it so!"

Why does it feel so bad to suggest that we should make commitments that
we know we're not going to keep? Why are we like this about commitment
and consistency? Why does doing what we say we are going to do have such
a powerful hold over us?

It feels so bad for good reasons. There are explanations from evolution
that we've discussed. And there are explanations from ordinary modern
life. We don't want to be known as someone who doesn't turn up when they
say they will. We don't want to think of ourselves as someone who
doesn't keep their word. We want to be the kind of parent who promises a
robot dog and then delivers.

But are there other reasons? Unconsciously, do we really want the world
to be an "exact match" with our ideas? Is this just a lazy reaction? One
that requires less thinking?

For a moment, let's return to that unlikely sage Sadaam Hussein. Let's
just try to think what that "something else entirely" -- the one that
isn't what we said we'd do and isn't what we planned to do -- might be.
It's hard to think about it isn't it? It requires imagination and a lot
of thought. My brow is furrowing just thinking about it as I write this.

Maybe our unconscious thinking is more comfortable with "all," "same"
and "exact match." The thing that Saddam admitted that he'd end up doing
and that we know we'll end up doing isn't going to be a simple thing to
think about. It's going to be "some" of what we said we'd do. It's going
to be different from what we intended to do in other ways. It's going to
be an inexact match.

Thinking in terms of "some," "different" and "inexact match" makes us
feel uncomfortable. We don't know why, maybe we've never thought about
why, but it does.

But what if this "all," "same" and "exact match" way of seeing is
another powerfully *unhelpful* way of seeing? What if wanting to do what
we've said we're going to do is just one example of a bunch of ways of
unhelpful thinking. What if the problem is precisely that we don't give
them much thought?

Spoiler alert -- it *is* precisely the problem. These unconscious ways
of seeing cause us all sorts of trouble. And that's what we'll talk
about in the next chapter (Chapter ALL).
CHAPTER ALL

> "Instant gratification isn't soon enough."
>
> Carrie Fisher

"All," "same," "forever" -- "exact match".

"Some," "different," "for a while" -- "inexact match."

These are two different ways of thinking about the world. Our
unconscious mind favors "all," "same," "forever." It wants projects to
match their initial descriptions. It wants a project to do "exactly what
it says on the biscuit tin." This kind of thinking can make projects
impossible. We need to be able to spot when our unconscious fascination
with all, same, forever and exact match is causing problems. We need to
be clear. Focusing on "some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact
match" is a powerful way of thinking. It's a way to create possible,
deliverable and valuable projects. It's the way to stop worrying so much
about the biscuit tin and deliver delicious biscuits.

# Symmetrical and asymmetrical thinking

Here are some of the things I've been saying in previous chapters:

"All projects are swamps."

"All projects are bets."

"It's always a bad idea to think of project management as about keeping
promises."

Notice the "all," "all" and "always". An important part of how we
understand the world is by generalizing. By drawing the conclusion that
"All cats meow," I don't have to spend too much time checking each
individual cat. And when I hear a "meow," even if I don't know the local
cats, I can draw the conclusion that a cat made the noise. Closely
related to this use of "all" is "same." Let's say I own a large, ginger
cat, Pericles. I fed him in the morning. I can be relatively comfortable
with the idea that it is the same ginger cat that's meowing at me in the
evening. Even though he's trying to look as if he has never been fed.

When we think about time in terms of "all" and "same," we get "forever."
We tend to think about many things in terms of forever, even when it's
obvious that we shouldn't. For example, our favorite pets don't live
forever. For many of us, this is our first introduction to the idea of
mortality -- that *none* of us lives forever.

Death is a difficult idea to comprehend. Especially our own death. Even
now, when we're grown up, the idea that we're alive now but at some
point we won't be is very difficult to accept. A survey in 2021 by Pew
in the USA found that over 60 percent of Americans believed that there
was an afterlife (Pew).

Isn't this a bit strange? If we think about it for just a second, we
realize that pets and people don't live forever. So why would our first
idea about life be that we *do* live forever?

Chilean psychoanalyst Ignacio Matte Blanco (Matte Blanco) offers an
explanation: our unconscious mind thinks in terms of "all," "same" and
"forever."

These thoughts often come to us without effort. But it requires effort
to think carefully. It takes energy to disagree with these unconscious
thoughts. The unconscious thoughts are already there. They're the
tendency that the Pew survey discovered when it comes to believing in
immortality. It's a tendency to think in terms of all, same and forever.

If we can spot these generalizations that we've made unconsciously, we
can challenge them. We can notice that they came to us without any
conscious thinking. We can think more realistically about the world. We
can admit to ourselves that, in the end, we will die. And on careful
examination of the real world, we might notice -- this is a different
cat. Hang on! Am I feeding two cats?

# Symmetry and the unconscious

It is worth looking at the ideas of psychoanalyst Matte Blanco a little
more closely. Because, as project managers, these ideas aren't distant
things that don't concern us. They are ideas that relate directly to the
business of doing things and delivering things -- the main subject of
this book.

Matte Blanco's central idea is that we don't just use one logic when
we're thinking and processing the world. Sigmund Freud (Freud) drew a
distinction between two kinds of mind: conscious and unconscious. Matte
Blanco makes the same distinction. But then he also draws a distinction
between the kind of logic that is favored in conscious and unconscious
thinking. His idea is that:

> "The unconscious is symmetrical in its logic."

And the core features of symmetrical, unconscious logic are "same,"
"all" and "forever." There's a direct consequence of thinking like this.
It is the belief that thoughts about the world and the actual world can
-- and should -- exactly match.

According to Matte Blanco, in the unconscious all relations are
symmetrical. They are mirror images. And we see these ideas being
suggested in our lives, even when they don't make conscious sense. For
example, just because I like my cat, that does not mean that my cat
likes me. Although I hold onto that idea, and he lets me, especially
when I am feeding him.

Some of these "false" symmetries are easy to spot. The idea that,
because I can't see my cat, my cat can't see me is obviously wrong (I
know he likes to spy on me). Matte Blanco takes these ideas to their
extremes. For the unconscious, he says, if I'm reading a book, the book
is also reading me. This seems very strange and wrong. Then again, the
reality of modern life is that almost all content that we consume online
is also, to some degree, consuming us.

But some other symmetrical ways of thinking are hard to spot. If I know
a lot about a subject, it can be very difficult to imagine what it's
like to *not* know.

The idea that other people see the world as we see it and that they know
about it what we know about it is also symmetrical thinking. In social
psychology, this is known as "social projection" (Allport).

There is a crucial step in our psychological development as human
beings. This is grasping the idea that other people have, literally, a
different point of view. Someone who is sitting on the other side of the
table from me, looking at me, is not seeing the same things that I am
seeing. And that this results in different thoughts and beliefs.

As we grow up, we realize other people are seeing the world from a
different point of view. We realize they have different thoughts and
beliefs. This is part of what psychologists call a "theory of mind."
Note that having a theory of mind is a combination of symmetrical and
asymmetrical thinking. The explicit reasoning goes something like this:

• I have a mind. This is asymmetrical thinking -- ***some.***

• The people that I see in the world are like me. This is symmetrical
thinking -- ***all.***

• So, they also have minds. This is symmetrical thinking -- ***all.***

• And those minds can have different thoughts, feelings and beliefs from
me. This is asymmetrical thinking -- ***some.***

Note that here we're using a chain of both asymmetrical "some" and "all"
thinking. This shows us that these logics aren't exclusive of each
other. They can be combined. And sometimes they can co-exist. Matte
Blanco calls this "bi-logic." Combining the logics is an important part
of understanding the world. And if we don't understand when to use
"some" and when to use "all" thinking, it can cause us serious problems.

## **Where's Sally's ball?**

In 1985, David Baron-Cohen (Baron-Cohen) ran an experiment. His subjects
were neurotypical children and autistic children. The results suggest a
difference in how they understand a crucial experiment. This is that
autistic children have a less developed "theory of mind." They have a
less asymmetrical understanding of their knowledge and other people's.

The experiment involved a covered basket, a box with a lid and two
fictional children: Sally and Anne.

Sally has a ball. She puts it in her basket (the basket has a cover) and
then leaves. Anne takes the ball out of Sally's basket and puts it in
her box. The question that the children watching this scene were asked
is, "When Sally comes back, where will she look for her ball?"

Neurotypical children understand that Sally will be under the impression
that the ball is still in her basket.

Autistic children struggle with this. They often don't get the right
answer. They tend to think that Sally will look for the ball in Anne's
box. This is symmetrical thinking on the part of the autistic children.
They think that because they know where the ball is, Sally will know the
same thing. In neurotypical adults, this question about real points of
view, at least with balls, baskets and boxes, isn't a challenge. They
get that Sally and Anne see and know different things.

But what about more metaphorical points of view? How do we think about
other people thinking about the world? Not how they physically see it.
But their thoughts and opinions on the world. This is far more of a
challenge. Part of this challenge is known as the "curse of knowledge"
(Heath). This is similar to the concept of social projection that we
mentioned a moment ago. Other people don't know what we know. They don't
see the world as we see it. And they don't have our values. But we are
constantly tempted to think that they do.

In a 1990 experiment, psychologist Elisabeth Newton (Newton) gave
subjects a simple task. They were asked to "tap out" the rhythm of
popular tunes to an audience of other subjects. For example, "Happy
Birthday" might be "ta, tata, ta, ta, taaaa." Then they were asked to
estimate the success rate of their communication. How many of the
popular tunes that they tapped out would be guessed by their audience?
The average estimate was 50 percent -- the tappers imagined that their
audience would get the right message with about half of the tunes.

*The real figure was 2.5* *percent.*

The tappers overestimated the effectiveness of their communication by a
factor of 20.

What this result shows is that in some situations we tend to think
"symmetrically." We think "everyone else should be able to hear the tune
that I hear in my head." And we do this unconsciously.

And this raises an awkward, uncomfortable, question -- what if this is
happening in other parts of our lives? What if we're communicating in a
way that's the equivalent to "tapping out the music in our heads?" We
can expect the result to be effective just 2.5 percent of the time!

Maybe there are things we could do to improve our score. If we repeated
the tapping with the same audience would the score improve? If we
practiced, could we get better at helping a new audience get it right
first time? Is there a way of "cheating" that can increase the score?
Would it help if we nodded along? What about winking? What if we mouthed
the words? Or do we just have to admit that tapping the rhythm is a
terrible way of communicating a popular song?

This kind of thinking about how to improve the task is conscious. It's
the logic that spots differences. It understands that we might have
different or partial understanding. It's a logic that also has a sense
of timing. We can try one thing, and if that doesn't work, we could try
another thing. It's a logic that allows us to think about iteration and
learning. Maybe we're terrible at this thing *now.* But, over time,
maybe we can get better.

This is asymmetrical thinking. It's thinking about things in terms of
"some," "different," "for a while." It's a kind of thinking that
understands communication can be poor. We can try something. And it
might be only 2.5 percent effective. But then we could try some other
things, and maybe we could improve that score by a small, incremental
amount. Sometimes, many times, the results of our efforts to achieve
something are an inexact match.

## Symmetrical thinking, asymmetrical thinking and bi-logic

The following are the three most important points that we need to take
from Ignacio Matte Blanco.

***Point one:***

*There are two kinds of logic:*

*Symmetrical logic -- this is the logic of same, all and forever. People
using this logic want things to exactly match. They get upset when they
don't.*

*Asymmetrical logic -- this is the logic of different, some and
temporary. It can deal with inexact matches.*

***Point two:***

*We unconsciously favor symmetrical logic. Symmetrical thinking is not
conscious. And so, the fact that we've **chosen** symmetrical rather
than asymmetrical thinking is also not conscious.*

***Point three:***

*These two different logics can combine. Matt Blanco refers to this as
bi-logic. The logics can co-exist. They can be part of the same chain of
reasoning. We saw* *this when we looked at the idea of other minds.*

We see that our pets and other people die (asymmetrical). But we imagine
that we will live forever (symmetrical). This is an example of bi-logic.
We'll look at how these logics can combine, or carry on at different
levels, in greater detail later in the chapter.

# Some and all thinking in project management

We can look at the world using symmetrical "same logic." Or we can look
at the world using asymmetrical "different" logic. And which logic we
use makes a profound difference to how we see the world. In this
section, I'm going to walk through some key concepts in project
management. Whether we see them symmetrically or asymmetrically is very
important. It makes a huge difference to their use and meaning.

To think about things asymmetrically is *work*. Hard work! It requires
conscious effort and thought. This is work that we need to do ourselves
and need to persuade others to do, if we're going to give our projects
the best chance of success.

Very often, what we need to do in project management is to insert *some*
asymmetrical thinking. A project presents itself in terms of "all,"
"same" and "forever." There is also an idea that the project should be
an "exact match." It should be exactly as it was initially described. It
should do "exactly what it says on the tin." But what if we can arrange
a shift toward thinking about the project in terms of "some,"
"different" and "for a bit?" What if we can focus on the biscuit, rather
than the tin? Then we can change impossible projects into possible ones.

## Time

The symmetrical idea of time is that everything is always and forever.
In symmetric time, nothing starts and nothing ends. If things are
present, they were always present. If things aren't present, they will
never happen.

Of course, this way of thinking makes it very difficult to think about
change, especially constant change. And one kind of change is bringing
things into being. So, this view of time is troublesome if we're in the
business of trying to deliver things.

*Because delivering things needs asymmetrical time.*

Asymmetrical time is something that happens for a certain amount of
time, NOT forever. In asymmetrical time, we can have events.
Asymmetrical time is very closely associated with change. And the
reality is that:

> Everything is always changing.

This is a symmetrical statement about asymmetry, one that's been argued
since around 500 BC. One of the earliest philosophical slogans is "τὰ
πάντα ῥεῖ." This is ancient Greek for "everything flows." And it is
credited to the ancient Greek philosopher Heraclitus (Heraclitus).

Another way of putting this same idea -- again from Heraclitus -- is:

*"You can't step into the same river twice."*

The idea that there is any permanence in the world, that things aren't
continuously changing, is an illusion.

What does this mean for project management? In project management we're
often fighting with the idea that things never change. Of course, this
is particularly odd in the context of managing software development
because what we want is change. We want to go from the status of not
having a project, to the status of having a project.

In software development, people often behave as if once the idea for a
project has been hatched, the project already exists. This is the
"timelessness" of the unconscious, as described by Matte Blanco. And
it's closely related to the discussion that we had in (Chapter STREAM).
Because the *idea* of the project exists, the project itself already
exists in some form. Doesn't it? And the development team are -- doing
what exactly? I'm not sure. Are they just digging up something that
already exists? Are they typing it up?

This may seem very strange. I mention it because it has explanatory
power. Understanding that people think like this can help us to get
better at delivering projects. Stakeholders behave on projects in
curious ways. And many of these can be explained by realizing this one
thing:

*People think that once they've had the idea of a project, somehow, the
project already exists.*

Yes, of course this is, on one level crazy. But on another level, it
fits with unconscious, symmetrical thinking.

## Knowledge

Symmetrical knowledge is closely connected to the idea of symmetrical
time. Symmetrical knowledge is timeless. It has been known forever.
Symmetrical knowledge has been the *same* forever. Some knowledge
appears to be like this.

The "knowledge" that:

> "Two plus two equals four"

seems symmetrical in time. It seems like it's been there forever and
always will be.

There is also another aspect to symmetrical thinking and knowledge. If
we're not careful, our view of what others know and can know tends to be
symmetrical. We've discussed this already as "the curse of knowledge."
We imagine that others know what we know. But we also imagine that they
can't know anything that we don't know.

The opposite of this view is an asymmetrical view of knowledge. In this
asymmetrical view, I know things, other people know different things. In
this partial view, it's unlikely that anybody knows everything. And
everybody's knowledge of the world can *change.* We can learn. So can
other people.

What are the implications of this for project management?

Well, one implication is that there are no experts.

OK, there are experts, I just got a bit too symmetrical in my thinking
there. But experts aren't what we might be tempted to think they are.
Experts aren't people who know "everything." Experts are people with a
particular point of view and a particular set of experiences. Of course,
we want them to have extensive expertise and be able to solve all our
problems. In many situations, we also want them to tell us what to do.
This is yet another bias -- authority bias. We want to believe people in
authority. The psychologist Stanley Milgram (Milgram) showed our urge to
obey authority. He showed that subjects in his experiment were very
obedient to authority. They were willing to carry out orders given to
them by authority figures. And they would do this, even when they
thought those orders were to give other subjects fatal electric shocks!

But maybe the most effective experts are the ones that understand they
only know *some* things. In his book "Sources of Power," Gary Klein
(Klein) studied people who were experts. One thing he identified was a
feature of people regarded as experts. These people were much better
than others at knowing the limits of their expertise. They had a better
idea of what they were and weren't capable of. That is asymmetrical
thinking.

## Choice

Symmetrical thinking wants everything. And that's how it responds when
offered a choice. In "always, forever" thinking, there is no beginning
and no end to time. So, anything that is imagined already exists. But by
the same logic, anything that doesn't already exist will never come into
existence, and this is intolerable.

This kind of symmetrical thinking is extremely familiar to most project
managers. It's familiar to any project manager who's ever asked someone
to prioritize the features of a product. That might be a senior
stakeholder. It might be a product manager. When you ask for a priority,
when you ask them to choose, almost always the initial answer is that
they don\'t want to choose. They want everything.

This is a powerful emotional response to the idea of losing things --
even things that don't exist. This idea is explored by Daniel Kahneman
(Kahneman) as part of his "prospect theory." In the "coin flip"
experiment described by Kahneman, participants were made an offer. They
were offered the opportunity to have a fifty percent chance of winning
\$150 along with a fifty percent chance of losing \$100. Most
participants refused the gamble. Even though, overall, the bet is likely
to pay out \$25.

You might say that Kahneman's experiment is a different situation. The
participants already have the money. It's real. It's in their hands.
What they're worried about is losing it.

But this is exactly the point that I'm making. The people who refuse to
prioritize feel like they have all the functionality in their hands
already. That's the power and problem of symmetrical thinking. Once the
idea exists, in some sense, the reality seems to exist as well. If
anyone tries to simplify the idea, or even decides to realize it one bit
at a time, in sequence, that feels like a loss.

This is the basic fact about doing projects that we're pointing out. We
can't implement all the features at once. Some things have to happen
before other things. And ideas need work to be made into reality.

This reminds me of a joke. A woman buys her husband two ties for his
birthday. He's delighted with the ties. He goes to get dressed for his
birthday dinner and comes back wearing one of them. His wife then says
to him.

*"What\'s wrong with the other one?"*

To paraphrase the philosopher S øren Kierkegaard (Kierkegaard)

*"Do it or don\'t do it* *-- you'll regret it."*

Let's say we do think about making a choice. This involves asymmetry.
Thinking about some. Thinking about different. Thinking about for a
while. It involves picking a side. Making a choice. And so, it involves
inevitable regret. It involves choosing a tie to wear for your birthday
and dealing with the negative emotions and repercussions of doing so.

The first project that I worked on as an Agile project manager was an
academic publishing website. We were tasked with delivering for a strict
deadline. The deadline was dictated by the university calendar and there
was nothing we could do to change it.

The received wisdom of Agile is that in such situations, the way to make
sure that you deliver on time is to reduce scope. Take the list of
things that users and stakeholders want and make it shorter. Or look
inside each of the things on that list and do less for each of them.

Of course, this required some kind of prioritization. And then there was
something beyond the prioritization. There was an awkward conversation.
Some of the things on the list wouldn't be delivered. Or rather, they
wouldn\'t be delivered before the hard-and-fast deadline. There was no
doubt that the project was going to continue beyond the deadline. So,
for any of the things on the list -- we weren\'t saying "no" -- we were
just saying "not yet."

But oh! The wailing and gnashing of teeth! The threats. The complaints.

Eventually, we ended up with a list of things in three colors.

-   Green items. These were the ones that we were comfortable delivering
    by the deadline.

-   Amber items. These were the ones that we might deliver by the
    deadline -- but we weren\'t making any promises.

-   Red items. These were items that we weren't going to deliver by the
    deadline.

Notice what we were doing here. We were inserting a ton of asymmetry.

We delivered all the green items. We delivered some of the amber items.
We went live.

What was fascinating is what happened to the remaining amber items and
red items in that list.

*Nothing happened to them.*

They were never heard of again. Nobody ever mentioned them. Nobody
noticed that they were missing.

The focus of everybody\'s thoughts and discussions after launch wasn't
on things that hadn't been done yet. It was either on fixing things that
didn\'t work or on enhancing things that did.

Here again we see the awesome power of working software. Part of this
power is focusing discussion *asymmetrically.* Users look at something
and they want more of this thing, or they notice that it's missing an
important aspect. And that then becomes the new priority. Working
software has a powerful effect. It pulls people's attention in the right
direction. Away from thinking about same, all and forever. The focus
isn't on all the features in a list. Working software encourages the
right kind of thinking. This is realistic thinking about the project.
This is thinking about "different," "some" and "for a while."

## Speed

Here's another activity that I do in my training courses.

I find a sturdy chair.

I explain that I'm going to do some work. I'm going to step up onto the
chair and then step down again as many times as I can in thirty seconds.
I get someone to time me. Then I ask the rest of the group to count out
loud how many times I step up onto the chair in thirty seconds.

The number of step-ups I can do in a half a minute is about sixteen.
While out of breath and putting my shoes back on, I ask the group
whether they think I could push myself and do eighteen rather than
sixteen. They agree that I probably could.

I ask them if they think I could do thirty-two "chair step-ups" in half
a minute. They agree -- I couldn't.

Then I ask what they think I would do if I were told in no uncertain
terms that I had to do thirty-two step-ups in half a minute. Cheat? Hurt
myself? Feign illness? Run away?

What I'm demonstrating here is possibly the most dangerous and misused
concept in the whole of Agile. It's a concept so controversial that many
people simply don't use it. And they advise others not to use it. The
concept I'm talking about is *velocity.* We've already discussed this a
little in (Chapter ESCAPE).

Velocity isn't the same as speed. In science and engineering there's a
clear difference between them. Speed is the distance traveled in a
certain amount of time. Hence "miles (distance) per hour (time)." But in
speed, the direction isn't specified. Velocity is speed plus direction.
If we're told that a ship is traveling at 5 knots (5 nautical miles per
hour) in a north-westerly direction, we know its velocity. For things
like spaceships and bouncing balls we might need to express velocity
differently. We might express it as a vector. A vector is a collection
of speeds in different directions. Often these are along the x, y, and z
axes.

Is velocity, when we talk about it in software development, more than
speed? If we think about it, we realize that it must be.

A project is moving in more than one direction. It's moving in at least
these directions, as well as the direction of increased functionality.

• Test servers to live servers.

• Dummy data to like-live data to live data.

• No real users, to test users, to a few real users, to lots of users.

This should all sound familiar. True velocity for software is travel
along multiple axes. And this is what we were attempting to do when we
discussed trying to help software escape (Chapter ESCAPE). We want
software to be using live servers. We want it to be interacting with
live users. We want it to be using live data.

What's typically called "velocity" in Agile isn't really velocity. It's
speed along one vector -- functionality. In Agile, "velocity" is often
inaccurately used to describe is the number of things that can be done
per sprint -- typically a two-week period.

The symmetrical view of speed is connected to the symmetrical view of
time. How long should a project take? In symmetrical thinking, it should
have already happened.

So, for most people, the answer to how fast a project should happen is
as fast as possible. Thinking about speed symmetrically tends to result
in the wrong kind of demands. Demands to go *faster than is possible.*

In contrast, asymmetric thinking about speed is focused on going as fast
as is sensible. What do I mean by "sensible"? Well, do you have a notion
of a "sensible" speed to drive in traffic? It's closely related to what
happens if you try to move through obstacles rather than wait for them
to move out of the way. Isn't it? If it weren't, that would be an
"[insensible]{.mark}" speed -- a speed that didn't pay attention to
what's going on in the immediate surroundings of the car. Again, this is
something that we've already discussed in (Chapter CAR).

But what if you're on an empty four-lane road, do you still have a
concept of a safe speed? I'm guessing you do. Will this vary between
individuals? Yes, it will.

But no matter how fast you go, it won't be fast enough for symmetrical
thinking. And it may be fast enough to result in an interesting
conversation with your passengers. And maybe with police and paramedics.
A conversation about what just happened. A conversation while hanging
upside down with an airbag in your face.

Here's the single most common response to any discussion of velocity. In
fact, this is almost the only response to a report of velocity. The only
one that a software development team ever hears from senior
stakeholders.

*"Why can't you go faster? Why can't it be double?"*

This is a request for "[insensible]{.mark}" speed. Isn't that a bit
peculiar?\
This is like seeing me breathless and sweaty after doing 16 step-ups
onto a chair and saying that next time, I should do 32 . It is
"[insensible]{.mark}" to the realities of what's possible.

Symmetrical thinking can offer us an explanation for this kind of
request. It can explain the curious behavior of people wanting to double
the speed of development. It can provide some understanding of why
people are acting as if things should already exist. Even if they've
only just been thought of. It can also explain why clients and senior
stakeholders often end up screaming at teams to go faster. In terms of
symmetrical thinking, anything that can be imagined should already
exist. So, the speed should be infinite. Implementing an idea should
take no time at all.

## Cost

The most common, symmetrical way of thinking about software is that it
should be free. Once the idea is there, the reality already exists. This
is subconscious thinking again. It's just a simple matter of writing it
down. Isn't it? How can that cost so much? Why are we paying so much for
"typists?" And if we need this project to finish sooner, can't they just
type faster?

This may sound insane, because on one level it is. But just because it's
insane, doesn't mean I haven't heard it; it doesn't mean, if you're a
project manager, you won't hear it.

On another level, we can't really call this insane. Because it's a very
common, if not the most common, way of thinking about software
development. Most people have an "all, same and forever" reaction to
thinking about software. And this often means that they have a peculiar
"all," "same" and "forever" way of thinking about its costs.

Sometimes rather than behaving as if software should appear for free, we
see behavior that makes it look as if money were no object.

This sounds really weird, and again, I wouldn't think to talk about it
if I hadn't seen it -- multiple times.

I have worked on several projects where money seemed to be no object. In
my experience, these projects aren't any easier because of this. Money
being no object combines quite badly with a hard deadline. It makes it
very tempting for senior management to just keep hiring more people in
the hope that many hands will make light work.

What's rarest is connecting the cost to value. An asymmetrical way of
seeing money understands that doing things is expensive. And it
understands that there's a cost associated with everything. But it also
understands that there is a potential value.

This kind of asymmetrical thinking requires a great deal more mental
effort. Attaching value to a whole project is hard enough. Attaching
value to individual parts of a project is even harder. Also, too much
thought in this direction could be dangerous. It might lead whoever is
doing the thinking to an unthinkable conclusion. Maybe this project
isn't worth it? Maybe it should be stopped. Thinking too much about
actual costs might undo the work of the "hiding hand" that we talked
about in (Chapter BET).

So, when it comes to money and costs, there are two common attitudes.
One is continuous complaining that the project costs *any* money. The
other is no discussion of costs at all! In my experience, the only real
way to get stakeholders to think asymmetrically about costs is to
connect them to value.

Again, working software is the way to encourage asymmetrical thinking.
And of course, we do this by organizing the escape (Chapter ESCAPE) of
software into the hands of users. "Pull" from real customers who value
the software seems to be the only thing. It's the only thing that will
make stakeholders do the hard work of thinking. The "some," "different"
and "for a bit" thinking that they need to do. The thinking they need to
do about costs versus returns, money versus value.

## Value

I was sitting quietly in my office one day when the building manager
walked in. In his hands he was holding two color samples. A beige and a
gray. He was smiling. He held up both colors. He explained that the
offices were going to be redecorated and so he was asking members of
staff their opinion on a new color for the walls. Did I think the beige
or the gray? I picked the gray. The instant I did, his face dropped,
there was no smile anymore. "Everyone else picked the beige!"

Edward de Bono (de Bono) noted that people are often reluctant to reveal
their values. That's possibly because there is a lot of asymmetry in
people's values. Many people have had the experience of expressing their
values and being treated like a weirdo. It's like they picked the gray
when everyone else picked the beige.

The symmetrical view of values is that our values are the same as
everybody else's. At the same time, we tend to think that anyone who
doesn't value the things we value is a lunatic, a barbarian or a
monster. By now, you might be unsurprised to find out that this failing
in our thinking already has a name. It's known as the "false consensus
effect" (Ross).

If we could avoid this bias, we'd be able to take a fully asymmetrical
view. This would be "à chacun son goût" -- as the French say -- each to
their own. There is no accounting for taste. and other people's values
are not our values.

When we're doing project management, we need to take this more
sophisticated, asymmetric view. We need to be seeing successful projects
in a helpful way.

Successful software connects user values to organizational values.
Here's an example.

Users like saying what's on their mind. Organizations like harvesting
that data and then using it to sell targeted advertising.

Here is another example.

Users like not going to jail for tax evasion. Governments like their
citizens to file tax returns and pay tax.

We've already talked about this in (Chapter STREAM). A successful
project makes connections. It finds what's valuable to the user and
what's valuable to the organization. It connects these things using the
functionality of the software. This is what we're doing when we build
the product value stream.

There is a spectrum of values in the organization. There is a spectrum
of values out there in the world of users and customers who will use our
software. A successful product is one that connects *some* of these
organizational values to *some* of these customer values. This makes for
a queasy, bumpy ride. So much so that many people instinctively avoid
it. The organization might well be wary of revealing what its *real*
values are. And the users might have values that we really wish they
didn't have and absolutely don't want to support.

## Aboutness

How can marks on a page -- or sounds in the air -- be "about" the world?
An advert for wood preserver in the UK claimed that the wood preserver
did "exactly what it says on the tin." And in these discussions, I've
recast this in terms of something I understand and feel passionate
about: biscuits. A biscuit tin has pictures and descriptions of the
biscuits that we expect to find inside.

This "exactly what it says on the tin," idea is a powerful one, both in
advertising wood preserver and in the way that I and others think about
wanting a biscuit. By now we have a bit of an understanding of why this
might be. This is a kind of symmetry that appeals to us, and that
appeal, is, at least initially, unconscious. Our unconscious minds like
the exact match. But what is this "exact match"?

Let's imagine that my cat, Pericles, is sitting on a mat. And let's say
that I describe this in an English sentence.

*The cat sits on the mat.*

I then pull up the voice recording app on my phone, and read out this
sentence:

*"THE CAT SITS ON THE MAT"*

I also draw a sketch that shows Pericles sitting on a mat.

Now we have three things:

• A written sentence -- "The cat sits on the mat."

• A recording -- "THE CAT! SITS ON THE MAT!"

• A drawing of a cat sitting on the mat.

OK, here's where it gets tricky. What relationship do these things have
to Pericles, who, surprisingly, is being well-behaved and is still
sitting on the mat?

How do we know that the marks on paper that make up the written sentence
"The cat sits on the mat" are true? How *could* we know that? And what
if the cat does move? We have these things -- a sentence, a recording
and a drawing. We have this idea that they can be *about* the world. How
do we now know that these things are now false because Pericles moved?

This is the philosophical problem of "intentionality." How can things
that aren't the world -- sentences on a page, recordings of a sentence
or artworks -- be *about* the world.

Here is a definition of "intentionality" according to the internet
encyclopedia of philosophy (O'Madagain).

> "A feature of thoughts and words, whereby they pick out, refer to, or
> are about things, is intentionality. In a word, intentionality is
> aboutness."

The reason that we're talking about this is because we identified two
kinds of thinking. There is "all", "same" and "forever" thinking. And
there is "some", "different" and "for a while" thinking. And they have
very different ways of thinking about intentionality.

Part of "same," "all" and "forever" thinking is not thinking about
intentionality *at all*. This kind of thinking is unconscious and it
doesn't trouble itself about the difference between marks on a page and
the real world. It assumes that there can be an exact match.

Right at the start of this chapter, I mentioned biscuits. And some of
you may have been looking forward to those biscuits. But this is just
text on a page, there are no biscuits! The attitude of symmetrical
thinking is that what it says on the biscuit tin, and what is in the tin
are, if not the same, then straightforwardly related.

But let's forget the biscuits for a moment. This problem of
intentionality is very important to us as project managers. We're in the
business of delivering things into the world. And we need to make them
match noises in the air and marks on a page. What exactly *could* be the
correspondence between the marks on the page or the sounds in the air?
How do we agree on the match? Can we point to it?

Would diagrams help? Well not really. Because the questions about how
drawings match the world are just as tricky. For example, what makes a
map right or wrong? How can a radically distorted map, like the famous
London underground map, be right or wrong? How can one thing be about
another thing? Whatever diagram we draw of a system that we're
developing for a project, it won't be the system. The philosopher Alfred
Korzybski (Korzybski) captured this in the phrase

> "The map isn't the territory."

The answer to these tricky questions is a "some" answer. It's an
asymmetrical answer. *Sometimes* we do understand. *Sometimes,* we
understand how sentences, sounds and drawings match the world. We
understand in cases that we've seen lots of times before. Together,
we've all come to an agreement on what constitutes a cat sitting on a
mat. This works fine for things that are practiced a lot. We've seen
lots of cats. We've seen them on and off mats.

We can start to get relatively good agreement between different
observers. They can agree when a sentence about cats and mats is true.
But what about unusual situations? Those that haven't been observed
multiple times? For those, we'll probably need to put in more work and
have more discussion. Sooner or later, we might come to some agreement.
Or we might not. In new situations that we haven't all encountered
before, figuring out whether that cat is on the mat is much harder. And
a good example of this is when we're trying to decide if a working
prototype matches an initial description of a project. This isn't as
simple as we might like it to be. We haven't seen this particular
prototype or this particular description before. This is a brand new,
unusual situation. And of course, we encounter a lot of these
situations. They are brand new, and no one can agree on what constitutes
a match. We come across these situations a lot in project management.

Thirty years ago, I sat (not on a mat, but on a chair) in a conference
room. It was my first week in my new job as a software developer. I was
attending an internal course on "successful software development."

I hadn't been long out of university. It hadn't quite dawned on me how
strange I would sound. Nor how odd (to me) the reactions of people not
familiar with philosophy would be. I was thinking symmetrically. I was
imagining that people knew what I knew. I was suffering from the curse
of knowledge. But not for long. Reality was about to put me straight.

At the time there was a strong belief in software development circles
that the key to success was the right notation. This "notation" was a
collection of logical symbols. The idea was that these symbols
specifically captured the functions of a computer program. They weren't
ordinary language. They weren't quite mathematics. The idea was that if
only we had the right notation, then we could express exactly what we
wanted the software to do. We could write this down in the requirements
phase. And then we could just hand this over to the developers. From
then on it would be "just a simple matter of programming." If only we
could make the right magic marks on the biscuit tin, we would get the
right biscuits.

I heard myself saying:

> "Yes, but this is just the problem of intentionality."

I didn't say any more.

Because if enough people look at you with a sufficiently bewildered
expression you stop. You think for a minute. You begin to realize that
they don't see the world as you see it. And it was about day three of my
new job. So, I shut up. But what I was thinking was "This will never
work."

Even then, I realized that trying to get everything right at the
beginning wasn't the way to approach the problem. I realized that the
only way to make sure people get the software they want is to build
something. Build something, show them it and then take suggestions for
how it needs to change. I didn't know that was called Agile -- this was
1994, it wasn't called Agile -- yet.

What was bothering me was my asymmetrical view of language, diagrams and
pictures. This was in contrast to the prevailing view in the room for
that course. The view in the room was a more symmetrical, "engineering"
view. It was the idea that the behavior of the software could be
adequately captured in something that wasn't the software, in notation.
It was the view that then software could be written that exactly,
symmetrically, matched the notation.

It was a powerful idea. People wrote books about it. People made money
running training courses about it.

But it was wrong. It was wrong then, and it always will be. There can
never be an exact match between descriptions of a thing and a thing.
There will never be an exact match between biscuit tins and biscuits,
because they're made of different things. Not realizing there won't be
an exact match is unconscious thinking. It's "all," "same," "forever"
and "exact match thinking". Agreement will always be best where we've
done lots of work, and vaguest where what we're talking about is new.
Whatever agreement we finally find is something that we construct out of
lots of repetitions. We collect together lots of examples of "some",
"different" and "for a while." Until we start to get an agreement on
what's a match and what isn't.

And it's probably not a good idea to mention this in your first week in
a new job.

# The wreck of an idea

In an asymmetrical way of thinking, the idea and the thing are
different. Implementation of the idea needs work. The result of this
work may not now or ever exactly match the idea. And this mismatch can
be upsetting.

Let's return to a quote attributed to Iris Murdoch said that UI touched
on in (Chapter STREAM):

> "Every novel is the wreck of a perfect idea."

Perhaps it's ironic that there is no definite evidence that she ever
actually said this. But there is an approximate, rather than exact,
match to these sentiments that is a verifiable quote.\
\
*"The artist is continually correcting the work, and the conception is
continually being left behind." (Murdoch)*

As a writer, Iris Murdoch had ideas for novels. But then, having had the
idea, she had to write the actual book. She had to "leave the conception
behind." This is the pain that she's describing. And of course, this is
one of the things that we have to do as project managers. To make a
real, functioning project, we have, to some degree, to "leave the
conception behind."

Here's one of the weird things about managing projects. When you talk to
other project managers of course you talk about projects. You talk about
projects that they worked on. And they complain about how the reality of
the project turned out to be the "wreck of an idea." But so often these
projects, that they complain about, *delivered.* They escaped! They made
it out into the real world. And they are still delivering: they're
making money for their owners or delivering value in some other way.
They're making lives easier and better for their users. But the project
managers can still remember the "perfect" idea. They can't leave the
conception behind. In their minds, the project, the real one in the real
world that's delivering value, is the wreck of a perfect idea.

But here's the thing:

The *real* novel in the *real* world is the one that entertains and
inspires people, even though it might have left the conception behind.

The *real* project that is being used by users to do what they need or
want to do is also the wreck of the idea. In the language of biscuits
and tins:

> The real thing in the real world is the biscuit.

It's only the biscuit that provides real value. It's only the actual
biscuit that has real nourishment. It doesn't matter what is written on
the tin. By itself, that won't get you there. Those are just words.
You'll starve.

To survive and thrive, we need real biscuits. We need real, working
software, even if it's a "wreck" of the initial idea.

# Conclusion

Our unconscious mind has a certain way of looking at the world. It
favors "all," "same," "forever." It favors exact match. And this has
implications for project management.

The unconscious wants projects to be an exact match. It wants the final
product to match the initial description. It wants a project to do
"exactly what it says on the biscuit tin." And the unconscious mind
doesn't see a problem with this. There is no "problem" of intentionality
in unconscious, "all," "same" and "forever" thinking. In this kind of
thinking, sounds, diagrams and marks on a page can exactly match real
things in the real world.

## Symmetrical thinking -- the problem

In unconscious, symmetrical thinking, ideas and reality are the same
thing. And because of this, crucial aspects of project management are
very difficult. The time that a project takes is infuriating. The
unconscious way of thinking of projects wants them to happen
instantaneously. For the unconscious mind, once the idea of a project
exists, the project itself is, in some sense, already there.

The unconscious wants the project to be an exact match for the original
idea. But this means there isn't really space for the inevitable
learning that happens during a project. And the cost that is associated
with doing projects also feels intolerable. If the idea exists, and so
the reality also kind of exists, what the hell are we paying all these
people for?

Part of thinking about the world symmetrically is thinking that everyone
in the world sees it the way that you do. It's thinking that other
people know what you know and they have your values. This makes learning
the values of users hard. It makes exploring the values of organizations
also hard. But we know from previous chapters that this is exactly what
we need to do to make projects succeed. Exploring user and
organizational values is exactly what seeing projects as swamps is about
(Chapter SWAMP). It's what thinking about constructing value streams is
about (Chapter STREAM). It's what thinking about successful projects as
escape attempts is about (Chapter ESCAPE). And of course, what's
important is how these swamps, streams and escape routes are unique and
different. What we're looking for is "some," "different" and "for a
while." We're looking for an inexact match between our conception and
reality.

Unconscious, symmetrical thinking, when it's written down, can sound
crazy. But it is really common. And this kind of thinking can make
projects impossible. We need to be able to spot when our unconscious
fascination with all, same, forever and exact match is causing problems.
We need to be clear. Focusing on "some," "different," "for a while" and
"inexact match" is a helpful way of seeing. It's how we create possible,
deliverable and valuable projects. It's the way to stop worrying about
the tin and deliver delicious biscuits.

## Asymmetrical thinking -- the solution?

Understanding the problem is one thing. Solving problem is something
else entirely. What can we do about problematic symmetrical thinking?
What *should* we do about it?

Well, having identified the problem we can start to develop a way of
seeing that might provide a solution. This is a way of seeing that
allows us to recognize and tackle clients and product managers who
refuse to prioritize. It provides us with a way of dealing with senior
stakeholders. Those are the ones who seem permanently furious because
their project hasn't arrived instantly and for free. It provides us with
a way of understanding why entire business processes are obsessed with
exact match.

What we need is a more sophisticated way of seeing project success. A
strategy, not only for thinking, but for *doing* things on projects that
works in terms of "some." A way to help us do "some" things, over "some"
time. An approach that understands the most important thing about the
product we create. That is its value to its users and to its
stakeholders. This is a focus on an inexact match. An understanding that
the biscuit tin and the biscuit are different things. Oh, no doubt, it's
important that there's *some* kind of resemblance. But people can only
eat biscuits.

Fortunately for us, *some* people have already done *some* of the
necessary thinking. And we can build on this thinking. We can use it to
put together a powerful strategy. We can deliver valuable things in a
short space of time. And we can do this even if they aren't an exact
match for an initial description. This is what we're going to explore in
the next chapter.
CHAPTER SOME

> "Brevity is the soul of wit."
>
> William Shakespeare

In the previous chapter we discussed the temptations of symmetrical
thinking. We think about the world in terms of "all," "same," "forever"
and "exact match." It is often our first, unconscious instinct. But when
we are managing projects, we need to see the possible dangers of this
kind of thinking. We need to understand the value of thinking in terms
of "some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match." Sometimes.

# *Some* aspects of a successful project

So, what does a successful project look like?

It happens over ***some*** time.

It has ***some*** features. Everything doesn't happen *all at once.*
There is a list of features, we prioritize it. We decide which of those
features to do first. We remember the man in the joke who was given two
neck ties for his birthday. We realize we have to pick a tie!

By implementing ***some*** of the features, we get ***some*** knowledge.
We find out stuff as we go along.

We develop the project at ***some*** speed. This is a "safe" speed. It's
a speed that does not do damage to the people doing the work. It adds
value without unnecessary risk to the product. Moving at this speed
benefits the organizations that are associated with it. It helps them
more than it damages them.

This thing that we've developed is a real thing in the real world. It
has positive features but also limitations. It is ***different*** from
the ideal and from the initial idea.

> It doesn't necessarily do what it says on the tin.

It is valued by ***some*** people who aren't us. A successful project
generates value that outweighs its cost. It has value that customers are
willing to pay for. It generates financial value, social value or kudos
for its owners.

# An ambivalent manifesto

So how do we get to this asymmetric ideal project? Agile approaches
help.

In the Agile manifesto, there are four key values. Each of these values
is an asymmetric statement. They do not say:

> "This thing is good; this other thing is bad."

But rather, they say:

> "We favor this thing over this other thing."

Agile ways of working are about "some" and asymmetry.

I've mentioned how in 2001 a bunch of guys got together in a ski lodge
in Utah. They were all somehow involved in the development of software.
They had a shared interest. They were interested in "lightweight"
methods (Hohl). These were ways of managing projects that were the
opposite of "heavyweight" methods. And those were the methods that were
popular at the time. I mentioned this in the last chapter (Chapter ALL).
The thinking at the time was that *everything* a system was going to do
should be captured at the beginning of the project. And this should be
captured in a complex notation.

The date is significant. The first internet boom was at its peak. People
expected that things would be delivered more quickly. Timescales were
changing. The worldwide web was changing them. Now there was talk about
developing products at "internet speeds." New internet products were
happening in months rather than years.

Products were launched in these new short timescales. This was because
they were launched as "Beta" versions. This was "some" software. It was
"for a while" software. These "Beta" versions weren't finished products.
They came with a certain expectation. The idea was that the software
would continue to be updated.

This was something else that was being changed by the internet. Software
could be downloaded now. We didn't need to get a new copy on a CD or a
DVD.

Sometimes, the software was being released even earlier, in "Alpha"
versions. Here the expectation was that the site, or the software might
break! But the creators wanted early feedback. They wanted to get some
of the functionality out in the world where users could see it.

The old methods were still around. They were still heavyweight. But
using them to develop software seemed, to some people, wrong. The old
methods couldn't keep up. They couldn't deal with these Alpha and Beta
approaches. The guys meeting in the ski lodge in Utah felt that
something different was needed.

The output of that meeting was the Agile manifesto. It's less than a
hundred words long. And it begins with an asymmetrical statement:

> "We are uncovering..."
