Notice, it doesn't say "We've uncovered!" The manifesto then goes on to
describe four primary values. We're going to talk through how these
values encourage the right behaviors. They counter harmful "all," "same"
and "forever" thinking. They insert helpful "some,, "different" and "for
a while" thinking. This is the first of the four values in the Agile
manifesto.

> Individuals and interactions over processes and tools.

People write software. Despite the current enthusiasm for "vibe coding"
using AI, this is the truth. And each of those people is unique. Any
person who fills a role isn't immediately interchangeable with any
other. This value draws attention to the "some," "different" and "for a
while" nature of teams. We have to work with individuals. We have to
work with their skills, aspirations, loyalties and limitations. No
matter how good our toolset or our process, producing software is not
just a matter of cranking a handle.

Developers aren't typists. There isn't a solution that pre-exists that
they are writing down. It doesn't matter what is being said in the
current furor around AI. No tool is going to write your software for you
without feedback from a human, or group of humans, and iteration.

> Working software over comprehensive documentation.

This is the second value in the Agile manifesto. What does this show?
The authors get what we're talking about in (Chapter ESCAPE). A small,
working *"something"* is hugely valuable. The people who wrote the
manifesto knew this. They knew that having *something* that works is far
more valuable than a "comprehensive" list of requirements.

And this is asymmetrical thinking. It communicates that value can be
delivered from a small thing. And when we see how that small thing is
used and how it performs, our understanding and knowledge can be
changed. As a result of delivering *something,* we learn more about the
project and the product. This is also asymmetrical thinking. We show
something to some potential users; we release it into the wild. Then, if
we're lucky, we connect with "pull" -- the desire for what we're selling
from actual customers.

Showing working software to users puts the project on a more
"asymmetric" footing. The focus moves away from working through a list
of requirements. Now the focus is on enhancing or expanding existing
features.

> Customer collaboration over contract negotiation.

This is the third Agile value. The idea that everything can be nailed
down in a contract is symmetrical thinking. "Customer collaboration" is
the opposite of "does what it says on the tin" thinking. "Customer
collaboration" disagrees with a fantasy. This is the fantasy that there
can be an exact match between marks on a page and actual, working
software. It understands that there cannot be a precise match.

And if the drunk on the train from (chapter COMMIT) flashed through your
mind, well, he probably should have done. Because "customer
collaboration" recognizes that you can't fight with the client. You
can't argue over the "foolish" commitment that you probably made in the
initial contract. You have to talk to your clients, your customers. You
have to negotiate with them. You have to arrive at a new agreement based
on new information.

"Some," "different" and "for a while" thinking involves prioritization.
What can be done first? Having done that, what next? It requires
discussion on a case-by-case basis. It requires noticing changes. And
then it requires us to acknowledge them. There will be changes in the
situation and changes in our understanding. Collaboration is
asymmetrical. You don't just do what's in your head. You have to pay
attention to your partners. You have to try to figure out what's in
their heads. And of course, as you do this, you learn, you change. Which
brings us to the fourth and final Agile value.

> Responding to change over following a plan.

Doing exactly what it says in the plan is symmetrical. Modifying what we
do in light of the obstacles and opportunities that we discover is
asymmetrical.

To quote Kent Beck (Beck), who was one of the guys in the Utah ski
lodge:

> "The problem isn\'t change, per se, because change is going to happen;
> the problem, rather, is the inability to cope with change when it
> comes."

We might remember the slogans of Heraclitus -- "Everything flows" -- we
can't step into the same river twice.

Let's say we're working on a project that claims it's going to be a
"world-class licensing service." The meaning of those words might be
continually changing. While we were developing the service, did another
service appear that changed the meaning of what it was to be
"world-class?"

Did the legislation change? Does licensing mean something different now?
Was there a court case? Did the decision change what the duties of the
service were?

I read somewhere that the half-life of any organizational team is two
and a half years. In two and a half years, half of any team will have
gone. That might be your development team. It might be the leadership
team that commissioned your project. It will most likely be both.

The rules are changing. The people are changing. And of course, the
technology is changing. Did the technical definition of what a "service"
means change? Were technologies that are now mandatory simply not in
existence when we started to build?

We've already mentioned one problem with "all," "same" and "forever"
thinking. It only thinks of forever. That means it can't imagine
learning. It can't imagine not knowing a thing. And then knowing it.

Responding to change *is* learning. And it gives us the best chance we
have of making a project succeed. It gives us the very best chance of
making an impossible project possible.

> "We intend to glorify the love of danger, the custom of energy, the
> love of daring."
>
> Filippo Tommaso Marinetti.

Marinetti wrote the "Futurist Manifesto" (Marinetti). It shows the idea
of a manifesto as a radical, uncompromising, strident declaration. But
perhaps this is what is interesting about the Agile manifesto. It is
radical because it is *not* uncompromising. It pretty much consists of a
list of compromises. That's what makes it radical*.*

The Agile manifesto doesn't reject symmetrical thinking. The idea of
symmetrical thinking is there in the manifesto. This is the idea that
the way to make projects succeed is by making the world in an "all,"
"same," "forever" mirror image. An exact match. A mirror image of
documents. Documents that we're familiar with, like plans. Documents
that we find ourselves trying to match exactly. The contracts and
specifications that we all struggle with. The Agile manifesto doesn't
reject any of those documents.

But it puts forward a different idea. This is the idea that we *also*
need, and should *prefer,* "some," "different" and "for a while" things
and thinking. We should prefer working software. We should focus on
collaboration and response to change.

So, the Agile manifesto shows awareness of the two types of thinking
that we've been discussing.

It acknowledges symmetrical thinking. This is thinking in terms of
"all," "same," "forever" and "exact match." And it shows an
understanding of asymmetrical thinking. This is thinking in terms of
"some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match." Both exist at
the same time. And so it also acknowledges a third important point. This
is also an important point in Matte Blanco's work. There is also
"bi-logic." These two kinds of logic -- symmetrical and asymmetrical --
coexist and combine. And they do this in several ways.

# Bi-logic

As a project manager, you meet senior stakeholders who are at the top of
their field. They are extremely capable people. Their thinking about
their own field of expertise is subtle. It's intricate. It's
asymmetrical. Why is this? Well, when someone is successful in an area,
they become acutely aware of the lay of the land. And the lay of the
land is uneven. It's asymmetrical.

For example, senior stakeholders can be very alert to changes. They pay
close attention to power moves. They notice changes in the structure of
their organizations. They know what these changes mean for their own
position. They know about costs, profits and trade-offs inside their own
business. When it comes to their own personal sphere of knowledge, they
will be very aware of the asymmetries.

They'll know details, like how long things actually take vs. estimates
and who to talk to if you really want something done. At the same time,
they will have useful generalizations. These are useful bits of
symmetrical thinking. These are ways of seeing that enable complex
situations to be grasped quickly. And they can do this, often, from
sparse, poor-quality information. They can do it even with information
that has been deliberately warped and distorted. They can deal with
noisy, messy situations in their own field with remarkable efficiency.

These senior stakeholders would be unimpressed if someone came along and
suggested a naïve symmetrical approach to success in their sphere of
knowledge. Should you *always* tell the truth? Well, actually, it's not
that simple. Should ability, or seniority, be the only things taken into
consideration when handing out promotions? Well, sometimes.

Yet, those same stakeholders often have an "all," "same," "forever" and
"exact match" view of things outside their area of knowledge. When it
comes to project management, they have a very symmetrical view of how it
is supposed to work. They insist that the initially agreed scope should
be the final scope. They are surprised and annoyed to find that
end-users have different views from them. They imagine that everyone
should have the same thoughts and feelings. Everyone should agree with
them about what will work and what is valuable.

We're all like this to some degree. There are areas where we have a lot
of experience. In these areas we have asymmetrical and practical bits of
know-how. And we also have useful "all," "same," "forever"
generalizations.

And then there are areas where we have no experience. In these areas
we're more symmetrical and idealistic.

When we learn about something new, we go on a journey. We start off with
naïve generalizations. Let's say:

*All swans are white.*

At some point we become disabused of this idea. We encounter some black
swans. Or even just one black swan! This should be enough to make us
doubt our initial generalizations.

As we get more experience in a new area, we start to develop a deeper
understanding. So many experiences don't fit our initial "all," "same"
and "forever" thinking. And so, we're forced to draw more informed
conclusions. Ones where we can construct asymmetrical ideas. For
example, young swans -- are gray!

This is like the conclusion that Nassim Taleb (Taleb) reaches at the end
of his book "Black Swan." In this book he has a central claim. A certain
situation has gone on in a certain way for a long time. But that does
not mean it can be expected to continue in the same way forever. Again,
this is a "some," "different" and "for a while" conclusion about *all*
things. Things are never the same forever.

There is symmetrical logic. And there is asymmetrical logic. And they
can and do combine. And that combination is bi-logic.

One way that they combine is on the same level, as part of the same
statement. These are statements like the one that Nassim Taleb explains
in his book.

> **All** swans are white, except **sometimes** they aren't, and you'll
> **never** be able to predict when the black swan will occur.

Some combinations of symmetry and asymmetry seem to be appealing. They
appeal to the subconscious. In project management we get this a lot.

*This is a new idea (some thinking).*

*It's something that's the same (all thinking).*

*But it's different (some thinking).*

But this isn't the only kind of bi-logic that we encounter. There are at
least two kinds that I've identified. And if there are two kinds, my
guess is that there are probably a bunch of other flavors, that I've not
yet picked out.

## Chained bi-logic

This is the kind of bi-logic that we've just discussed. Symmetrical
thinking and asymmetrical thinking are linked together in a chain. Using
this thinking can be extremely nuanced and the sign of an expert, such
as Nassim Taleb.

Another example of this thinking being used by experts is one that Gary
Klein gives in his book "Sources of Power" (Klein). A fire chief goes
into a burning building. He can see the flames. He can see smoke. But he
can't hear anything! He wonders if his hearing is faulty. Then he
realizes what's going on. He realizes that something is not right. He
yells at his crew to evacuate the building. A few seconds later the
floor that they were standing on collapses to reveal a fire in the
basement.

This is expertise. The fire chief has a symmetrical thought.

> "***All*** fires make a noise."

He combines it with an asymmetrical thought.

> "This ***one fire*** isn't making a noise."

This leads to a conclusion. This is another asymmetrical thought.

> "***Something*** about this fire is ***different*** (asymmetrical)."

And from that follows the crucial decision about a symmetrical action.

> "***Everybody*** out!"

## Two-level bi-logic

When I lived in Scotland, I would sometimes hear Scots people tell me
(often in bars) that they hated the English -- *"But you're OK, pal!"*

This is an example of a different kind of bi-logic. Sometimes, there is
asymmetrical thinking. And there is symmetrical thinking. They are
present, at the same time. They exist close together. Both logics are
happening. But they aren't being connected.

Aristotle concerned himself with a set of logical arguments called
syllogisms (Aristotle). Syllogisms are logical arguments that have two
statements, called premises. And then they have a conclusion. This is a
famous syllogism.

> Premise 1: All men are mortal.
>
> Premise 2: Socrates is a man.
>
> Conclusion: Socrates is mortal.

Most people can see that this is a valid argument. And it's an argument
that combines some and all thinking. Premise 1 is about "all." Premise 2
is about "some." And the reason it's a valid argument is that the
conclusion follows from the premises.

We can put the "you're OK, pal" argument in the same form. And then we
can see that it's not a valid argument. It is a false syllogism. Another
term for a false syllogism is a fallacy.

> Premise 1: All English people are hateful
>
> Premise 2: You are an English person
>
> Conclusion: You are OK, pal

In this example, the conclusion does not follow from both the premises.

As we've already discussed, experts combine these logics into chains of
thinking. There are two types of thinking. Thinking in terms of "some"
and thinking in terms of "all." And they are both used as part of the
reasoning process. The result can be split-second decisions. And, as
with the fire chief reasoning that the fire is under the floor, these
decisions can save lives.

But when people are novices in a field, they can't manage this kind of
bi-logic. People who are new to a field can't combine "some" thinking
and "all" thinking into a chain. Instead, the "all" thinking and the
"some" thinking are present at the same time. Here is a common two-level
bi-logical situation. It's one that you find yourself in a lot as a
project manager. It is the one where you and your team are being shouted
at because you are not delivering fast enough. This is the "all,"
"same," "forever" and "exact match" thinking that we talked about in the
previous chapter. But at the same time, you are being told that nothing
can be done about a particular (asymmetrical) problem. This is a "bricks
without straw" problem. It's the kind of problem that we talked about in
(Chapter NOT BET). And it's preventing the project from making any
progress.

*Premise 1: This project needs to go as fast as possible (at all
speed).*

*Premise 2: X is a problem that is slowing the project down.*

*Conclusion: Nothing should be done to fix problem X.*

What's really happening here is that the conclusion isn't being made
explicit. No conclusion is being drawn from these two premises. The
"all" logic of the first premise and the "some" logic of the second
premise need to be connected to draw the right conclusion. But in this
case, these two logics don't appear to be connected at all. They seem to
be happening on different levels.

There is a correct conclusion which is being missed.

*Premise 1: This project needs to go as fast as possible (at all
speed).*

*Premise 2: X is a problem that is slowing the project down.*

*Conclusion: We need to fix problem X.*

It's very difficult to call out the lack of connection between the two
logics. Certainly, it's hard to do this without sounding condescending.
It's difficult to avoid sounding insulting. But things don't get easier
if we avoid the awkwardness. In the end, doing that means having to say
things that sound even more insulting. It gets harder and harder to
avoid the craziness.

One example is the case that we talked about in (chapter BET).
Simultaneously we were being told that the project needed to speed up.
Because, in symmetrical thinking it should take no time at all -- right?
At the same time, we were being told that nothing could be done. There
was no help for the specific, asymmetrical problem of slow, unreliable
wi-fi.

This is where one member of the team ended up pointing out in
frustration:

> "To make internetz you need internetz."

Unfortunately, pointing out that people's thinking is crazy doesn't work
well. It rarely has the effect of making their thinking less crazy. We
have to come up with more subtle ways of connecting the two levels of
thinking. Ways that help the project and so, ultimately, help the
project to be built.

# What works

Pointing out that an argument is crazy doesn't work. But other things
*do* work. By now, we understand the difference between "all" and "some"
thinking. And we can use this understanding to change our stakeholders'
thinking. We can take projects that start out looking undeliverable and
impossible. And we can really make them deliverable and possible.

What works is a recombination of the ideas that we've taken from Matte
Blanco. "All," "same," "forever" and "exact match" is the preference of
the unconscious. "Some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match"
is the kind of thinking we need to add to get things done. But
successful thinking is going to always be bi-logical -- a combination of
both kinds of thinking. We're going to talk about three ways of using
our new understanding of "all" and "some" thinking. These approaches can
give our projects a much better chance of success. WYSIATI, magic and
local calls.

## WYSIATI (What you see is all there is)

In case of the project with (next to) no wi-fi, we created a wall of
shame. The developers put sticky notes on a wall. They put a new sticky
note on the wall with the date and time. They did this every time the
developers lost work or had to restart their laptops. I think it took a
week to get the wi-fi fixed. I don't know who saw the wall. I don't know
what they did. But the wi-fi got fixed and we never had a problem with
it again.

Why does this work? I think it's closely connected to the WYSIATI
principle discussed by Daniel Kahneman in his book "Thinking, Fast and
Slow" (Kahneman).

> "What you see is all there is."

Sticky notes were applied to the office wall. It was a thing that people
saw. And then it got fixed. Kahneman's point is that we think with the
ideas and facts that are immediately available to us. This has a very
important, powerful implication. It's especially important for anyone
who wants to change things. We're project managers. We want to change
things.

> If we want to change what people think, we need to change what they
> see.

We might theoretically imagine that we can drive through London at 30
miles an hour. That's the speed limit. Why shouldn't we be able to match
that? That's "same," "all," "exact match" thinking.

But the reality is that we'll stop at a traffic light. And we'll slow
down in traffic. We need to make it clear that we're not going to break
the law. To get asymmetrical thinking about any real-world problem, we
have to show the traffic. We have to show what's causing the traffic
jam. What are the project equivalents of a hole in the road or a bridge
being out? We need to make these clear to the people who are screaming
for the project to go faster. This is especially important if they are
the only ones who can repair the bridge and the holes in the road. This
is important, even if they are the only ones who can fix things and they
don't or won't fix things. Maybe there's some asymmetrical reason why
these things just can't be fixed. It would be good to know this now,
because it will probably change the whole way we approach solving the
problem.

It is possible, just possible, that no connection has been made between
the wi-fi not working and the slow progress of the project. If that's
the case, then joy can abound at the improvement in progress that can
result from rebooting the router. But it's also possible that whoever is
responsible for the wi-fi won't or can't fix it. Even in these cases,
it's better for everybody's safety and sanity if we know where we stand.

On Agile software development projects, there is often a show and tell
meeting every two weeks. This is obviously a powerful opportunity to
control what people see and hear. The team members show working
software. They show designs for new functionality. They discuss user
research. They do several asymmetrical things at the same time.

They show the actual pace of software development. They show that it's
slow and faltering or even, sometimes, they show it moving quickly. They
show that users don't think the same way about the product as the
stakeholders. They show that different users think about the product
differently from each other. More importantly, they don't think about it
as the stakeholders think they should.

Now we're armed with the ideas of symmetry and asymmetry. And so, we can
point to some asymmetrical principles that we should adhere to when
conducting show and tells.

A very important part of this show and tell experience is getting
feedback from the stakeholders. They will most likely be in the
audience. And it's from their comments that you can sometimes get
questions and observations that save projects -- or completely change
them.

Here are some of the valuable questions. And some slightly surprised
answers.

> ***"Will it meet the XYZ regulations?"***
>
> *What XYZ regulations?!*
>
> ***"Is there an audit view for the PQR people?"***
>
> *Who are they? We haven't talked to them. Nobody told us they needed
> to use this system.*

Note that these questions are focused questions. They are "some,"
"different" and "for a while" questions. But there are also a lot of
questions and comments that come out of show and tells that aren't so
useful. And many of these questions and suggestions are of an "all,"
"same," "forever" and "exact match" flavor.

> ***"Will it do everything that we've asked it to do?"***
>
> *Probably not. Because by "everything," the questioner vaguely means
> anything that has ever been discussed that the product might do.*
>
> ***"Does this do exactly what was agreed in the initial contract?"***
>
> *Again, probably not. Because the contract was vague about the
> details. Hopefully, what we're showing is working software that has
> been informed by user research. It is valuable to the users and
> valuable to the organization. And the details of that valuable
> connection won't have been written down in the original contract.*

What this shows us is yet another piece of "some" and "different"
thinking wisdom. Not *all* observations and suggestions from show and
tells should be acted on.

Of course, the power of show and tell, and the understanding that *"what
you see is all there is"* can be abused. Show and tell can be used to
show and tell things that *aren't* true!

But these might be things that match the "all," "same," "forever" and
"exact match" expectations of senior management. It can be a strong
temptation. But here's something that you might want to bear in mind.
Something to remember before you look at an issue on a status chart. An
issue that you know to be a big problem but still, you shrug and color
it green. This kind of behavior is so common it has a name: the
"Watermelon Effect" (Agile Management Office).

Or maybe you show a series of clickable html pages and pretend that it's
working software. This has been going on for a very long time -- since
before software was invented. And it also has a name that comes from
18th century Russia: a Potemkin village (Montefiore). This is a façade
built to impress royalty.

There's always a temptation to show people, especially powerful people,
want they want to see. But giving in to that temptation means not
showing them the real thing. And if you're not telling them what's
really happening, then you're getting further and further away from an
asymmetrical view that matches reality. What's a straightforward word
for an asymmetrical view that matches reality? The truth. What's the
opposite of that? That's right. A lie. And somewhere along the line, if
what you're showing in the show and tells is tending to be symmetrical
and matching expectations rather than real experience, that's what
you'll be doing.

You'll be telling a lie.

## Magic

Let's remember that line from Arthur C. Clarke (Clarke):

*"Sufficiently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic."*

Software is a technology. And it is sufficiently advanced. Working
software is magic. And people respond to it unconsciously.

And it's important to understand that the "magic" that Clarke is talking
about isn't real magic. Rather, what he's talking about is a response
which is very similar to a response to a conjuring trick.

A "book test" is a trick that's been used by magicians for hundreds of
years. It "forces" someone who's been selected from the audience to pick
a particular word on a particular page of a book. The illusion is that
the audience member has been free to pick any page at all. But just one
book (Anneman) describes at least fourteen different ways of doing the
trick. Multiple ways of making a subject choose the right book, the
right page, the right paragraph and right word.

And let's note the weird bi-logic of this situation.

Why do we go to a magic show? Surely for most of us, the reason is that
we want to be deceived. We know it's a trick, we know it isn't real. But
somehow, we enjoy the mismatch between what we see and what we know to
be true. It's about the magician being able to do "exactly" what it says
on the tin, just with his mind. Or it's about symmetry between what's in
the subject's head -- a word in a book -- and the magician's head. This
is the word that he chose, then manipulated the subject into picking.

There's at least one difference between a magic show and a demonstration
of working software, like a show and tell. The intuitive response of
many senior people is to avoid show and tells. They don't want to look
at demonstrations of actual progress. As we've already discussed in
(Chapter ESCAPE), this is because they fear it won't be exactly what
they have in their head. It won't be the number that they first thought
of. It will be the opposite of the experience that they go to conjuring
shows to see.

This is the game that you're playing when you show people working
software. You're seducing them away from thinking symmetrically about
lists of features. They're not, for a moment, wanting something that
does all the things that another system did. You're tricking them into
thinking about "some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match."

The responses can be weird. They can be a wild ride. And they can be
difficult to deal with -- if you don't know what you're doing. And by
"know what you're doing," I mean you know that you're interfering with
the symmetrical/asymmetrical boundary. What you're trying to do is to
get an unconsciously asymmetrical response. A response of the order of:

"The text on this button is wrong."

"When you click on this button, you shouldn't go to that page."

"There are some options missing from this drop-down list."

"Now you've done this, what next?"

"Could I see this with my real data?"

## Local calls

Here's another joke.

The leader of a country takes a trip. He and his entourage are flying to
an important international summit. Suddenly, their plane needs to make
an emergency landing. They land safely, but they're in the middle of
nowhere. They go to the nearest village and ask if there's a hotel.
There is only one hotel in the village -- it's a whorehouse. The great
leader shrugs, that's where they'll have to stay. The great leader wants
to ring his family and let them know he's safe. His mobile doesn't work
in this godforsaken remote place. So, he has to use the landline. The
madam who keeps the house of ill-repute informs him that the call will
be \$1,000 a minute. His aides rustle up the money and he makes the
call. Then he calls the head of his air force to get them to send a
helicopter to pick up him and his team. Again, the madam informs him
that the rate is \$1,000 per minute and his aides find the money.

Finally, he calls the parliament to let them know what happened. His
aides are struggling to find the money for the third call, but the madam
puts their mind at rest, this call is free.

The leader is puzzled. Why is this call to the parliament free when the
other calls were so expensive? The madam shrugs "From a whorehouse to a
whorehouse is a local call."

I often think of this joke when I see something that I've struggled and
failed to communicate. When I've been as successful as those poor souls
in the experiment trying to tap out "Happy Birthday" that we talked
about in (Chapter ALL).

Sometimes, I'm trying to change someone's mind. And I'm getting nowhere.
For example, I was once trying to persuade a test manager that Agile and
testing could work together. I was trying to explain the possibility of
having plans for testing and still being able to use an Agile approach.
I tried lots of different ways. I was getting nowhere.

Then I happened to overhear a conversation. This skeptical test manager
was talking to another test manager. This manager had been working using
Agile for a while. The conversation might have lasted five minutes. But
at the end of it, the skeptical test manager was persuaded. Someone with
the same background has a much easier time communicating a new idea.
Someone who speaks the same language can communicate what they need to
say using far less effort. For the person being persuaded, there's less
suspicion that the persuader has a sinister agenda. It's a local call.

And it's interesting to think -- now we know about "all" and "some"
thinking -- what does "speaking the same language" actually mean? Could
it be that two people with similar experiences draw the same generalized
conclusions about certain things? Could it be that they also agree that
*some* particular things are very important? Could it be that sharing
the same world view is about having the same kind of "all" thinking and
the same kind of "some" thinking? Yes, it could be.

> When we "see" the world as someone else does, what we are sharing is
> the same "some" thinking and the same "all" thinking.

We've seen this "local call" phenomena already in (Chapter ESCAPE). When
you have a clerk as a product owner, they can communicate easily with
other clerks.

Someone with the same background as a skeptical stakeholder can be far
more persuasive than I can. They can persuade the skeptic to at least
try the approach of prioritizing features. They can get someone to
accept the idea of leaving parts of a plan to be filled in later. They
can do this much more easily than someone like me, who is clearly an
enthusiast.

# Grudging acceptance

It would be wonderful to be able to say that any of these strategies to
change thinking are completely effective. But in my experience, none of
them lead to the change in "all" and "some" thinking that we would like
to see. Rather, they lead to a different kind of bi-logic. Which isn't
perfect, but it is more productive. I've experienced it as a continual
narrative complaining that Agile doesn't work, or that the product would
be so much better if we'd only stuck to the original idea. Of course,
the original idea was hopelessly vague, when it wasn't fundamentally
flawed.

I still hear that prioritization isn't necessary. There's no point! We
need everything! We now know that that's full-on symmetrical thinking.
And the complaints continue. Wouldn't it be better if we went back to
the "good old days" of gathering all the requirements right at the
beginning?

So, on one level, there's a strong element of symmetrical thinking
present. But it's combined with a grudging acceptance of the need to
prioritize. And there's a fascination with the magic of working
software. And following on from that, there's a focus on specific
updates. People suggest ways to make the current version better. They're
focusing on the existing thing. They don't worry so much about the items
that haven't yet been delivered. The focus is always on the most recent
version. Oh, there might still be "symmetrical" complaints that it would
be better if we'd just done what we said we were going to do right at
the start. And these complaints might carry on right up to the point
where the software starts to deliver value. Maybe beyond.

But still, the reality is that we've managed to inject enough
asymmetrical thinking. We've achieved enough focus on "some,"
"different" and "for now." We've managed to deliver *something* useful
and valuable.

# Conclusion

There are two ways of thinking about the world. One focuses on "all,"
"same" "forever" and "exact match." The other focuses on "some,"
"different," "for a while" and "inexact match." We call these ways of
thinking symmetrical and asymmetrical.

We have no option but to use symmetrical thinking. In many situations,
we literally don't get the chance to make a choice. We've evolved to
think symmetrically immediately and unconsciously. If we tried to
process each part of what came into our senses as unique, we would be
totally overwhelmed.

But we can't generalize about everything. Sometimes we need to pay
attention to details. We need to draw some distinctions.

If we're going to become an expert in any field, we need to know when to
think symmetrically and make generalizations. And we need to know when
to notice specific details. A true, rich, understanding of the world is
a "bi-logic" of specific observations and generalizations. We need both.

In this book, we take our inspiration from Alan Kay (Kay). We take
seriously his promise of extra IQ points if we change our point of view.
Thinking about things in terms of symmetry and asymmetry is, of course,
a point of view. And we can use this way of seeing to see Agile
differently. Agile is a way of injecting asymmetry into thinking about
project management.

When we use this symmetry/asymmetry lens, we see some interesting
things. A lot of projects appear impossible because they are too
symmetrical in their thinking.

We see someone refusing to prioritize a list of features. We see them
insisting that *everything* is needed. Now, we can see this as
subconscious, symmetrical thinking.

We see an excruciatingly elaborate process to confirm that what has been
built *exactly matches* what was originally described. We know that
there's an asymmetrical approach to that same list of features. The list
can be prioritized. The most important or high-value feature can be
delivered first. Users can get to see working software early. Their
reactions can be used to inform what's worked on next. Here, Agile
approaches are being used to bring about a shift from symmetry to
asymmetry. Something looked impossible. Now it's looking more possible.

Building working software is taking symmetrical thinking -- the dream
that an idea can be exactly and completely realized. And it's adding
asymmetry. It's producing a particular thing.

Here's another way that we inject asymmetry. We put working software in
the hands of users. And when we do this early and often, we shift the
focus of discussion. Putting *some* software in the hands of *some*
users encourages an *asymmetrical* thought -- "what next?" Is there
*something* about this feature that needs enhancing?

Doing user research makes *something* crystal clear. People know
*different* things. Different people have different values and opinions.
Not everyone has the same point of view. They don't share the same
values. User research breaks the "curse of knowledge." This is
everyone's tendency to think other people know what we know. It also
breaks another curse. This is one that doesn't need a special
psychological term. This is the curse of ignorance. User research helps
us learn.

There are four values in the Agile manifesto. Three are suggesting
approaches to developing software that avoid the idea of an exact match.
They are trying to introduce "some," "different," "for a while" and
"inexact match" thinking.

If we're ever going to be able to consider projects a success, we need
to understand this. There is a description of biscuits on a tin. And
there are biscuits inside the tin (we hope). And the description and the
biscuits are different things. Descriptions of biscuits don't feed
anybody. Biscuits do.

That's all very well. But what if you're *in charge* of the biscuit
factory? You have investors, you have regulators. You have customers
with allergies who could die if you mucked around too much with the
ingredients. You gave your workers a recipe for biscuits.

Why aren't they delivering **exactly** those biscuits?

Well, in response, we might ask if asking rhetorical questions is a
useful approach to management? But more usefully, we might ask if there
is a way of seeing that could make senior stakeholders smarter. Is there
are way of seeing that project managers and bosses, not just in biscuit
factories, but also in software development projects, could helpfully
use? Yes, there is, and that's what we'll talk about in the next chapter
(Chapter BOSS). But to get to it, we'll have to first deal with an
unhelpful way of seeing.
Chapter BOSS

> "All knowledge brings us nearer to our ignorance."
>
> T. S. Eliot

If a project is really happening, something, somewhere is probably going
wrong. That's what happens when things happen. The secret to
understanding projects from the point of view of a boss is that they
aren't just sources of bad news, fear and embarrassment. They're also
sources of data. To make sense of that data, and take advantage of the
huge opportunities it presents, you need a specialized team.

# Do you want the good news or the bad news?

The argument of this book is that, if you can look at things in the
right way, you can think about them much more effectively. As Alan Kay
says,

> "Point of view is worth 80 IQ points."

This second part of the book deals with unhelpful ways of seeing. In
(chapter COMMIT) we dealt with the difficulties of seeing projects
entirely through the lens of keeping promises. In (Chapter ALL and
Chapter SOME), we looked at the problems caused by imagining that
success in projects comes from an exact match to a plan, contract or set
of specifications. In this chapter, we are going to look at another
particularly unhelpful way of seeing. We're going to look at a way of
seeing a successful project as one where nothing goes wrong. It's a way
of seeing anything that doesn't go according to an original plan as bad
news.

And then we're going to look at a helpful way of seeing. It's a way of
seeing that expects things to "go wrong." But it's also a way of seeing
that can help us to deliver impossible-seeming projects, even when they
have lots of problems.

Understanding these two different ways of seeing is particularly
important for senior stakeholders -- the bosses. Because seeing things
in these two ways gives us an invaluable guide to the kind of things
that bosses should and shouldn't do. It's an important step toward being
able to make projects deliverable. It's a strategy for turning what
looks like bad news into potential good news.

I came to this realization when I was being asked to think about the
difference between ideas and reality. This was at a very important time
in my life -- just after I'd had lunch.

# Chicken soup for the shirt

I read a book called "Sleight of Mouth," by Robert Dilts (Dilts). As an
exercise in the book, Dilts asks the reader to try to understand the
difference between ideas and reality.

He asks his readers to:

> Think of a thing that happened.

And then he asks them to:

> Think of a thing that didn't happen.

This second thing is a thing that could have happened. But it didn't. If
you're going to try this exercise yourself, I suggest that the two
things should be of around the same level of interest. If the one that
happened is ordinary, the one that didn't happen should be ordinary as
well. So, for example, if the thing that did happen was:

> "I had sugar in my coffee this morning,"

the thing that didn't happen shouldn't be,

> *"I came to work by personal jet pack."*

My two example sentences were:

> "I had chicken soup for lunch."

as the thing that happened.

And:

> "I had a bacon sandwich for lunch."

as the thing that could have happened -- but didn't.

The next stage of the exercise is to think about these two statements.
How you think about real and imaginary things will be unique to you.
Some people see things that really happened in color and things that
they imagined in black and white. Maybe you don't have a mind's eye.
Some people can "taste" colors and "feel" the texture of sounds -- this
is called synesthesia. There are many ways of thinking about the world
using our senses.

How do you see, hear, feel or taste any difference between two
statements? A true one, and one that could be true -- but isn't.

I examined these two phrases in my mind's eye. And I tried to understand
the difference between them. How did I know I had chicken soup for lunch
and not a bacon sandwich?

Then I suddenly realized. There was a big difference.

> I spilled some of the soup on my shirt!

Thinking about that soup on my shirt was a big revelation. It might have
been a more memorable revelation if the soup had been borscht. The shirt
would have been ruined.

But even though this wasn't a serious spill, it made me see things in a
way that I hadn't seen them before. There is an important difference
between ideas and reality. And it's this:

> In reality -- stuff goes wrong.

When we do stuff, things will almost never go exactly the way that we
expect them to. Unexpected things will happen. There will be things
we've forgotten that will pop up to cause trouble. There will be other
things we've never even thought of that we will have to deal with. And
this is an important distinction. Projects that aren't happening don't
have stuff going wrong. Projects that are happening? They have stuff
going wrong.

Projects that are really happening generate all sorts of unexpected
problems, questions and mistakes -- like spillages on my shirt.

And this revelation has important implications. For example, no news of
stuff going wrong means either:

> A: The project isn't really happening.

Or

> B: Nobody is telling you about what's going wrong.

If you're a boss -- if you're in charge of a project -- this is
something you need to understand.

# The sound of the guns

Things go wrong in software development projects a lot. It's one of the
ways you can tell that projects are happening. Another area of human
endeavor where things go wrong -- a lot -- is war.

When people talk about what went wrong -- or even right - with a
software project, they often use ideas from people who have written
about war. When Henrik Kniberg wanted to give an account of his
experiences working with Scrum teams, he called it "Scrum and XP from
the Trenches" (Kniberg).

And right now, we're going to use a military way of seeing to help us
think about software.

Let's say your troops are already fighting on the battlefield. There
isn't time to give them detailed orders about how to behave. There isn't
time, even if you did know what orders to give them. What you need to do
is to provide them with a general rule of thumb. This is a general
principle that the troops can follow beyond the confusing mess of
standing orders.

One powerful military rule of thumb is captured in a famous saying
attributed, either to Napoleon or one to of his generals.

> "March toward the sound of the guns."

The sound of guns is where the trouble is; it's where the risk is
highest. But it's also where you have maximum opportunity to be
effective -- to defeat the enemy, to deliver the project.

That's how we're going to see projects, is it? As places where stuff
always goes wrong? And a way of seeing in terms of war and guns? Some
people will not like this view -- they'll see it as a "negative" view of
projects. They'll say it's a glass half-empty view.

What we're saying is definitely "glass half-empty." Projects are
generators of problems. And so they cause bad news, fear and
embarrassment. Isn't there a more positive, "glass half-full," way of
looking at projects and the news that comes out of them? There is. And
it's a way of seeing which is possibly bigger and more powerful. It's a
broader view than seeing projects just in terms of the negative news
that they produce.

# From counting toilet rolls to bringing down the government

Two people met in the waiting room of a government office in Washington
DC. Those two people were a young naval officer, Bob Woodward, and Mark
Felt, a senior official in the FBI (Woodward). They had a chance
conversation. Together, a few years later, they changed the course of
history.

Woodward was about to leave the navy and wasn't sure what direction to
take with his career. Did the world need another lawyer? What else was
there? In initial chats with Felt, Woodward sought career advice. Felt
was further on in his career and much more successful.

Felt's advice to Woodward was:

> "Go where the action is."

He told Woodward a story about his first government job. He became
involved in a nationwide task force. Its goal was to track national
consumption of toilet paper. It wasn't the world's most exciting work,
and Felt started to worry, as many young people do in boring jobs. Maybe
toilet paper would be the rest of his life. He had heard that lots of
interesting things were going on in the intelligence field. That was
where the action was. Felt got out of the toilet paper business and got
a job in intelligence.

Investigative journalism was another field where the action was in the
late sixties and early seventies. And Woodward became a journalist.

He found himself researching a story about a break-in at the Watergate
office building in central Washington DC. And now Woodward and Felt's
conversations were no longer about career advice. They were secret and
possibly dangerous. They took place in car parks in the middle of the
night. Woodward signaled when he wanted a meeting by moving a plant pot
to the front of his balcony. Felt sent back the time and location of a
meeting. It was written on Woodward's morning newspaper. Woodward never
knew how (Bernstein).

For a meeting, Woodward took first one cab, then another, to an agreed
location, carefully avoiding being followed.

Felt pointed Woodward down a trail. That trail led ultimately to the
resignation of Richard Nixon, President of the United States. Felt would
turn out to be the most famous confidential source for a news story in
history. But for decades he would only be known by his code name: Deep
Throat. And in one of those car parks, in the middle of the night he
would give Bob Woodward his most famous piece of advice.

"Follow the money."

# Follow the money

> "Go where the action is"

and

> "Follow the money."

These are great pieces of advice. They are the "glass half-full" version
of some of the things that we've already talked about. Up to now in this
chapter, we've been seeing projects as sources of bad news, problems and
embarrassment.

*"Go where the action is"*

is a lot like

*"March toward the sound of the guns."*

But with less of an air of combat trauma and lethal injury.

When Felt said "follow the money," he meant follow the paper trail. The
men who broke into the Watergate building were paid by someone. Who was
that person? And where did they get their money? Following those
payments ultimately led to Nixon's White House.

But we can think about "follow the money" in a different way. We can
combine it with the idea of "going where the action is." Where the
action is -- all of this unexpected news that is coming out of a project
-- is also where the *value* is. Maybe it's money, maybe it's some other
kind of value. But where the action is -- that's where we can discover
value.

> What do users really want?
>
> Could we give that to them relatively cheaply?
>
> If we could give them that, could we get them to pay for it?

This is the crucial glass half-full way of seeing projects. Yes,
projects are generators of bad news, problems and embarrassment. But
that's just another way of saying that projects are full of data. This
is data that we can construct into information. And some of this
information can be very lucrative. Projects generate data that tell you
what your customers like. The data tells you what they want more of and
what they'll pay for.

So, the glass half-full version of the Napoleonic saying, with no
reference to guns or war, might be something like this:

> Move toward the data.

# Data, information, knowledge and wisdom

Are we talking about just data here? Or are we talking about
information? Or knowledge? Are there any further stages of enlightenment
beyond knowledge?

And it's not just the amount of data that's important? Surely it's
something else? It's how *interesting* the data is. Someone could
collect as much data on toilet paper use as they wanted. It still
wouldn't be interesting.

To get to something interesting, we need to put together single pieces
of data into something bigger. We need to make *information.* Then we
can draw conclusions from the information.

In information science, this is known as the DIKW pyramid (Rowley).
Where:

"D" is data.

"I" is information.

"K" is knowledge.

"W" is Wisdom.

But even wisdom isn't enough. None of this effort to make sense of data
is worth anything unless we *act* on it, unless it changes our behavior.
Of course, there's a lot of data that, once we've made sense of it, we
will ignore. Deciding which bits to ignore and which bits to act on is a
crucial part of the sense-making that we have to do. But, if we're
really going to help our projects, at some point we will have to act on
the data that we receive.

Here are some examples from my real experience of how we've turned data
not just into wisdom, but beyond wisdom, into action.

This is a problematic, embarrassing example which very much fits the
"soup on the shirt" model of projects. We started to see data that
indicated that the technology we were relying on for the project didn't
perform as we expected. We just couldn't make it do the things we needed
it to do.

***Data:*** We tried to use technology X to perform function A. We
thought it could do this -- we never managed to make that happen.

***Data:*** We tried to get technology X to perform this other function
B. We thought it could -- we have not managed to make that happen.

***Information:*** All the attempts that we've made to use technology X
to implement functionality so far have failed.

***Knowledge***: Technology X doesn't work. Or we can't get it to work.

***Wisdom:*** We need to find a different way of implementing the
functions that we thought we could implement using technology X.

***Action:*** Find a different new technology or go with an older, more
established technology.

Here's another example. This was a project that was supposed to replace
an existing system with "like-for-like" functionality. The team noticed
this opportunity. It resulted in a big saving in resources.

***Data:*** Several reviewers of this research project approval form
report the same thing. Some projects are "continuation projects." They
carry on work from a previous project.

***Data:*** Reviewers want to be able to see easily which projects are
continuation projects.

***Data:*** Continuation projects must be reviewed and approved before
the deadline. There is an important issue with projects that aren't
reviewed in time. If the continuation project isn't approved, the rules
say that all the resources associated with the project must be
destroyed.

***Information:*** There is an opportunity here. An easy way to indicate
that a project is a continuation project could save a lot of resources.

***Knowledge/action:*** Put projects that are continuation projects at
the top of the list of projects that need to be reviewed. Show the
deadline and time remaining until the previous project license ends.

Knowledge and wisdom that can be acted upon. That's what we can
ultimately find by starting to look at data. That's what happens if we
follow "Deep Throat" and "maybe-Napoleon's" advice. If we walk toward
the sound of the guns or go where the action is. This is what happens if
we walk toward the data. And so, one might ask the question -- why would
anyone not do that?

# Inaction figures

Data sounds good. But to get to the knowledge and wisdom, data needs
processing into information. And information needs to be made sense of.
It needs to be verified and checked. Theories need to be formed and
tested before information can be turned into knowledge. And that's not
the end of it. This knowledge needs to be given meaning. It needs to be
turned into wisdom. And finally, for it to make any difference, this
wisdom must dictate some action. Otherwise, why are we bothering?

But what action?

And what will be its consequences? Those consequences are going to be
data!

This is a complex and uncertain process -- it's hard work. Even if
you're expecting this to happen and you know what you're doing. This is
a terrifying process if you're not expecting it to happen and you
strongly suspect that you don't know what you're doing.

Now we're coming to one of the main reasons why projects go wrong.

No, it isn't because of "poor estimation."

No, it isn't because the team didn't work hard enough or stay late or
work weekends.

A major reason why projects fail is that the people who manage those
projects aren't expecting them to generate data.

Bosses just aren't equipped to deal with projects that generate data.
They don't expect so much data, information or knowledge. They most
certainly don't expect their projects to produce requirements for
action. Especially requirements for action on *them.*

It's important to understand that it isn't just bosses who can't deal
with a lot of data. No one can. Our inability to deal with lots and lots
of individual bits of data is why we have the rough and ready, messy but
effective ways of thinking that Daniel Kahneman (Kahneman) is referring
to in his book "Thinking, Fast and Slow."

A specific kind of fast, effective but messy thinking that happens
repeatedly and reliably is called a cognitive bias. We have cognitive
biases because we simply cannot deal with all the data that meets our
senses. We can't process it fast enough. We have to take short cuts.

And so bosses do what most people do, almost without thinking. They have
a bias to avoid trouble. They *don't* walk toward the sound of the guns.
They *don't* move to where the action is. They run away from the action.
They avoid the fear and embarrassment. They avoid even the *possibility*
of fear and embarrassment. They avoid the data that comes out of a
project, simply because there's a lot of it. If they tried to make sense
of it and turn it into information, there would be too much information.

We all do this. We all, of necessity, ignore a lot of the data that is
presented to us. We do it unconsciously. This is understandable.
Avoiding fear and embarrassment is the right thing to do -- from an
evolutionary point of view. Obviously scared and embarrassed leaders
tend not to last long as leaders.

> "Avoid novelty."
>
> "Duck situations that require thought."

Those aren't things that you'll see in many mission statements. And this
is almost exactly the opposite of Mark Felt's advice to go where the
action is and follow the money. But it's the way people often behave in
organizations.

We simply don't have the resources to process all the information that
comes to our ears, eyes and fingertips.

Some of that data might be the story of the century -- like the links
between the Watergate burglars and figures connected to the White House.
Some of the data might just be endless details regarding the usage of
toilet paper. Figuring out which data is potentially interesting and
which is boring is work. And it's work we instinctively try to avoid
doing.

These instincts that have over millennia turned into the "fast thinking"
that Kahneman talks about, are vital. They're how we keep ourselves
safe. They're how we stay sane, and how we stay alive. But their
usefulness can be limited in organizations that are trying to deliver
new projects. This instinctive behavior can become a serious problem. It
can kill a project. And it can seriously hurt an organization.

You might be thinking to yourself, "Ah yes, those bosses behaving badly,
thinking fast, avoiding the data -- that's terrible." Never for a minute
imagining that I might mean you.

I mean you.

It's easy for us to see that others will tend to avoid fear and
embarrassment rather than doing what needs to be done. It's much more
difficult to see this behavior in ourselves. This problem is called by
the psychologist Emily Pronin the "bias blind spot" (Pronin).

Well, let's see, shall we? Let's have a little magazine-style poll.

1.  Have you ever used the phrase "ambitious deadline"? Give yourself 10
    points.

2.  Did you ever try to kick off a project knowing in your heart of
    hearts there'd be a problem with the regulators? Did you know that
    once the regulators got wind of it, they were going to smash it
    flat? Give yourself 20 points.

3.  Has someone on your team ever had the courage to point out a problem
    with the project. Have you ever told that person off for being
    "negative"? Give yourself 10 points for every occasion.

4.  Have you ever said, "There's no point in prioritizing this list, I
    want it all"? -- 50 points.

5.  Have you ever tried to compare the productivity of two teams? -- 100
    points.

6.  Have you ever tried to stop a software development team from talking
    to people who know how the product should work? -- 100 points.

7.  Have you ever tried to stop a software development team from doing
    user research? Have you stopped them from talking to the people who
    are going to use the software? 200 points.

8.  Are you feeling good about yourself because you haven't answered
    "yes" to any of these questions? Is that because you're too senior
    to be involved in any of this? But you suspect it's going on? Give
    yourself 1,000 points.

9.  Have you ever suggested to the project manager of a team, or even
    worse, members of the team itself, that they should be working
    harder? Did you say you're disappointed that they're not staying
    later, working weekends and looking more stressed? Give yourself
    5,000 points, and don't expect that humanitarian of the year award
    any time soon.

10. Did you avoid all chances to see early versions of the software as
    it was being developed? Did you make it impossible for the team to
    put early versions of the software in the hands of users? Did you
    stop the team from talking to people who know how it should work?
    And then, when you finally saw the software, did you leave an
    awkward, awkward pause before you said, "I thought we agreed..."?
    Did you then talk about a feature that nobody on the team had ever
    heard of before? Congratulations, you have won all the points. It
    isn't called "boss level" for nothing.

Most of these bits of "bad behavior" described above can be explained
using the point of view that we're discussing. The view of projects as
generators of bad news, fear and embarrassment.

What is someone saying when they use the phrase "ambitious deadline?"
What they're really saying is: "I know that this timescale is
impossible. But I'm going to appeal to positivity, hard work and common
sense (whatever that is) rather than addressing the issues with
delivering for the deadline." They're saying that they're going to
ignore the data.

# How to be an action figure

So, what should you do?

If you are the boss of a project, you are the boss of something that is
going to generate fear, embarrassment and problems. That's the glass
half-empty way of seeing it. But the glass half-full way of seeing it
isn't that much less challenging.

Projects are going to generate data. And this data will cause problems,
fear and embarrassment. But there will also be huge business
opportunities. Also, there will be confusing data. Data that might be
good, might be bad or might not be important at all. Interpreting this
data, making sense of it and deciding what to do about it is work. Work
that either you need to do, or you need to allow other people to do with
your blessing.

Running away, ignoring the data or shooting the messenger will only work
for a while. Sooner or later, the project is going to get into serious
trouble if you don\'t pay attention to the data it creates.

And this kind of bad behavior from bosses encourages bad behavior in the
team. It puts off the very people that, as a boss, you need. You need
the members of the team to gather and make sense of the data the project
is giving out. Bad behavior from the bosses encourages members of the
team to keep quiet about what they see.

Because there's a similar list, a similar poll, that we could write for
people who are working inside a team:

1.  Have you ever noticed something that you know is going to slow a
    project down, but you've said nothing about it?

```{=html}
<!-- -->
```
11. Have you ever seen an opportunity to make a project more effective,
    but because it's not on the plan, you said nothing about it?

12. Have you ever avoided user research because your instinct is that
    the users won't like the product?

13. Have you ever sat in meetings where the dreaded "ambitious deadline"
    was discussed? Did you think "yeah that's never going to happen"?
    Did you say nothing?

14. Have you ever avoided talking to anyone who knows how something
    should work?

15. Have you ever avoided talking to someone who could tell you what's
    happening more broadly across the project? Did you do this just so
    you could get on and finish something?

16. Have you found yourself slipping with your teammates into "agreed
    activity?" As if you're scrubbing the decks of your ship in the face
    of oncoming pirate ships, as we discussed in (Chapter SHIP)?

What we're seeing here are two ends of the same near-blocked
communication channel. The bosses are shouting down the channel. They
are saying how it should be. They are not listening to how it is. The
team members are telling the bosses what they think they want to hear.
They want a quiet life, so they are not telling anyone how it really is.
There's a name for this. It's called the SNAFU principle.

SNAFU is an acronym. It stands for Situation Normal, All F\*\*ked Up.

# The SNAFU principle and the emperor's new clothes

What is the "situation" here that's normal? Normal, but also all
f\*\*ked up? It's what we've just talked about. Communication in a
hierarchy. The basic idea laid out by the writer Robert Anton Wilson
(Wilson) is something we've already talked about in (Chapter SHIP). It's
that:

> "Adequate communication flows freely between equals. Communication
> between non-equals is warped and distorted by second-circuit
> Domination and Submission rituals perpetuating communication jam and a
> Game Without End."

Nearly all projects happen in a hierarchy. And so, warping of
communication in a hierarchy is something that should concern us.

Here's a famous example of the SNAFU principle in action. Two men come
to town. They've heard that the emperor has a weakness for the absolute
finest things in life, especially clothes. They tell him that if he
gives them two bags of gold, they can weave for him the finest, most
delicate cloth that could ever be woven. It is so fine that only the
cleverest people and people who are good at their job can see it.

The emperor gets excited. He thinks that he'll be able to use the cloth
as a way of distinguishing clever people from stupid people on his
staff. He also thinks he can fire some people who are obviously not good
at their jobs because they can't see the cloth.

But when the strangers reveal the "suit of clothes," the emperor gets
very worried. *He* can't see the clothes! But he doesn't dare say
anything. He is frightened that if he does, he will reveal himself to be
stupid. Maybe *he* is unfit for *his* job.

The emperor's courtiers and his subjects see him in his procession
through the streets. They can see that he's naked.

But they don't dare reveal this. They are frightened that the emperor
will shoot any messenger who embarrasses him. Finally, the situation
reaches a conclusion. A little boy points to the emperor as he marches
through the town. Before his father can stop him, he shouts,

*"The emperor's not wearing any clothes!"*

This story is clearly ridiculous. But it's also something that we
encounter every day. There are some new guys from out of town. They have
the emperor's ear. They're from outside the organization. And they have
sold the "emperor" -- the boss -- on an insane idea. Nobody in the
organization dares tell the boss.

The idea might be appearing naked in public. Or it might be an idea for
a new piece of software using a brand-new kind of wonder technology. The
result of this new idea is that the "emperor" is either metaphorically,
or literally, standing stark-naked in a public square. But the power of
the SNAFU principle is strong. Nobody tells the emperor the obvious bad
news. Nobody wants to tell him that he's been fooled and should stay
away from drafts.

# SNAFU works both ways

Here, in the case of the emperor, we see that people who are supposed to
communicate with the boss are avoiding the problem. They are avoiding
their own fear and embarrassment. And the result is that the information
that needs to get passed on up the hierarchy simply doesn't get passed
on.

The conmen who fooled the emperor did their absolute best to take
advantage of the SNAFU principle. They made it so that the emperor was
excited at the thought of people pointing out that the cloth wasn't
there -- so he could fire them!

They also made the emperor terrified of looking like a fool. At the same
time, they made him look as foolish as he could possibly be.

In (Chapter SHIP) we discussed the ideas of improvised theater pioneer
Keith Johnstone. We talked about the idea that all dialogue (and so all
business life) is a status transaction. What Robert Anton Wilson is
saying is that,

> "Any communication in a hierarchy is a status transaction."

And so, it's going to be warped. And in a work setting, that is nearly
all communications.

What if we look at the emperor's new clothes from Hans Christian
Andersen's point of view (Andersen)? We can see how he builds up the
toxic culture around the emperor. He has to do this to make the dynamic
of the story work.

The protagonist of the story isn't just a king. He's an emperor! In the
story we hear that the emperor doesn't review troops. He doesn't open
new buildings. He doesn't visit schools.

He is completely out of touch with what is going on in his empire.
Rather he spends all his time focused on his clothes.

When the two conmen knock on the gates of the palace, the emperor's
footman answers the door. He snootily tells them that the emperor is
reviewing a new shipment of shoes. He isn't available to visitors.

And crucially, the emperor has no trusted advisors. There's no mention
of a chancellor, a prime minister. There's no mention of a jester or a
fool. He doesn't seem to have a queen, or any family. Andersen puts the
emperor on his own.

Andersen is making the troubles of the emperor worse. He has to do this.
He needs drama and disaster to make the story work. But in doing so,
Andersen gives us some clues as to how we might fix the SNAFU principle,
at least as it works for managing projects.

# Safely shrinking the status difference

We can look at "the emperor's new clothes" as a guide to what *not* to
do. What's the absolute worst kind of situation to find out the bad news
that you're naked? It's probably in a public place, in front of lots of
people who are much lower than you in the hierarchy. This is the climax
of Andersen's story. Of course, it's also the worst part of many
people's nightmares.

There's only one thing that might make this nightmare worse. That's if
the news that you were naked came as a total surprise!

Andersen can't quite manage to add total surprise to the mix, because
even his emperor isn't that stupid.

But these things taken together give us a guide to how you, as a boss,
should be talking to advisors.

You should be talking to them in private. You should be talking to
people who aren't that much lower in the hierarchy than you who are
trusted advisors. That might be tricky in the situation that you're in
right now. They may well not exist in your organization. You may have to
recruit them. Or you may have to find likely candidates and nurture
them.

And talking to them isn't enough. *How* you talk to them, and how they
talk to you is just as crucial. If you're the boss, you can't be
shouting at your advisors. And you can't be ignoring them either. If
you're the boss, you need to be doing your best to stop the SNAFU
principle applying, at least for a while. You need to find a way of
talking where data, information, knowledge, wisdom and suggestions for
actions are exchanged, with as little distortion as possible.

# Surround yourself with good advisors and talk to them well

This is a concept that I have borrowed from author Patsy Rodenberg. She
is a voice coach to the stars. The idea is explored in detail in her
book "Presence" (Rodenberg).

The basic idea is that there are three modes which we use to communicate
with others. Rodenberg calls these first, second and third circle.

## Third-circle energy

If you're approaching an interaction in third circle, you're probably
raising your voice, or you're shouting. This is the "Let me tell you!"
interaction style. Third circle is the "push" energy. It's the energy
that doesn't want to listen. The energy that wants to tell. Any
discussion that involves pointing fingers and pushing out the jaw is
probably third-circle energy. Asking rhetorical questions is
third-circle energy.

> "Why is this taking so long?"
>
> "Can't you go faster?"
>
> "Why can't you just do what I tell you?"

These questions don't expect an answer.

This is the energy that bosses often adopt in interactions with their
juniors. It's the energy that amplifies the warp in communication that
typifies the SNAFU principle.

It's "shouting at people to go faster." It's the "deadline isn't
negotiable" growling that bosses fall into when dealing with their
projects. Also, I am ashamed to realize, it's the Jerry-Springer-style,

*"I don't solve people's problems I just televise them"*

energy. It's a style that project managers can slip into if they're not
careful or when they're not being listened to.

## First-circle energy

The opposite of third-circle interaction energy is first-circle energy.

First-circle energy is quiet and introverted. It offers no resistance.
First-circle energy doesn't argue. And it certainly says nothing that it
thinks is going to cause controversy. It doesn't want to start an
argument, or even prolong the conversation longer than the absolute
minimum.

First-circle energy wants to be left in peace. First-circle energy
notices the pirate ship on the horizon. Then it looks down and scrubs
the decks (Chapter SHIP).

What should be obvious by now, is that neither of these kinds of energy
-- first circle or third circle -- is terrifically helpful for the kind
of communication that we need up and down the hierarchy in a project. We
need another approach.

## Second-circle energy

Second-circle energy is about being able to "enter into friendly
relations with whatever appears on the scene." That's how Eugen Herrigel
puts it in his book "Zen in the Art of Archery" (Herrigel).

Second-circle energy isn't yelling. It isn't shouting at other people to
go faster. And second circle isn't ignoring what other people tell you
and retreating. It isn't refusing to listen to bad news.

Second circle is about being able to enter into friendly relations with
the people who you need to talk to. And to do the same with the people
who you need to have talk to *you.* It's about being able to talk to
them about what is going on in your projects. Second circle is setting
things up around your project so that you have the advisors that the
naked emperor doesn't have. Advisors who are prepared to tell you when
you are naked.

And this kind of conversation isn't new to us in this book. Because it's
the same kind of conversation that was the solution to the problems that
we encountered in (Chapter COMMIT). It's the conversation that *isn't*
stridently telling the team what they've agreed to, so that's what they
need to deliver!

It's the kind of conversation that negotiates what can be delivered,
based on real progress and an understanding of newly discovered value.

Using the way of seeing that we've discussed in this current chapter, we
can see another way that this second-circle kind of conversation is
different from either first or third circle.

It's a conversation about the data. It's about the data, the
information, the knowledge that's coming out of the project. First
circle is:

*"Leave me alone."*

Third circle is:

*"Let me tell you."*\
Second circle is:

*"Let's talk."*

Why do we normally avoid these second-circle conversations? One answer
is that these conversations make us extremely vulnerable. Second circle
is different from third and first circle. Second-circle conversations
produce change. And change is scary.

Let's say I'm a boss. You tell me something. It's a surprise. It's not
good news. Let's say I'm willing to listen and be changed by it. The way
that I'm changed by it speaks volumes about who I am. It reveals how
smart I am, how capable -- or incapable -- I am. It shows how much I
understand what's going on in my business. It shows how much power I do
or don't have. It shows how much I understand the organization where I
work.

How much safer is it, certainly in the short term, to avoid this kind of
second-circle discussion? If I move into third circle, my immediate
problems go away. If I shout,

*"That's not what we agreed in the contract! Why can't you keep your
word?"* I'm off the hook. I don't have to change. I don't have to be
judged on how I changed, how I made sense of the new information that
you're telling me. There are no actions, so I'm not going to be judged
on them.

Or I could just quietly slip into first circle. I could just ignore you.
I don't even have to do that. I could get a "baby seal" like the one
that we talked about in (Chapter BET). I could hire someone to ignore
you! Hire someone to sit in the reporting chain between you and me. A
baby seal who will only tell me good news. I'm far, far away from where
the action is. There is no sound of guns. La! La! La! I'm not listening!

We are only willing to have second-circle conversations when we feel
comfortable being vulnerable.

So, we're only prepared to have second-circle conversations with people
we trust. But really the only way to gain that trust is by making
ourselves vulnerable. Someone has to make the first move. This is
something that we discussed in (Chapter SHIP), when we talked about
psychological safety. The only way that psychological safety can get
going is if someone is willing to be vulnerable. Someone needs to voice
a critical opinion. Someone needs to admit that they don't know
something.

And if you're the boss, it probably has to be you. You're the one who
has to temporarily lower your status. You're the one who has to show
that you're listening, by being changed by what you hear. You're the one
who can mess things up by growling or saber rattling when you don't like
what you hear.

# Trusted advisor, trusted boss

In their book "The Trusted Advisor," Maister, Green and Galford
(Maister) talk a lot about trust. They are mainly concerned with helping
consultants to get their clients to trust them.

But their advice could just as easily apply to those clients, who are
bosses. Bosses need to get into frank, second-circle conversations with
their team. And to do that, the bosses need messengers from the team to
trust them.

As with so many aspects of the difficulties of project management,
there's nothing astounding in the advice that Maister et al. offer.
What's difficult about following their suggestions is doing them while
being frightened, while dealing with bad news and while being
embarrassed.

So how can bosses go about increasing trust?

***Listen actively.*** Pay attention to what you're being told. This is
the give and take of "second-circle" communication. There are lots of
reasons why this is a good idea. In this chapter, we've talked a lot
about the value of the data, information and knowledge that a project
generates. To take advantage of any of this, you need to pay attention.
Yes, that's where the trouble is, but it's also where the value and the
money are! If you slip into "third-circle" shouting, your advisors will
either go silent or only tell you what you want to hear.

***Be reliable.*** If you say you're going to talk to someone about the
problems with the wi-fi, then do that.

***Demonstrate expertise humbly.*** This advice might seem like it's
only for the consultant, and not the client.

But there's an equivalent for you as the client, or boss:

***Don't throw your weight about.*** Yes, you're senior. Yes, you're in
charge. But if you assert that seniority, then you will narrow the
channel of communication -- the one that is already nearly closed due to
the SNAFU principle.

***Show empathy.*** This business of project management is difficult for
everybody. And the position that others are in, in relation to the
project, isn't the position that we're in. The more you can see the
difficulties of the person that you're talking to in second circle, the
better chance you have of not slipping out of second circle into either
first or third.

***Be transparent.*** Information-hiding is the main way in which the
SNAFU principle gets implemented.

As Maister et al. point out, trust is often an asymmetric relationship.
Just because your client trusts you, doesn't mean that you trust your
client! It's also context-dependent. Trust only needs to apply to
matters regarding this project.
