But for project management to work, the trust needs to be mutual. So,
when you're looking for advisors that you can trust, you're also looking
for advisors who can trust you. Are you behaving in a way that means
that your advisors can trust you? If you aren't, then you may very well
be wearing the famous emperor's new clothes -- and no one is going to
tell you.

There are at least two kinds of trusted and trusting advisors that you
could have for software development projects. And you need them both.

## Project manager (PM)

Project managers concern themselves with two different kinds of problem.

The first kind of problem is those that stop the project dead in its
tracks. These are the classics, like "marvelous technology X doesn't
actually work," and "there's no wi-fi in the development office."

The second kind of problems is those that affect the delivery and
timeline. This is pretty much the same problem again and again -- things
are taking longer than we expected/wanted/dreamed that they would take.

This book is called "Delivering the Impossible." And as I write this, I
feel that I've buried the answer on how to do this, how to deliver the
impossible, far too deep in the book.

How do you deliver projects if you're a boss? *You encourage the project
managers to tell you their projects' problems and you help fix them.*

It really is that simple. But what we've seen is that to get to that
point where it is simple, we have to go through many stages. As another
commentator on war, Carl von Clausewitz (Clausewitz) said,

*"Everything is very simple, but not on that account very easy."*

If you're the boss, you need to understand that projects generate data.
You need to understand that a lot of that data presents itself as bad
news, problems and embarrassment. You need to have in-place project
managers who are trusted advisors inside the projects. You need to
create safe places. You need situations where you can have second-circle
discussions with your trusted advisors. And these trusted advisors need
to be able to tell you the bad news.

> Really? I'm not wearing any clothes?

If, as a boss, you can manage to have this kind of second-circle
conversation when needed, then you can transform those impossible,
not-deliverable projects. You can turn them into possible, deliverable
projects. It is relatively simple. But that doesn't mean it's easy.

This only works if the project manager is someone you trust and if the
project manager is someone who has the courage to raise bad news with
you. The project manager has to trust you.

You broach conversations about bad news in a situation where you feel
safe. Not in the public square. The project manager tells you that the
wi-fi doesn't work. You contact whoever it is who supplies the wi-fi.
You get the wi-fi fixed.

This strategy holds out a possibility that is appealing. Maybe you can
set up an organization where your project manager feels comfortable
enough to bring the project's problems to you. Maybe you can address
these problems. If you can do this, your projects will have a much
better chance of being possible and deliverable. Or at least they would
be if you could decide what you're delivering.

And to understand that -- what it is that you should be delivering --
you need a different kind of trusted advisor. You need a product owner.

## Product owner (PO)

As we said above, it's the job of the project manager (PM) to make sure
that the project does the thing *right.* It's the job of the product
owner (PO) to make sure that the project does the *right thing.*

The kind of bad news that the PO brings to the boss is arguably worse
than the kinds of bad news that the PM brings to the boss.

Here are some of the kinds of bad news that the PO might bring to the
boss. This is because the product owners think about the *right thing.*

> "We tried an early version of the software with users. They aren't
> interested in the feature that you thought would be this product's big
> selling point."
>
> "Nearly all the users that we talk to are asking about a certain
> feature. It's one that the product doesn't have. It's the only thing
> they talk about. They say they'd pay for the product if it had that
> feature, but otherwise they're not interested."

Many problems that a product owner brings to you are about value and
then also possibly about cost.

> "The users don't value this thing that we thought they would value."
>
> "The users value this other thing that either we haven't done very
> much of, or we haven't done at all."
>
> "The users value this thing, but we can't find out a way of delivering
> that thing in a way that doesn't cost us more money than we can
> charge."

The problems that product owners bring to their bosses tend to be more
fundamental. They aren't the kind of problems that project managers
bring to their bosses. PMs tend to bring practical, analytic problems --
this thing is wrong, it needs fixing. POs tend to bring synthetic
problems. These are problems where bits need to be put together in a new
way to find a solution:

*"The response of users isn't what we thought it would be. They value
some parts of the idea more than we thought, they don't value other
parts of the idea at all. What do you want us to do?"*

Now might be a good point to remind ourselves what's going on here. The
project is producing data. It's producing data that needs to be
processed, it needs to be turned into information, knowledge, wisdom and
ultimately action. But these are far more threatening kinds of problems
to bosses than the ones project managers bring. Because this kind of
news feels like it is simultaneously saying two things. It's saying,

*"Your thinking up to now hasn't been that great."*

And it's also saying,

*"You really need to do some more thinking."*

That is personal.

So maybe it isn't surprising that this kind of news is the hardest to
deliver. It's the hardest to get bosses to act on. And it's the kind of
news that's most likely to result in "bad behavior." What kind of bad
behavior? Oh, the usual stuff.

Ignoring the problem.

Ignoring the need to make decisions about scope and functionality.

Asking rhetorical questions: "I thought we'd agreed!?"

Shouting at people to go faster.

Messenger shooting.

We can see now that all these behaviors are either slipping into
first-circle (ignoring what's happening) or succumbing to third-circle
(shouting) modes of communication.

This is going to happen. So, a good product owner needs skills to tackle
this kind of behavior.

First and foremost, they need to be able to avoid getting shot! And
still deliver the news. And to do that, they need to be able to develop
a relationship with their boss. It's the kind of relationship that Hans
Christian Andersen deliberately didn't give his naked emperor. It's the
relationship of trusted advisor.

Secondly, the product owner cannot be in denial about the issues that
the project is facing. They need to be clear what they are. They need to
be sold on the idea of the empirical process. This means all the basics
of transparency, inspection and adaptation that we talked about in
(Chapter CAR). They need to understand why projects must be transparent.
They need to understand inspection and adaptation. Seeing what's going
on and then changing what you do because of what you see. That is how
successful projects happen.

But then there's a final thing, a final quality that a product owner
needs. They need to know what they're doing.

This isn't a general appeal for competence. It's a much more focused
kind of practical understanding.

We've touched on this in other chapters. It's something we mentioned in
(Chapter STREAM). It's the understanding that *we're creating a value
stream.* We're putting together the bits that will create value. We're
not just working an existing value stream. We're not even assembling a
value stream out of bits that we know will work. We're creating a new
thing.

We talked about it again in (Chapter SOME), when we discussed the
difference between the biscuit tin and the biscuit. The biscuit is the
thing that we're trying to make, not its description. The biscuit is the
thing that users respond to, that users get value from. There are things
that we'll find out about the biscuit that are important that won't be
captured in any description on the tin.

The PO needs this understanding. It's an understanding that the product
being made isn't already decided. It needs to be created -- it needs to
be made up.

The bet (Chapter BET) of the project is that in this space of possible
biscuits, there's a biscuit that people might buy. It's a biscuit that
would have value for them. It's a biscuit that they would want to eat.
They'd pay for it. And the organization could make a profit on the sale.

But it needs to be made up. And there's only one reliable way to find
that biscuit. And that's to develop the biscuit, at least partly, in
response to data. Data that you get from people who try early, prototype
biscuits, data that you get from people who make the biscuit.

If you're a boss, do you understand now what you're looking for from a
product owner? You're looking for someone who is prepared to do most of
this interpretation of the data. And you want them to do that roughly in
line with an understanding of your wishes. You're looking for someone
who deals with most of the details and only brings to you the big
decisions. And even when they bring to you the big decisions, these are
accompanied with thought-through options.

Does this sound like a lot to expect from an employee? Does building the
right kind of relationship with your product owner sound hard? A
relationship where they bring to you the things that they need to bring
to you, but don't bother you with things you don't need to know about.
Yes, it's hard.

But getting this relationship right is the best chance that you have of
helping your project succeed.

This is the second golden opportunity that we've talked about in this
chapter.

The first golden opportunity is fixing the problems that the project
manager brings to you. The technology has been over-hyped; it can't
deliver the goods. The wi-fi doesn't work. Fix these and you can make
the project possible. It can be delivered. And this is hugely important.

But this second opportunity is much better than that -- maybe this is in
fact a diamond rather than a golden opportunity! These are the
conversations with your product owner. They aren't only an opportunity
to make the project possible and deliverable. They're also an
opportunity to make it *valuable.*

# Conclusion

Any project that is happening generates data. If a project isn't
generating data, it probably isn't happening. Beyond "everything is on
schedule" and "everything is going fine," maybe as a boss, you don't
expect the data that projects create.

You're not expecting to have to make sense of the data. You're not
expecting to have to make decisions about the threats and opportunities
that projects present. These are threats to your identity:

*"You thought this was a good idea? Are you stupid?"*

And these are opportunities, maybe, but then opportunities are a kind of
threat.

> "If we can fix this problem, this project could be a huge success.
> Only an idiot would fumble this."

Oh man, that sounds a lot like:

*"Only people who aren't good at their job think these clothes don't
exist."*

You'd better make sure that the people you surrounded yourself with are
trusted advisors and not some guys from out of town who are selling
invisible cloth.

You probably weren't ready for the project to reveal your personal
strengths and weaknesses so starkly. You probably didn't think that so
many questions would arise from the data.

So, it's easy to start to see projects as sources of embarrassment,
problems and bad news. And then it's easy to do what everyone does with
sources of embarrassment, problems and bad news -- avoid them.

But this is exactly the wrong thing to do. If you're a boss, what you
need to be doing is moving toward the "sound of the guns" -- the bad
news. You need to be moving to where the action is.

> To give your projects any kind of chance of success, you need to
> engage with the data.

But nobody is saying that you can, or should, do this alone. You can't.
You need advisors. You need people who can make sense of the data that
is coming from the project. They can do a lot of the structuring into
information and knowledge for you. They only bother you with the big
problems, the big decisions and the big opportunities.

You need to see projects as sources of data to be turned into actions
that can create value. If you can do that, then you will be keen to put
people in your team that you can trust. You trust them to make sense of
what's going on. You have a nuanced and informed idea of what project
success looks like. Project success is taking what looks like bad news,
failure and embarrassment, and with the help of trusted advisors turning
the "bad news" from data into information, from information into
knowledge, then wisdom and then, finally, action.

There are no guarantees here. If you want one of them, you should buy a
toaster. Every project is a bet (Chapter BET). Remember? But listening
to your trusted advisors and acting on what they say gives your project
the best chance of being deliverable, possible and valuable.
Chapter CONCLUSION

> "I honestly believe it is better to know nothing than to know what
> ain't so."
>
> Josh Billings (Billings)

 

> "Most projects go smoothly."
>
> "All will be well."
>
> "On time and to budget is the recipe for success."

These are things that we know that just ain't so. We can't successfully
deliver projects unless we tackle these wrong ideas. To do that, we need
to understand where they come from. Then we can know how to counter
them. Better ways of seeing are part of the answer. But the full answer
involves a conscious combination of "all" and "some" thinking.

# What we know that ain't so

A lot of people think that they "know" who authored this quote about
knowing what ain't so. They think they "know" it was Mark Twain (real
name Samuel Langhorne Clemens). But a website that tries to chase down
the original source of quotes doesn't find any support for this
attribution (Quote Investigator). The original quote is probably by a
writer known as Josh Billings (real name Henry Wheeler Shaw).

If we're being particularly thoughtful, this might lead us to ask a
question. Are there things about project management that we know? Things
that just ain't so? If there are, how do we find out about them?

The inspiration for this book is another quote. It's from the computer
science pioneer Alan Kay:

> "Point of view is worth 80 IQ points." (Kay)

In this book, we've talked about a bunch of different points of view.
We've talked about streams, swamps, cars and pirate ships. We've talked
about escape, bets, commitments, some (rather than all) and bad news
(for bosses).

The Alan Kay quote makes clear the potential benefits of a different
point of view. If you can look at things in the right way, you can be
much cleverer about how you approach them. But there's a worry here. If
you look at things in the *wrong* way, you could *lose* 80 IQ points.

For most of us, losing 80 IQ points would make us very stupid. This
might be the reason that some projects fail even when they are staffed
by clever people. Those clever people are looking at those projects in
the wrong way. And looking at things in the wrong way makes them dumb.

We almost never consciously decide to see things in these unhelpful
ways. Rather, these ways of seeing come to us unconsciously. These ways
of seeing are things that we "know that just ain't so."

In this book, I present ways of seeing that are helpful, that can make
us smart in the way that we think about projects.

But why do I need to do this? What's the need for me to describe each of
these ways of seeing? I need to do this because, these ways of seeing,
streams, swamp, car and so on -- each of them has a *shadow* way of
seeing. What's more, this shadow way of seeing tends to have unconscious
appeal. We find ourselves accepting it without any conscious thought.
This is a way of seeing that, if we're not careful, can make us dumb.
It's a way of seeing that just ain't so.

In (Chapter SHIP), we used an example that wasn't from project
management. Improvisers on a theater stage are pretending to be on a
ship. They agree to scrub the decks rather than pay attention to the
pirate ship on the horizon. It looks like they *"know"* that this is the
right thing to do.

Then we looked at a similar example from project management. When a
developers in the team notice an important problem, sometimes they
decide not to mention it to their bosses. They somehow "know" that this
is the right thing to do. In both examples, this is something that they
seem to "know." But it's something they "know," that just ain't so.

In improvised theater, the best way to make the scene interesting would
be to deal with the pirate ship on the horizon.

And in project management, the best way to make the project successful
is to call out the problem. If the problem is being discussed out in the
open, it can be dealt with.

Similarly, people think they "know" the things that are most important
for a project to be successful. Those most important things are that
it's "on time" and "to budget."

The idea of on time and to budget is very closely connected to the idea
of commitment and consistency. Most people seem to "know" that this is
what managing projects is about. They "know" it. Even though it just
ain't so.

In the first six chapters of this book, the ways of seeing that I talk
about are ways of seeing that can add to our IQ. They can make us
smarter. But for each of these, there's a shadow idea that could take
away IQ points. It could make us dumb.

Up to this point, I haven't made it clear what those "shadow" ideas are.
So here they are:

1.  *Having the idea is enough. (Chapter STREAM)*

2.  *A project is free to do whatever it needs to do. (Chapter SWAMP)*

3.  

4.  *Nobody needs to see the project until it's finished. (Chapter
    ESCAPE)*

5.  *The project has no problems. People who say the project has
    problems are bad people (Chapter SHIP)*

6.  

7.  *Once we've decided on a destination, the project will drive itself.
    (Chapter CAR)*

8.  *The project is certain to succeed. (Chapter BET)(Chapter NOT BET)*

These are six "shadow" ways of seeing. These are six things that people
act as if they "know." And these are six things that just ain't so. For
each of them, I've provided a different way of seeing, one that offers
the opportunity to make us smarter. And these are the ways of seeing
that we've talked about in the first part of this book.

# Better ways of seeing

For each shadow statement, we can write a smarter statement.

1.  ***Having the idea isn't enough.*** We need to construct a value
    stream. An arrangement that puts all the valuable aspects of the
    idea together. An arrangement that delivers what users value
    (Chapter STREAM).

2.  ***A project isn't free to do whatever it needs to do.*** The
    project takes place in a complex and contested environment. It needs
    to find a way to comply with many rules and regulations (Chapter
    SWAMP).

3.  

4.  

5.  ***It's helpful to get partially finished software in the hands of
    users.*** And trying to help the software escape will let us know
    what kind of challenges we're dealing with (Chapter ESCAPE).

6.  ***The project will have some problems.*** And the way to make the
    project a success is to tackle these problems. People who point out
    these problems aren't bad people (Chapter SHIP).

7.  ***Once we've decided on*** ***a destination, the project won't
    drive itself.*** We'll need a clean windscreen -- transparency. And
    we'll need to look out of it -- inspection. And we'll need to change
    direction because of what we see -- adaptation. (Chapter CAR).

8.  ***The project is not certain to succeed.*** But it might be certain
    to fail. A project is a bet. Delivering a successful project is
    about trying to improve the odds of winning (Chapter BET)(Chapter
    NOT BET).

# How to know what you're doing

In the introduction, I asked you why you'd picked up this book. My guess
was that you are a project manager and that you'd experienced some
problems delivering projects. You would like new and better ways to
approach these problems and so get better at your job.

What's another way of describing being good at your job? How about this?

> You know what you're doing.

We're at a point now where we can give ourselves a better explanation of
what we're doing as project managers. We can give ourselves this
explanation in terms of conscious and unconscious, symmetrical and
asymmetrical thinking.

# Four steps to project management success

Using all of what we've learned from helpful ways of seeing and
unhelpful ways of seeing, I'm going to take us on a journey. I want to
answer the question that Alan Kay didn't ask,

> "***Which** points of view are worth 80 IQ points*?"

That's where we want to get to, but we can't start there. Right at the
beginning of this book I talked about the possibility of points of view
being able to make us smarter. And I also discussed the possibility that
they could make us dumb. So, which points of view hold out the
possibility of making us dumb? From our discussions in part 2 of this
book, in (Chapter COMMIT) and (Chapter ALL), we know the answer to this:

> *The unconsciously symmetrical ones.*

These are the things that we know that just ain't so. And so, on the
journey to project success, this is where we have to start.

## Step 1 -- Unconsciously symmetrical -- living the dream/nightmare

The first stage of the journey to project success is blissful ignorance.
Well, sometimes it's blissful. Sometimes it is extremely painful. This
is the place of unconscious, symmetrical thinking. This is where the
shadow patterns that we've talked about in this chapter hang out.

It's what's going on when we don't bother trying to put software in the
hands of users as soon as we possibly can.

It's also where "foolish" consistency happens. This thinking produces
some of the most unhelpful ideas.

For example:

> A successful project will be on time and to budget.

This is symmetrical thinking because it's about an exact match. It's
thinking that what's most important about a project is that it mirrors
initial thinking. The initially imagined time should match the actual
time. The initially imagined cost should match the final cost.

It's always going to be hard to sound like we're saying that being on
time and to budget isn't important. And please note, that's not what
we're saying. Rather, what we're saying is that it's foolish to focus on
time and budget if we're not also paying attention to value.

What might happen if we do pay attention to value? It may well become
clear that something that's not on time and not to budget could be the
best outcome for the project. Something that has far more value than
what we would have ended up with if we'd only paid attention to being on
time and to budget.

Here is another example of unconscious, "all," "same," "forever" and
"exact match" thinking:

> A project should do exactly what it says in the initial specification.

Again, this is unconscious attraction to the symmetry of exact match and
mirroring. This ignores the real value that might be discovered in the
course of development of a project. It also ignores the potential
problem that what is said on the "biscuit tin" of the specification
might be impossible. It's easy to describe impossible things. I can
write that the biscuit should be made only of butter, sugar and flour
and also be low-calorie. This is logically impossible (unless it's a
really, really, tiny biscuit).

There are also biscuits that can be described that are logically
possible, they're just practically impossible. For instance, I could
specify a biscuit a mile wide. But where would it be baked? And how
would we ever fit it into a tin?

This doesn't just apply to biscuits. There are ideas about software. And
there are specifications of software. There are designs and descriptions
of how software will work. And then there is the actual working
software. And they all have different constraints. What is fast? What is
slow? What is expensive? What is secure? What is maintainable? What is
possible?

The answers to these questions are different for ideas and for working
software. And they are different for the documents that try to capture
the ideas.

This kind of "exact match" symmetrical thinking can be really strong.
It's tempting to think that, once the idea for a project has been
formed, in some sense the project already exists. As I said earlier in
this chapter, this sounds crazy as a suggestion. But it explains some of
the strange ways that people behave in relation to projects and project
teams.

It explains the utter refusal to prioritize a list of features. Just
being asked to think about prioritization seems to make some people feel
bad. Actually, it makes some people go a bit crazy. For them, in some
sense, the reality already exists just because the idea exists. If
someone suggests that some parts of the idea are more important, that
feels bad. If someone explains some parts of the idea will need to be
done before other parts, that feels bad. It doesn't feel like starting
to put the reality together. *It feels like pulling the idea apart!*

It's easy for people to think about their projects in this unconscious,
symmetrical way. And when they do, it's very tempting to think that
anyone who points out problems with the idea is a bad person. If the
idea already exists, anyone that tries to point out problems with the
idea isn't trying to help. They're trying to break the idea!

This is what we're fighting in project management. These unconscious,
symmetrical ways of thinking are the things that we all "know" that
ain't so. These are the ways of thinking that can take away 80 of our
precious IQ points. They can make projects fail. Projects can be made
undeliverable. They can become impossible. These unconscious thoughts
can turn the "dream" that began a project into a total nightmare.

So, unconscious "all" thinking. This is where we have to start from. How
do we get out of here? Well, Alan Kay's suggestion is that we could take
a different point of view.

## Step 2 -- Unconsciously asymmetrical -- helpful ways of seeing

How do you make projects more deliverable and less impossible? You use a
helpful way of seeing, like the idea of the pirate ship (Chapter SHIP).
Is there a problem on the horizon? Is there a pirate ship sailing toward
you and your crew? Well, then, you get ready to deal with the pirate
ship. You call it out. You don't keep quiet and scrub the decks. You
resist the notion that all will be well.

How do you control a project? You use a helpful way of seeing. You
control the project like you control a car (Chapter CAR). You make sure
your mirrors, windows and windscreen are clear -- ***transparency.***
And you look out of them -- ***inspection.*** And you change where you
go in response to what you see -- ***adaptation***.

The ways of seeing that I talk about in the early chapters of this book
-- swamp, ship, car -- are appealing. But they aren't as compelling as
the shadow ways of seeing that we're combating.

At least, not initially. It's only once these helpful ways of seeing,
like the swamp, and the ship, and the car are explained that they become
compelling for different reasons. They're attractive because they have a
different kind of symmetry. They match the experiences of anyone who has
worked on projects and tried to deliver them.

Of course, the environments where we deliver projects are like swamps
(Chapter SWAMP)! They're complex spaces. Of course, projects are bets
(Chapter BET, Chapter NOT BET). There is a real chance they won't
succeed.

These ways of seeing have practical power. Seeing the mission you're on
with your software as an escape attempt has practical power (Chapter
ESCAPE). It encourages you to do the right kinds of things. Trying to
help your software escape reveals the fences. It reveals the traps and
tripwires that are stopping your software from escaping. Sometimes you
will manage to help your software escape into the hands of users. If you
do, it will completely change the dynamic of your project.

Here's the power of a different way of seeing. You don't need to know a
lot, if anything, about symmetry and asymmetry to understand it. The
helpful ways of seeing that I talk about in the first seven chapters of
this book (including both Chapter BET and Chapter NOT BET) are helpful
in isolation. And then they become more helpful if taken all together.
They are ways of seeing that make us smarter. They help us think about
projects more asymmetrically. But they do this unconsciously.

We want to have the best chance of delivering projects. And to get that
best chance we need to be aware of the initial intuitions we're likely
to have about a project. Initially, we'll think about "on time" and "to
budget." Initially, unconsciously, we'll think about "exact match." How
can we get past these initial, unconscious thoughts?

To really know what we're doing, we need conscious asymmetry.

## Step 3 -- Consciously asymmetrical, looking for trouble, knowing what we're doing

The poet John Keats was dead from consumption by the age of twenty-six.
But in his tragically short life, he managed to write some of the most
beautiful poems in the English language.

Keats talks about an important aspect of creativity. It's something that
he called "negative capability." He explained this as:

> "Being in uncertainties, mysteries, doubts, without any irritable
> reaching after fact and reason." (Keats)

Most of us aren't poets, so we have to do a little bit of reaching after
fact and reason. But do we need to be irritable about it? And does that
mean that we can't be comfortable with uncertainties, mysteries and
doubts?

In (Chapter SOME), we characterized what project managers are doing. We
did this in terms of symmetrical and asymmetrical thinking. They're
trying to insert "some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match."
And the helpful ways of seeing that we've talked about do something very
interesting. They let us think in these asymmetrical ways, but they do
it *unconsciously.*

When we see projects in terms of swamps, streams, ships and cars, we do
*something.* We do it over a *certain space of time.* And we try to make
sure that it has *some* value to *somebody.* The working software that
we try to help escape has *some relation* to the description on the tin.
But it is made from *different* stuff. So, it is fundamentally
different. The biscuit can't be the biscuit tin. It's *an inexact
match.*

Now, we're pushing into the region of conscious asymmetry. We're moving
toward uncertainties. We're doing it deliberately. We're walking toward
mysteries and doubts.

For example, when we start to explore the environment in which the
project is going to be deployed, we don't know what we'll find. When we
start to research with users, we don't know what they'll say. When we
focus on developing working software, we don't know how it will perform.

But we know that we still have to keep doing this, despite the clamor
from almost everybody, and also from parts of our own minds! This is our
unconscious mind. It wants the comfort of all, same, forever and the
defensibility of doing exactly what it says on the tin.

In (Chapter COMMIT) and (Chapter ALL), we started to unpack why
symmetrical thinking is problematic. We started to understand why it
isn't enough for project management.

This is the third step in our journey. If we can take it, we can make
our projects possible and deliverable. This is the area of
*"uncertainties, mysteries and doubts,"* that Keats talks about. But
also, in the language of biscuits and the biscuit tin, it's where the
biscuit is!

It's where something that is both valuable to users and valuable to the
organization exists. It's a particular thing. It's a product of "some,"
"different," "for a while" and "inexact" thinking. It has been
consciously put together.

This is how we're breaking down our thinking.

Step 1 -- there is unconscious "all," "same," "forever" and "exact
match" thinking. This happens before we even know it. And this is where
all the trouble starts.

Step 2 -- there is unconscious "some," "different," "for a while" and
"inexact match" thinking. This is using helpful ways of seeing, like
ships, cars and swamps, to focus on *some* aspects of a project. By
using these helpful ways of seeing, we end up focusing on "some" rather
than "all."

When we're thinking about ships or cars, there's a shift in thinking.
The focus is on "some" but isn't conscious. That's why we need to take
the next step, the step into the unknown.

Step 3 -- This is conscious "some," "different," "for a while" and
"inexact match" thinking. This is where *we know what we're doing.* And
so, paradoxically, that means, we know we don't know what we're doing.
We deliberately look for uncertain things. We embrace negative
capability. We talk to users. We find out what's wrong with the
technology. We get *something* working. And we do it in a particular
complex environment.

But still, this isn't quite the end of the story. To really know what
we're doing, it isn't enough to be consciously asymmetrical in our
thinking.

## Step 4 -- Consciously symmetrical -- principles for project success

Are we saying that symmetrical thinking is bad? No, we're not saying
that at all. Are we saying that all unconscious symmetrical thinking is
bad? We're not even saying that.

Without "all," "same," "forever" and "exact match" thinking, life is
impossible. And a lot of that thinking must be unconscious. The idea
that objects continue to be objects, whether we look at them or not, is
symmetrical. The idea that, because the sun rose in the east today and
all the days before that, it will do the same tomorrow, is symmetrical.
We can't really function without symmetrical ideas.

Similarly, we're not knocking commitment and consistency. Commitment and
consistency, in themselves, aren't bad. They are symmetrical principles
that we all need to use. But *foolish* commitment and consistency are
bad.

There's one aspect of symmetrical thinking that we haven't explored.
This is how it connects to idealism and principled thinking. Conscious
"all," "same," "forever" thinking results in general rules. And these
are the kind of principles that make the world a much better place. For
example:

> Always be polite.
>
> Everyone is equal before the law.
>
> Treat others the same way that you'd like to be treated.

In project management, we can see now that we are advocating some
similar "all," "same" and "forever" principles.

> Always focus on "driving the car" -- transparency, inspection and
> adaptation.
>
> Always be talking to users.
>
> Always be pushing working software into the hands of users.

Delivering the impossible isn't about always, in every case, resisting
"all" thinking. That would be impossible. But it is about being
consciously consistent and committed to the right things.

This is conscious "all," "same," "forever" and "exact match thinking."
It's *always* thinking about "some," "different," "for a while" and
"inexact match"! It's consciously pushing again and again for asymmetry.
To do this we need "negative capability."

We need to be always (symmetrically), consciously, thinking about the
asymmetric aspects of the project. This is hard. Because unconsciously,
we'll always be attracted to "all," "same," "forever" and "exact match"
thinking.

We need to be consciously symmetrical about always being consciously
asymmetrical! We need to always apply empirical principles. We need to
keep talking to users, we need to keep exploring the organization. We
need to keep pushing for software to be released.

This is the recipe for being a successful project manager. It's a
mixture. It has commitment and consistency. But this commitment and
consistency isn't foolish. It is conscious symmetry and conscious
asymmetry. It's about both all, same and forever, and also some,
different and for a while. It's the third kind of thinking that we
talked about in (Chapter ALL): a bi-logic.

In the mundane world of actually managing a project, this boils down to
a few basic things. It means being transparent about what's going on. We
do user research in advance of working software being available. When
working software is available, we test it and get the users' feedback.
All of this is inspection. But there's another aspect to inspection.

We need to know about the organizational environment of the project. We
need to know about the swamp (Chapter SWAMP). Organizational research
is, in a way, downright weird. People will try to stop us. These are the
people we're working for. These are the very people we're trying to
help. They will try to stop us achieving the transparency, inspection
and adaptation that we need, that the project needs.

The American bank robber Willie Sutton was supposedly once asked by his
FBI interrogators why he robbed banks. Sutton's reply was simple:

*"Because that's where the money is."*

The quote, as we might suspect by now, having seen so many other falsely
attributed quotes in this book, is probably apocryphal. But it's no less
powerful for that. What we need to do in projects has a similar
explanation. Why should we be pushing ourselves toward "some,"
"different," "for a while" and "inexact match"? Why should we be doing
this consciously? Because that's where the value is; that's where the
money is.

# What about the boss?

We haven't yet mentioned the boss (Chapter BOSS).

A smart boss needs to know what they're doing.

This project got funded. It got started. That means somebody, somewhere
said "f\*\*k it."

But then, when the project starts to happen, things will go wrong. That
was the revelation that came from my "thought experiment" with soup and
sandwiches (Chapter BOSS). There was the imaginary lunch of a bacon
sandwich, and my real lunch of chicken soup. In reality -- the soup got
spilled.

When things do start to go wrong, whoever is responsible for the project
might start to panic. They might realize that by starting the project,
they bought themselves a lot of bad news.

This is the most common way to look at projects that are going wrong.
And this is tempting for the boss. It's tempting to see projects that
are happening as sources of embarrassment. Things are really happening.
That is scary. But, as we discussed in (Chapter BOSS) and in (Chapter
BET), there is another way of looking at them.

Projects are things that generate data. They generate a lot of data. And
that data needs to be made sense of.

I hope that some bosses read this book. And I hope that they have the
imagination to use the helpful way of seeing that I talk about in
(Chapter BOSS).

But this book is mainly for project managers. And my guess is that any
project managers reading this might be thinking the same thing.

*What if the boss I'm dealing with hasn't read this book?*

They haven't enthusiastically accepted its advice. What if this boss is
still in the grip of the "shadow" ways of seeing?

They don't want to be bothered with the endless stream of problems that
come out of a project. They think that the only sign that a project is
going well is a continuous, monotonous stream of good reports. They want
exactly what it says on the biscuit tin. Even if what it says there is:

> "All butter, low-fat, sugar-free honey biscuits. Wholewheat and
> gluten-free."

They don't want to be taken on a journey by their project managers and
product owners. They don't want to travel toward something that is full
of uncertainties, mysteries and doubts. They are probably thinking:

> "I'm the boss!"
>
> "I'm perfectly entitled to reach irritably for fact and reason."
>
> "I maneuvered this team into promising something, and I want the thing
> that they promised. Even if it's impossible."

Projects where the boss behaves like this are very common. And they are
exactly the kind of projects that we should be dealing with. The ones
where the boss is behaving like the emperor in Hans Christian Andersen's
story. These are the projects where a book called "Delivering the
Impossible" should deliver.

That's why it isn't only the project managers that need to go on a
journey from unconscious "all," "same," "forever" and "exact match" to
"some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match." They need to
also bring the bosses with them.

But before the project managers can help the bosses, *they* need to know
what they're doing. *They* need to know that their job is to push
continuously for "some," "different," "for a while" thinking. Project
managers need to know the value of *inexact match.*

For example, the "show and tell" meeting is an occasion where the team
members show actual progress. They do this by demonstrating working
software to the bosses. One week there's a demonstration of a painfully
small bit of functionality. Two weeks later (typically) there's a demo
of a little bit more working software.

Showing this progress to bosses obviously comes with some risks. It can
result in explosions. It can result in threats. But what it does do is
show the asymmetrical nature of actual progress. It shows how long it
takes to bring something from an idea to a reality. *Some* software
takes *some* time.

And this subtly eases one of the most difficult symmetrical beliefs that
bosses tend to suffer from. This is the ridiculous belief that once the
idea exists, the product already exists.

The bosses can see the slow progress of software development. But this,
typically, doesn't result in any conscious admission from the bosses.
There's no admission that everything on the list of demands can't and
won't be done by the deadline. The idea that the project will succeed by
being on time and to budget is rarely abandoned completely.

But sometimes implementing something first results in the right kind of
behavior. It results in negotiation.

*"If we didn't do X next, could we do Y instead?"*

This is giving some items a higher priority than others. Prioritization
is impossible in thinking that is only about all, same and forever.

Something similar happens, when you share the top concerns of the team.
These are the kinds of things that come out in retrospectives. It's what
happens when you plaster those concerns on a wall, such as the wi-fi
wall of shame.

The bosses still might say

*"If you were professionals, you'd be able to deliver this project on*
*time and to* *budget without wi-fi and without talking to the people
who know how the software should work."*

But the single, asymmetric issue of the wi-fi might still get fixed.

Similarly, showing the boss the results of user research is powerful.
Looking at research done with users can result in a powerful change of
attitude from the bosses. Because it's an injection of unconscious
"some" thinking. Users like some things. Users are bored by other
things. Users really don't like other things. This is asymmetry. It's
the absolute opposite of thinking that everything about an idea is
equally valuable. Even more powerful is having the bosses see the raw
footage of the user research.

What we're talking about here is *showing* the bosses the asymmetries of
the project, rather than *telling* them. Telling them doesn't work. What
does work is letting them come to the realization the project is about
"some" and "different." What does work is gently walking them through
the realization that the project will happen over *some* time. What is
most effective is getting them to understand that what they get,
ultimately, won't be an exact match with the initial idea. These kinds
of approaches, we can see by now, are about injecting "some" into the
conversation. And that does work. Sometimes.

# Putting it all together

Things are different if we know what we're doing. They're different if
we're conscious of the need to get the bosses to see the asymmetries of
the project.

We know we can highlight this "some," "different," "for a while"
thinking. And then we can give ourselves the best chance of delivering.
We can deliver a possible, valuable project. We can address the specific
problems that need fixing. We can highlight the new opportunities that
have been discovered that were never in the original plan.

Seeing projects in certain ways makes us dumb. Fortunately, there are
other ways of seeing projects, there are other points of view. And if we
can see projects in these ways, we will be considerably smarter in the
way that we manage them.

But we can do better than that. With the help of thinkers like Matte
Blanco (Matte Blanco) we can *know what we're doing* when we're using
these helpful ways of seeing. We can realize that these ways of seeing
give us an opportunity to see projects less symmetrically. To see them
in terms of "some," "different," "for a while" and "inexact match."

We understand that the business of project management is about "some,"
"different" and "for a while." It's about being comfortable with things
that don't match exactly. It's about adding the right amount of
asymmetry. That's how we deliver the impossible.

Now, we can gently encourage the bosses into thinking about "some,"
"different" and for a while. We can help them be comfortable with an
inexact match. We can help them with asymmetrical thinking. And
initially, this doesn't have to be conscious.

How do we start to get our bosses to think asymmetrically? We find ways
to talk to them about the project's problems. There's a ship on the
horizon that needs to be dealt with. And we need to do this in an
environment where the status of the boss isn't threatened.

Ambitious deadlines, vague requirements or ridiculously detailed
requirements -- we're probably going to be forced into foolish
commitments. But they're only really foolish if we see them as such. If
we see them as the start of a negotiation, we're on the way to taking an
impossible project and turning it into a possible one. And if we commit
to the right things -- empirical process, user research and working
software -- we're giving ourselves the best-possible chance.

# What's at stake?

It was the height of the Cuban missile crisis. Many commentators thought
that there was a good chance of nuclear war. President John F. Kennedy
turned to his friend Barry Goldwater and said,

*"Barry, do you want this f\*\*\*ing job?"*

Sadly, again(!) there's no good source to support this story. But we can
imagine that if you're President of the United States, especially in the
middle of a Cold War, there's no need to keep pushing your "negative
capability." There will be uncertainties, mysteries and doubts. They
will come and find you!

And in many cases, like the Cuban missile crisis, it's pretty clear
what's at stake.

In this final chapter, what I'm advocating is a constant push. It's a
push to uncover uncertainties, mysteries and doubts. And what I'm
advocating is that you bring these to the attention of the bosses. You
need to talk about these things with people who are senior to you,
people who have more status and power than you. These are people who
could get you fired.

But you have in your power something that the people who have the money,
who are shouting at you and threatening you, don't have. You and your
team can deliver working software into the world. And, as I mentioned at
the very beginning of the book, when I quoted Mark Andreessen, software
is eating everything. The way that we find love, the way we entertain
ourselves, the way we order food, the way we get our political opinions,
but also the way government implements its policies -- software has
already eaten all of this. When we start to address how to get better at
delivering software projects, that's what's at stake.

You don't think this is powerful stuff?

Try it. Try firmly and gently broaching pirate ship issues with your
boss.

Try focusing on user research. Find out more about the organization.
Find out more about the swamp. Try focusing on delivering value. Try
helping some working software to escape.

At various times in my career, people from several professions have made
a similar bitter joke. These were people in the public sector, academia,
and teaching. They told me that the trouble with their profession is
that the stakes are so low. I've never been exactly sure what they mean.
These are very valuable professions. Society depends on them. I think
what they might mean is that in their profession, there is a great deal
of unseemly political maneuvering. There is in-fighting. And a lot of
the time, the fighting is over small prizes and tiny bits of progress
that don't seem to make much of a difference. I don't think that this is
a good description of these professions. And when people say it, I think
it's just a sign of frustration.

But we absolutely can't say that the stakes are low for many software
development projects. The stakes on so many projects are enormously
high.

The software is important for the organizations that fund it. It is
important for the people that work on it. Very often, it changes the
lives of the people who use it. It's important. The stakes are high.

How do we deliver the impossible? In bits. Bits that we work on over a
short space of time.

But what if what is asked for is genuinely impossible? Then we don't
deliver that.

We deliver something that is *possible.* Because those are the only
kinds of things that can be delivered! But we can make this project
possible by adding *some* rather than *all.* We can make it possible by
understanding that what gets delivered won't be an exact match. We can
make this project deliverable. We can do that by understanding that we
can stop, just for a while, thinking about *forever.*

We get the details of what we can do to make things possible from user
research. We understand what we can and can't do in a particular project
space from organizational research. And we learn how to make the thing
work from trying to release working software. We learn how to please
users by pushing working software as far as possible. And we push it as
soon as possible. Sometimes we don't get all the way. Sometimes the
software doesn't completely escape. But however far we get, we learn.
And sometimes we manage to put software in the hands of real users.

A project is a bet (Chapter BET, Chapter Not BET). The ways of seeing
that I've talked about in this book give us the best chance of making
that bet pay off.

There are better ways of seeing projects: swamps, ships, bets and cars.
Seeing things in these ways can make us smarter. And beyond these ways
of seeing are other ways of seeing. Seeing projects in terms of negative
capability. Seeing them in terms of symmetry *and* asymmetry. These are
ways of seeing that allow us to know what we're doing. These are the
ways of seeing that can make even the most difficult projects possible
and deliverable.

 

 

 
# Afterword

I have heard Mark speak at many Agile and software development events
over the years. On each occasion I have always been intrigued by his
fresh insights into how real projects actually run within regular
organizations, particularly his evaluation of the myriad reasons why a
project may stumble or fail.

In this masterpiece, Mark brought his experience and expertise to the
seemingly intractable problem of turning an impossible project into one
that is possible. He pulled apart this complex topic and examined the
component pieces. He investigated what truly happens at different
project life-cycle stages, demonstrated how this differs from what is
supposed or reported to happen, and showed how you risk failure if you
attended to what is supposedly happening whilst neglecting what's truly
happening.

On your journey through this book, you encountered many fresh ideas and
ways of looking at projects, some of which may have felt
counterintuitive. By keeping an open mind, your journey was rewarded,
casting away many old preconceptions about what a project is and how it
runs. Having got to this stage of the book, you'll have a vastly
different and profoundly better viewpoint of what a project is and how
to manage it for success.

You needn't read many books on project management before you recognize
that they tend to fall into one of two categories. Firstly, you have the
"paint by numbers" books, offering you a basic checklist-type approach
to running a project. These books work fine on a simple project that
goes exactly to plan. Unfortunately, most worthwhile projects are
neither simple nor go to plan.

Secondly, you have books which do recognize that projects are complex
beasts that encounter problems. These books promise a solution, provided
the reader follows a closely defined methodology. Little is said about
what you should do if your project cannot fit into this methodology, or
if you follow its steps and don't obtain the promised result.

This book is very different from both the paint by numbers and the
methodology approaches. In it, problems are neither ignored nor
magically resolved in a way that is convenient for the author but rarely
occurs in real life.

The book started with a fundamental question rarely asked by project
managers -- why we are even doing a project at all? We learned that it
is to add value. Through this insight, we learned about the two value
streams, an unexpected insight for many project team members. When I
recall some of the failed projects I have been part of, I can now see
that their failure was partly due to our inability to build this value
stream.

Projects rarely take place within a vacuum or greenfield site, but
instead take place within an existing environment, a fact that other
project management books rarely acknowledge. For good reasons, Mark
termed this environment the "swamp." After reading this book, you now
understand that for your project to succeed, you must coexist with the
swamp's current inhabitants, many of whom will be indifferent to you and
some of whom will be antithetical to your goals.

For your project to be a success, the software produced must escape from
the development team into the hands of your customers or users. Through
this book, we now understand the importance of that escape, the
obstacles we face in that journey, what we can do to help in this
process, and the benefits we'll enjoy even if our software doesn't
manage to fully escape.

Mark used a windscreen analogy to understand the importance of
transparency, inspection and adaptation. Although these concepts are
familiar to most of us conversant with Agile, Mark explored three
important areas where this analogy breaks down and problems occur.
Firstly, unlike projects, most car journeys are uneventful and
successful. Secondly, there is only ever one person driving the car.
Thirdly, nobody ever got in trouble for pointing out a hazard visible
through the windshield. Pointing out where analogies break down is an
important activity that is not practiced enough. How many times have we
been fooled by a seductive analogy extended into areas where it is not
valid?

For myself, I found the most useful chapter was Chapter BET. In this
chapter, Mark laid bare a widely recognized but rarely acknowledged fact
-- the decision to proceed with the project is the equivalent of
gambling on an unknown future outcome. Mark helped us grasp the
implications of this and showed how this gamble, plus our refusal to
acknowledge it as such, invariably leads to various mind games and
theatrical activities. Mark extended the analogy to a poker hand, and
showed us that, far from being an occasion for recriminations,
additional funding requests should be viewed as an opportunity to
increase the ante on what is now a far more attractive bet.

Mark also showed that when projects are loaded with sufficient
additional limitations, the project is no longer a gamble under
conditions of uncertainty, but instead has become a certain failure.

Mark also explored a common misconception of project management, one
that is often used to manipulate us. It is the belief that project
management is all about keeping promises and honoring commitments. He
shows how this misconception, together with our inherent desire to honor
commitments, is often manipulated to force projects to try and deliver
on impossible and unreasonable commitments.

The chapters on "all" and "some" help us understand why there is so much
focus on delivery of "everything" and "exactly what it says on the tin,"
rather than focusing on delivery of what the project has by now
discovered to be of more value.

This book wraps up with a chapter for stakeholders and senior leaders.
Here, you gain an insight into many of the pressures that bosses find
themselves under, and why they behave in ways that appear to be
detrimental to a project outcome. You should use this insight to seek
alternative ways for bosses to look at the apparently bad news coming
out from the project and use it to secure a better outcome for
themselves and their project.

This chapter is rare within books on project management. Unlike most
other books, it acknowledges the challenges and pressures that project
stakeholders face, offers explanations for their sometimes-puzzling
behavior, then suggests solutions and a way forward to solving the
dilemma of balancing the conflicting needs of business and IT.

This is a well-researched book on an important topic, offering fresh
insights that help you view your projects in more constructive ways,
thereby enhancing your chances of achieving a successful outcome. I hope
you enjoyed it as much as I did and you now apply these insights here to
turn your next impossible project into one that is both possible and
truly valuable.

Andrew Brown

Author of *Taming Your Dragon: Addressing Your Technical Debt*
Delivering the Impossible

Successful Ways of Seeing Software Project Management

Mark Stringer

**INDEX 247**

**About the Author**

**Mark Stringer** has worked in the software development industry for 30
years. He's been a programmer, applications researcher and, for the last
15 years, a project manager. He's worked with organizations such as IBM,
Xerox and Cambridge University.

Mark wrote this book because of a realization -- he seemed to be
repeatedly reading the same book about project management. The same
book, but with different titles. This book claimed that, if only you
followed some prescribed methodology, everything would be lovely. His
experience was, no matter what methodology you used, things often
weren't lovely.

In recent years, as well as writing this book, he has written and
performed the one-man shows "Delivering the Impossible," and "The
Biscuit Tin and the Biscuit," at the Brighton and Edinburgh Fringe
Festivals. These are possibly the only fringe shows about project
management -- ever! He has an honors degree in philosophy from the
University of St. Andrews, Scotland.

**About the Technical Reviewer**

This page is customarily left blank

Acknowledgments

I have just a small number of people to thank. But they are very
important. Without them, this book wouldn't have happened. First and
foremost, I need to thank Andrew Brown for reading and re-reading full
chapters and half-formed, barely sketched-out chapters. When he was
experiencing the most difficult of times, he still managed to read what
I had written, comment on it, and say yes to a video call. Andrew, my
heartfelt thanks.

Next, I'd like to thank my wife Themis Chalvantzi-Stringer. Three years
ago, she encouraged me to take a show to the Edinburgh Festival Fringe
with the title "Delivering the Impossible." Hey! That's the title of
this book! If I had not done that, this book probably wouldn't exist. I
never would have discovered how helpful talking out loud, in a field, to
no one, is for organizing your thoughts. And "Delivering the Impossible"
wouldn't have had its premiere performance, in front of a large audience
who were expecting an entirely different show! So, also thanks to Peter
Merre for messaging me from his sick bed and lending me his venue spot
and his fringe audience.

Finally, I'd like to thank my father, Peter Stringer, to whom this book
is dedicated. He didn't know much about project management, or software
development. But he loved books. He literally showed me where the
library was. He took me with him from an early age. And he recommended
to me all kinds of books: poetry, sci-fi, historical fiction. All the
time I knew him, he read at least one book a week.

This page is customarily left blank
